The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Second-Figure Syllogism to Distinguish Eternal Speech from Relation
Commentator constructs a second-figure syllogism to prove eternal speech is not a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a categorical syllogism in the second figure from an Ash'ari theological argument and identify its mood.

Core Idea
What exists externally cannot be a mere mental relation — that is the logical core of this Ash'ari proof.

This passage from the commentary on Bajuri's gloss on Bannani shows how a theological claim — that the eternal speech of Allah is an attribute of meaning, not a relation — is supported by a categorical syllogism. The commentator explicitly constructs a second-figure argument to distinguish between external existence and mental considerations. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The commentator states: 'لا شيء من النسب بموجود في الخارج' — 'No relation exists in the external world.' This is a universal negative proposition (E-type).
2
Identify the minor premise
The commentator states: 'الكلام القديم موجود في الخارج' — 'The eternal speech exists in the external world.' This is a universal affirmative proposition (A-type).
3
Recognize the figure and mood
The middle term 'موجود في الخارج' (exists externally) is the predicate in both premises, placing this in the second figure. The mood is 'thani al-thani' (second of the second), which corresponds to Camestres in Latin: major E, minor A, conclusion E.
The Arabic term 'ثاني الثاني' is used in the text.
4
Derive the conclusion
From the two premises, the valid conclusion is 'لا شيء من النسب بالكلام القديم' — 'No relation is the eternal speech.' This is also a universal negative.
5
Understand the ontological basis
The argument relies on the premise that relations are 'umur i'tibariyya' (mental considerations) with no external reality, while the eternal speech is an actual existent attribute of meaning. This ontological distinction is what drives the syllogism.
This distinction is central in Ash'ari theology to preserve the real nature of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نِسْبَةٌ بَيْنَ مُفْرَدَيْنِ قَائِمَةٌ بِنَفْسِ المُتَكَلِّمِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَهِيَ مَدْلُولُ جُمَلِ القُرْآنِ. : بِأَنَّهُ لَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الكَلَامُ القَدِيمُ هُوَ النِّسْبَةُ لِأُمُورٍ: - مِنْهَا : أَنَّ النِّسَبَ أُمُورٌ اعْتِبَارِيَّةٌ لَا وُجُودَ لَهَا فِي الخَارِجِ، وَالكَلَامُ القَدِيمُ وُجُودِيٌّ مُتَحَقِّقُ فِي الخَارِجِ كَسَائِرِ صِفَاتِ المَعَانِي. قوله : (نِسْبَةٌ) احترز عن : المعاني المفهومة من مفردات القرآن؛ كمدلول «الله»، ومدلول «الأرض» و «السماء»، ونحو ذلك، فليست كلام الله تعالى قطعاً . قوله : (مِنْهَا : أَنَّ النِّسَبَ أُمُورُ اعْتِبَارِيَّةُ) أشار بهذا الكلام إلى تركيب قياس من ثاني الشكل الثَّاني، وبيانُهُ أن تقول: «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ النِّسَبِ بِمَوجُودٍ في الخارج، وَالكَلَامُ القَدِيمُ مُوجُودٌ في الخارج ينتج من ثاني الثَّاني : «لَا شَيْء مِنَ النِّسَبِ بِالكَلَامِ القَدِيمِ».
A relation between two terms subsisting in the speaker's mind, and in that case it is the meaning of the sentences of the Quran. [It is] that it is not valid for the eternal speech to be the relation, for reasons: among them: that relations are mental considerations with no existence in the external world, and the eternal speech is existent, actualized in the external world like other attributes of meanings. His saying: (relation) he excludes from it the meanings understood from the individual words of the Quran, such as the meaning of 'Allah', the meaning of 'earth' and 'sky', and so on; these are absolutely not the speech of Allah. His saying: (among them: that relations are mental considerations) he indicated by this statement the construction of a syllogism from the second of the second figure, and its explanation is that you say: 'No relation exists in the external world, and the eternal speech exists in the external world,' producing from the second of the second: 'No relation is the eternal speech.'
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that argues that the eternal speech of Allah (kalam qadim) cannot be identified with the relational meanings (nisab) indicated by Quranic sentences. The commentator first clarifies that the term 'nisba' in the matn refers specifically to the relation between two terms that subsists in the speaker's mind, not to the meanings of individual words like 'Allah' or 'earth'. Such individual meanings are definitely not the eternal speech. Then he presents the first reason: relations are mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) with no external existence, whereas the eternal speech is an actual existent attribute like other attributes of meaning. This leads to a syllogism in the second figure (specifically the second mood of the second figure, called 'thani al-thani'): the major premise is 'No relation exists externally', the minor is 'The eternal speech exists externally'. The conclusion is 'Therefore, no relation is the eternal speech.' Thus, the eternal speech cannot be a relation. This argument relies on the ontological distinction between mental and external existence, a key topic in Islamic logic and theology.

Key Terms
نسبة
كلام قديم
أمور اعتبارية
ثاني الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the syllogism constructed in this passage?
A) The eternal speech is a relation.
B) No relation is the eternal speech.
C) Some relations are eternal speech.
D) The eternal speech exists externally.

Key Takeaway
A second-figure syllogism can prove that the eternal speech is not a relation by distinguishing external existence from mental considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a second-figure categorical syllogism to prove that the attribute of 'power' (qudra) in Allah is not a mere mental concept. Use the premises: 'No mental consideration exists externally' and 'The attribute of power exists externally.' Write the conclusion in Arabic or English.
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Multiplicity of Relations and Oneness of Divine Speech
Commentator uses Quranic examples and formal syllogism to argue relations are many, speech is one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the second-figure categorical syllogism from the passage and verify its validity using the awwal al-thani mood.

Core Idea
One simple attribute cannot be identical to many distinct relations.

The Ash'ari position holds that Allah's eternal speech is one simple attribute, while the Quranic sentences indicate many distinct relations. The commentator uses clear Quranic examples to show that these relations are truly different from each other and then formalizes the argument as a categorical syllogism from the second figure. In this lesson, you will dissect that syllogism and see how the structure of the argument leads to the conclusion that the relations are not the eternal speech itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Read the formal syllogism at the end of the passage. The first premise (major) states 'These relations are truly multiple.' The second premise (minor) states 'The speech of Allah is not truly multiple.' Identify which term is the predicate in both premises (the middle term).
The middle term is 'truly multiple/not truly multiple'.
2
Determine the figure and mood
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. The major premise is universal affirmative ('multiple') and the minor universal negative ('not multiple'), so the mood is 'Camestres' (AEE in the second figure). Confirm that the conclusion must be universal negative.
The passage says 'from the first of the second,' which is awwal al-thani, i.e., the first mood of the second figure.
3
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion is: 'These relations are not the speech of Allah.' Check that the conclusion follows necessarily: if all relations are multiple and speech is not multiple, then no relation is speech.
This is a valid syllogism in the second figure with true premises, so the conclusion is sound.
4
Test with a concrete Islamic example
Take one relation: 'the relation indicated by 'And We sent you as a messenger''. According to the syllogism, this relation is not the eternal speech. Confirm that this matches the Ash'ari distinction between the eternal attribute and the created expressions.
This shows the practical implications for kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهَا : أَنَّ النِّسَبَ المَدْلُولَ عَلَيْهَا بِجُمَلِ القُرْآنِ كَثِيرَةٌ جِدًا، وَمُتَغَايرَةٌ تَغَايُراً حَقِيقِيًّا بَيِّناً، وَكَلَامُ اللهِ تَعَالَى القَدِيمُ صِفَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ لَا تَعَدُّدَ فِيهَا عِنْدَ أَهْلِ الحَقِّ كَسَائِرِ المَعَانِي. قوله : (وَمُتَغَايرَةٌ تَغَايُراً حَقِيقِيًّا ) قال الهلالي : إذ لا يرتاب ذو مسكة في أنَّ النسبة المدلول عليها بقوله تعالى : وَأَرْسَلْنَكَ لِلنَّاسِ رَسُولًا ﴾ [النساء : ۷۹] ليست عين النّسبة المدلول عليها بقوله تعالى : وَأَرْسَلْنَا الرِّيحَ لَوَقِحَ ﴾ [الحجر: ۲۲]، والمدلول عليها بقوله جل وعلا: وَأَوْحَيْنَا إِلَى إِبْرَاهِيمَ الله [النساء: ١٦٣] غير المدلول عليه بقوله سبحانه: ﴿ وَأَوْحَى رَبُّكَ إِلَى النَّحْلِ [النحل : ٦٨]، وإذا كان التغاير في مثل هذه الأمثلة واضحاً فكيف به في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ سُعِدُوا ﴾ [هود : ۱۰۸] ... إلخ مع قوله: ﴿فَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ شَقُواْ﴾ [هود: (١٠٦)، وفي قوله تعالى: ﴿ادْخُلُوهَا بِسَلَم ، امنين [الحجر: ٤٦] مع قوله تعالى : واخْرُجْ مِنْهَا مَذْهُ ومَا مَدْحُورًا ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۸)، وإذا اتضح التغاير مع الاتحاد في النوع - أعني : الإنشائية والخبريَّة ، فهو مع الاختلاف أوضح وأجلى. قوله: (وَكَلَامُ اللهِ تَعَالَى القَدِيمُ صِفَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ) أي : عند الأشاعرة، ويأتي هنا قياسٌ مِنَ الثَّاني فيقال: هَذِهِ النِّسَبُ المَدْلُولُ عَلَيْهَا بِأَلفَاظ القُرَآنِ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ حَقِيقَةٌ، وَكَلَامُ اللهِ تَعَالَى لَيْسَ بِمُتَعَدِّدٍ حَقِيقَةٌ، ينتج مِن أوَّل الثَّاني : هَذِهِ النِّسَبُ المَدْلُولُ عَلَيْهَا بِأَلفَاظِ القُرْآنِ لَيْسَتْ بِكَلَامِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى .
And among them: that the relations indicated by the sentences of the Quran are very many, and truly distinct in a clear distinction, and the eternal speech of Allah the Most High is one attribute with no multiplicity in it according to the People of Truth, like other attributes of meaning. His saying: (and truly distinct) al-Hilali said: For no sensible person doubts that the relation indicated by His saying 'And We sent you as a messenger to the people' [an-Nisa 79] is not the very relation indicated by His saying 'And We sent the winds as pollinators' [al-Hijr 22], and that indicated by His saying 'And We revealed to Ibrahim' [an-Nisa 163] is other than that indicated by His saying 'And your Lord revealed to the bee' [an-Nahl 68]. If the distinction is clear in such examples, how much more so in His saying 'As for those who are blessed' [Hud 108] with His saying 'As for those who are wretched' [Hud 106], and in His saying 'Enter it in peace, secure' [al-Hijr 46] with His saying 'Get out of it, despised and banished' [al-A'raf 18]. And when the distinction is clear with unity of type – I mean the imperative and declarative – then it is even clearer and more evident with difference of type. His saying: (and the eternal speech of Allah is one attribute) i.e., according to the Ash'aris. And a syllogism from the second [figure] is presented here: it is said: These relations indicated by the words of the Quran are truly multiple, and the speech of Allah is not truly multiple, producing from the first of the second: These relations indicated by the words of the Quran are not the speech of Allah.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage continues the argument that the eternal speech cannot be identified with the relations indicated by Quranic sentences. The second reason is based on multiplicity: the relations are numerous and genuinely distinct, whereas the eternal speech is one and simple according to Ash'ari doctrine. The commentator illustrates this with Quranic examples: the relation of sending a messenger is different from sending winds; revelation to Ibrahim differs from revelation to the bee. Even within similar types (e.g., imperative sentences), the relations are distinct. Since the relations are many and the divine speech is one, they cannot be identical. The commentator then formalizes this as a syllogism from the first mood of the second figure (awwal al-thani): 'These relations are truly multiple; the speech of Allah is not truly multiple; therefore, these relations are not the speech of Allah.' The major premise is 'These relations are multiple,' the minor is 'The speech of Allah is not multiple,' and the conclusion follows from the second figure's rule (the middle term is the predicate of both premises). This argument exploits the principle that one simple attribute cannot be the same as many distinct things.

Key Terms
تغاير حقيقي (taghayur haqiqi): Real distinction, meaning that two things are different in their essence, not merely in mental consideration.
أول الثاني (awwal al-thani): The first mood of the second figure of the categorical syllogism, where the major premise is universal affirmative and the minor is universal negative, producing a universal negative conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the syllogism presented in the passage, what is the relationship between the relations indicated by Quranic words and the eternal speech?
A) They are identical because the Quran is the speech of Allah.
B) They are different because the relations are multiple and the speech is one.
C) They are the same in essence but different in manifestation.
D) They are similar but not identical.

Key Takeaway
The second-figure categorical syllogism demonstrates that the many distinct relations in the Quran cannot be the one eternal speech, preserving the Ash'ari doctrine of the uncreated attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Write out the categorical syllogism from the passage in standard form (major premise, minor premise, conclusion). Identify the middle term and confirm it is the predicate in both premises. Then test the syllogism for validity using the rules of the second figure (e.g., one premise must be negative, the conclusion negative).
?
Task 2: Choose another pair of Quranic verses that indicate different relations (e.g., a promise and a threat). Use the same syllogistic form to show that those relations are not the eternal speech. Explain how the multiplicity of relations supports the oneness of the attribute.

The Argument from Changelessness: Divine Speech is Not a Relation
Argument that relations change but divine speech is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the modus tollens argument that divine speech cannot be a relation because it is immutable, and explain the interpretative shift from 'relation' to 'possessor of a relation'.

Core Idea
If divine speech were a relation, it would change; but it is eternal, so it cannot be a relation.

One of the key arguments against defining internal speech (kalam nafsi) as a relation is that relations change when their terms change, whereas Allah's speech is eternal and unchanging. The author presents this third argument and then addresses an apparent counterstatement from Ibn al-Hajib. By understanding this argument and its resolution, you will see how Ash'ari theologians maintained the immutability of divine speech while accounting for its relation to created things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implicit conditional premise
The passage states 'كونه نسبة يقتضي التغير' (its being a relation entails change). This means: If the eternal speech were a relation (in itself), it would change when the terms of the relation change.
The terms here are the subject and object of the relation, e.g., 'Zayd is standing' vs 'Amr is standing'.
2
Identify the denial of the consequent
The passage asserts that 'كلامه تعالى قديم لا يلحقه التغير' (His speech is eternal, change does not affect it). This denies the consequent: the eternal speech does not change.
This is a premise from revelation and theology, not from observation.
3
Conclude the denial of the antecedent
From 'If P then Q' and 'not Q', we conclude 'not P'. Therefore, the eternal speech is not a relation (in itself).
This is a valid modus tollens argument.
4
Analyze the objection from Ibn al-Hajib
Ibn al-Hajib reportedly defined internal speech as 'a relation' (نسبة). The commentators reinterpret this by estimating a genitive construction, so it means 'possessor of a relation' (ذو نسبة).
This preserves the attribute's immutability: the relation is something it has, not what it is.
5
Recognize the subtle ontological shift
The eternal speech is an attribute of meaning (صفة معنى) that subsists in the essence and is related to objects, but it is not identical with the relation. The relation is mental and changeable; the attribute is real and eternal.
This distinction is crucial in Ash'ari kalam to defend the uncreatedness of the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
- وَمِنْهَا : أَنَّ كَوْنَهُ نِسْبَةٌ يَقْتَضِي التَّغَيُّرَ - أَيْ : بِتَغَيرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ، وَكَلَامُهُ تَعَالَى قَدِيمٌ لا يَلْحَقُهُ التَّغَيُّر وَأَمَّا كَلَامُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ فَقَدْ تَأوَّلَهُ المُحَقِّقُونَ مِنْ شُرَّاحِهِ» وَغَيْرِهِمْ: بِأَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنْ تَقْدِيرِ مُضَافٍ؛ أَيْ : ذُو نِسْبَةٍ . قوله : (أَيْ: بِتَغَيُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مثلاً قولك : زَيْدُ قَائِمٌ» نسبة، و: «عَمْرُو قَائِمُ» نسبةٌ أخرى، والطرفان متغايران، فيحصل التغاير في النسبة بسبب تغير الطرفين. قوله : (وَأَمَّا كَلَامُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ فَقَدْ تَأَوَّلَهُ ... إلخ) ممَّن تأوَّله ابن زكري في محصل المقاصد ونصه : قول ابن الحاجب الكلام النَّفسي نسبة يجب ردُّه إن لم يرد أنَّه ذو نسبة. اهـ. وممَّن صرَّح بهذا المضاف صاحب «المراصد» ونصه : وَقُلْ مُعَرِّفُ الكَلَامِ النَّفْسِي وَهُوَ المُضَافُ لِلْجَنَابِ القُدْسِي صِفَةٌ مَعْنَى قَائِمٌ بِالذَّات ذُو نِسْبَةٍ مُنَافِضُ الصفات فتحصل من هذا : أنَّ الكلام القديم ليس هو نفس النسبة ، بل هو صفة معنى متعلّق بالنسبة لا عينها .
- And among them: that its being a relation entails change – i.e., with change of the two terms – and His speech the Most High is eternal, change does not affect it. As for the statement of Ibn al-Hajib, the verifiers among his commentators and others interpreted it as necessitating the estimation of a genitive construction, i.e., 'possessor of a relation'. His saying: (i.e., with change of the two terms, for example your saying 'Zayd is standing' is a relation, and 'Amr is standing' is another relation, and the two terms differ, so change occurs in the relation due to change of the terms. His saying: (As for the statement of Ibn al-Hajib, it has been interpreted...) Among those who interpreted it is Ibn Zakari in Mukhtasar al-Maqasid, whose text is: 'Ibn al-Hajib's statement that the internal speech is a relation must be rejected unless he means that it is possessor of a relation.' End. And among those who explicitly stated this genitive is the author of al-Marasid, whose text is: 'And say defining the internal speech, and it is the genitive of the holy presence, an attribute of meaning subsisting in essence, possessor of a relation, opposite of attributes.' So from this it is concluded that the eternal speech is not the relation itself, but an attribute of meaning related to a relation, not the relation itself.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents the third reason why the eternal speech cannot be a relation: a relation changes when its terms change, but the eternal speech is immutable. For example, the relation 'Zayd is standing' differs from 'Amr is standing' because the terms Zayd and Amr differ. If the eternal speech were a relation, it would have to change with different terms, which contradicts its eternity. The commentator then addresses an objection: Ibn al-Hajib reportedly defined internal speech (kalam nafsi) as a relation. The commentator explains that this statement has been interpreted by verifiers to mean that the internal speech is not the relation itself but rather the 'possessor of a relation' (dhu nisba). In other words, the internal speech is an attribute of meaning that is related to a relation, not the relation itself. This interpretation is supported by quotes from Ibn Zakari and the author of al-Marasid. Thus, even if one accepts Ibn al-Hajib's phrase, the eternal speech remains an attribute, not a mere relation. The distinction is subtle but crucial: the relation is something mental and changeable, whereas the attribute that 'possesses' the relation is real and immutable.

Key Terms
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi): Internal speech; the eternal, unspoken attribute of speech in the Divine Essence, as opposed to the articulated, created words. [Note: This term is central in Ash'ari kalam and is distinct from the mutakallimun's notion of speech as attribute.]
ذو نسبة (dhu nisba): Possessor of a relation; an entity that has a relation without being the relation itself. [Note: This interpretation emerges to reconcile Ibn al-Hajib's wording with Ash'ari orthodoxy.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the change of relations with their terms support the argument that eternal speech is not a relation?
A) Because the eternal speech is unchanging, and if it were a relation it would change.
B) Because relations are mental and eternal speech is external.
C) Because relations are many and eternal speech is one.
D) Because relations are created and eternal speech is not.

Key Takeaway
The eternal speech is not a relation but an immutable attribute that relates to its objects without changing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid modus tollens argument against the view that 'Allah's knowledge is a relation' using the same structure: if knowledge were a relation, it would change when the objects known change; but Allah's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; therefore, it is not a relation (but rather an attribute that possesses relations).
?
Read the statement of Ibn al-Hajib: 'الكلام النفسي نسبة'. Write a brief explanation of how the commentators resolve it by adding the implied word 'ذو' (possessor). In your own words, explain why this reinterpretation is necessary to preserve the belief in an immutable divine speech.

Types of Necessary Conditional (Luzumiyya)
Author defines the necessary conditional and categorizes it by type of connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five types of connection in a necessary conditional (luzumiyya) and recognize the debate over the classification of the sunrise-daytime example.

Core Idea
The necessary conditional has five types of connection: rational, habitual, legal, shared cause, and mutual implication.

We now turn to a finer division of the conditional proposition (muttasila). The necessary conditional (luzumiyya) is defined by a real connection (mu'jib) between antecedent and consequent. This connection can take one of five forms: rational, habitual, legal, shared cause, or mutual implication. Each form is illustrated with examples, and the passage includes a scholarly discussion about whether the connection between sunrise and daytime is rational or habitual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum (luzumiyya)
Locate the term 'luzumiyya' in the Arabic. Note that it is defined as 'the one in which the companionship between its two sides is due to a necessitator'. Understand that this is the genus of conditional propositions.
The author contrasts it with ittifaqiyya (coincidental) which is mentioned implicitly.
2
Extract the defining characteristic: necessitator (mu'jib)
The defining feature of luzumiyya is that the connection is not merely accidental but is caused by a mu'jib, meaning a cause that makes separation impossible. This is the differentia that distinguishes it from the coincidental conditional.
The commentary explains 'mu'jib' as a cause that necessitates the companionship.
3
List the five subtypes of connection
The passage enumerates five kinds of connection: rational ('aqlī), habitual ('ādī), legal (sharʿī), shared cause (ishtirāk fī sabab), and mutual implication (mutadāyifān). For each, note the defining basis: rational necessity, customary occurrence, divine law, common effect, and reciprocal definition.
The first three are introduced with the phrase 'the first of them is a cause for the second, or the opposite', indicating that the causal direction can be reversed.
4
Analyze each example
Read each example and identify which subtype it belongs to: (1) Human → Animal: rational (part-whole). (2) Sunrise → Light: debated (rational or habitual). (3) Sun's decline → Zuhr prayer: legal. (4) Light exists → Stars hidden: shared cause (both caused by the absence of the sun's overpowering light). (5) Zayd is father of Amr → Amr is his son: mutual implication (correlatives).
The debate on sunrise-daytime is a key nuance: some say rational because sun is part of daytime definition; others say habitual because absolute light suffices; legally it is defined differently.
5
Understand the scholarly disagreement on sunrise and daytime
The commentary mentions a difference of opinion: al-Mughnī considers it rational, al-Mukhtasar considers it habitual, and al-Zarqānī notes that legally (sharʿan) daytime starts at dawn, so no necessary correlation. This shows how the same example can be classified differently based on definitions.
This illustrates that the classification of a conditional depends on the nature of the connection, which may vary by perspective.

ثُمَّ هِيَ تَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى لُزُومِيَّةٍ، وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي تَكُونُ الصُّحْبَةُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِمُوجِب»؛ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ الأَوَّلُ مِنْهُمَا سَبَباً لِلنَّانِي، أَوْ عَكْسَهُ عَقْلاً أَوْ عَادَةً أَوْ شَرْعاً، أَوْ يَشْتَرِكُ الطَّرَفَانِ فِي سَبَبٍ، أَوْ يَكُونَا مُتَضَايفَيْنِ : فَالعَقْلِيُّ ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً ، كَانَ حَيَوَاناً». وَالعَادِيُّ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلَّمَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ وُجِدَ الضَّوْءُ». وَالشَّرْعِيُّ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا مَالَتِ الشَّمْسُ وَجَبَتْ صَلَاةُ الظُّهْرِ، وَيَكُونُ الأَوَّلُ مُسَبَّباً عَنِ الثَّانِي فِي عَكْسِ هَذِهِ الأَمْثِلَةِ . قوله : (ثُمَّ هِيَ تَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى لُزُومِيَّةٍ) قد ترك النَّاظم التَّعرُّض لهذا التقسيم، وتعرّض له صاحب القادرية»، وكذا شيخنا سيدى حمدون، ونصه : وَانُسْبُ إِلَى اللُّزُومِ حَيْثُ تُوجَب أَوْ لَا ، فَذِي لِلِاتّفَاقِ تُنْسَب قوله : (لِمُوجِب) أي: لسبب اقتضاها؛ بحيث يتعذَّر انفكاك المستصحب عن صاحبه. : قوله : (فَالعَقْلِيُّ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ إِنْسَاناً ... إلخ) بيانه : أنَّ الحيوان جزء من حقيقة الإنسان، والكلُّ يستحيل أن ينفك عن جزئه . ونظير هذا المثال قولك : كلَّما كانتِ الشَّمس طالعة كان النَّهار موجوداً»، فالتلازم بينهما عقلي كما في المغني»؛ لأنَّ للشَّمس دخلاً في مسمَّى النَّهار ؛ لأنَّ النَّهار - كما قال السَّنوسي في كبراه) : عبارة عن ظهور الشَّمس فوق الأفق، فلها دخل في مسمَّى النَّهار . - وقيل : التَّلازم بينهما عادي، وعليه درج في المختصر » ؛ بناءً على أنَّ مطلق الضوء كافٍ في مسمَّى النَّهار؛ لأنَّ النَّهار هو الزَّمان الذي ينتشر فيه ذلك الضَّوء الخاص، ويمكن أن يخلق الله تعالى ذلك الزَّمان بضوئه المخصوص من غير طلوع شمس، وأن تطلع الشَّمس فوق الآفاق على هيئة النُّجوم بلا نهار وأما من جهة الشَّرع، فلا تلازم بينهما كما قال الزَّرقانيُّ ؛ لأنَّ النَّهار في الشَّرع من طلوع الفجر . وَالمُشْتَرِكَانِ فِي سَبَبٍ ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ الضَّوْءُ مَوْجُوداً، فَالكَوَاكِبُ خَفِيَّةٌ ) . والمُتَضَايفَانِ ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ زَيْدٌ أَباً لِعَمْرٍو، فَعَمْرُو ابْنٌ لَهُ .
Then it divides into: necessary (luzumiyya), which is the one in which the companionship between its two sides is due to a necessitator; that the first of them is a cause for the second, or the opposite, rationally, habitually, or legally, or the two sides share a cause, or they are correlatives. The rational: like 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal.' The habitual: like 'Whenever the sun rises, light exists.' The legal: like 'Whenever the sun declines, the noon prayer becomes obligatory.' And the first is caused by the second in the opposite of these examples. His statement: (Then it divides into necessary) — the author (al-nazim) omitted this division, but the author of al-Qadiriyya and our teacher Sayyidi Hamdun mentioned it, and its text: 'Attributing to necessity where it is obligatory or not, so this one is attributed to coincidence.' His statement: (due to a necessitator) meaning a cause that necessitates it such that the separation of the accompanied from its companion is impossible. His statement: (the rational: like 'Whenever something is a human...') Its explanation: that the animal is part of the reality of the human, and the whole cannot be separated from its part. A similar example is your statement: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' The correlation between them is rational as in al-Mughni; because the sun has a role in the meaning of daytime; since daytime — as al-Sanusi said in his Kubra — is an expression of the sun's appearance above the horizon, so it has a role in the meaning of daytime. And it is said: the correlation between them is habitual, and according to this it is stated in al-Mukhtasar; based on the fact that absolute light suffices for the meaning of daytime; because daytime is the time in which that specific light spreads, and Allah can create that time with its specific light without sunrise, and that the sun rises above horizons in the form of stars without daytime. As for from the legal perspective, there is no correlation between them as al-Zarqani said; because daytime in law is from dawn. The two sharing a cause: like 'Whenever light exists, the stars are hidden.' The correlatives: like 'Whenever Zayd is a father of Amr, then Amr is his son.'
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the division of the conditional proposition (muttasila) into the necessary type (luzumiyya). The author first defines luzumiyya as having a real connection (mu'jib) between antecedent and consequent, then lists five forms of connection: rational, habitual, legal, shared cause, and mutual implication. For each, an example is given: rational (human implies animal), habitual (sunrise implies light), legal (sun's decline implies zuhr prayer), shared cause (light implies hidden stars), mutual implication (fatherhood implies sonship). The commentary then clarifies that the original poet (al-nazim) omitted this subdivision, but later scholars like the author of al-Qadiriyya added it. The gloss explains 'mu'jib' as a cause that makes separation impossible. It also discusses a debated example: the correlation between sunrise and daytime. Some consider it rational because the sun is part of the definition of daytime, others habitual because absolute light suffices, and legally it's defined from dawn. The passage ends with examples of shared cause and mutual implication.

Checkpoint
In the passage, the example 'Whenever light exists, the stars are hidden' belongs to which type of luzumiyya?
A) Rational
B) Habitual
C) Legal
D) Shared cause

Key Takeaway
The necessary conditional (luzumiyya) is defined by a real cause (mu'jib) that can be rational, habitual, legal, shared, or reciprocal, and the same empirical example may be classified differently depending on the perspective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Specific Definition of the Accidental Conditional (Ittifaqiyya)
Definition of the accidental conditional with its specific sense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific definition of the accidental conditional and explain why the connection is not necessary.

Core Idea
The accidental conditional is a conditional where the truth of antecedent and consequent is coincidental with no real connection.

The author begins by defining the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) as one where the companionship between its two sides is without a necessitator. In this lesson, we will break down this definition and examine the classic example of 'Whenever the sun rises, man is an animal'. We will see that neither reason, law, nor custom requires any connection between the two statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'ittifaqiyya' (accidental conditional). Note that it is a type of conditional proposition.
2
Extract the differentia
The definition states that the companionship (suhba) between the two sides is 'without a necessitator' (li-ghayri mujib). This means that the truth of one does not imply the truth of the other.
The commentary clarifies that 'without a necessitator' means no correlation at all.
3
Analyze the example
The example 'Whenever the sun rises, man is an animal' shows that sunrise and man being an animal are simultaneously true but independent. No rational, legal, or habitual connection exists.
The commentary adds a similar example: 'If the sky is above the earth, then honey is sweet.'
4
Contrast with necessary conditional
Unlike the necessary conditional (luzumiyya), where the antecedent entails the consequent, here there is no entailment. This is the key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَى اتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ : الَّتِي تَكُونُ الصُّحْبَةُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِغَيْرِ مُوجِبٍ؛ بِأَنِ اتَّفَقَ فَقَطْ صِدْقُهُمَا فِي الوُجُودِ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ ، فَالإِنْسَانُ حَيَوَانٌ، فَإِنَّ أَحَداً مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لَا يَقْتَضِي الآخَرَ ، لَا عَقْلاً وَلَا شَرْعاً وَلَا عَادَةٌ . وَقَدْ تُفَسَّرُ «الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ) بِأَعَمَّ مِمَّا مَرَّ ، وَهِيَ : أَنْ يَكُونَ تَالِيهَا صَادِقاً، وَلَا يُنَافِي في صِدْقِهِ صِدْقَ المُقَدَّم»، وَفَائِدَتُهَا : رَفْعُ مَا يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنَ التَّنَافُرِ بَيْنَ أَمْرَيْنِ ؛ نَحْوُ : لَوْ لَمْ يَخَفِ اللَّهَ لَمْ يَعْصِهِ» (١) ، قَدْ تُتَوَهَّمُ مُنَافَاةُ عَدَمِ الخَوْفِ لِعَدَمِ المَعْصِيَةِ، وَأَنَّ مَنْ لَمْ يَخَفْ . قوله : (لِغَيْرِ مُوجِب أي بأن لا يقتضي صدق أحدهما الآخر بوجه. قوله : ( كُلَّمَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ ... إلخ) أي : فإنَّ طلوع الشَّمس لا إشعار له بحيوانية الإنسان، ولا يقتضيها لا عقلاً، ولا شرعاً، ولا عادةً. ونظير المثال : إن كانتِ السَّماء فوق الأرض، فالعسل حلو . قوله : ( بِأَعَمَّ) إنَّما كانت أعم؛ لأنَّه اعتبر في الأولى صدق المقدَّم، ولم يعتبر في هذه، بل اللزوم بين التَّالي وبينه ؛ سواءٌ فُرِض صدقه أم لا ؛ كما في قولك: إن كانتِ السَّماء فوق الأرض، فالنبيذُ مسكر . ثمَّ إن إجراء هذه الأسامي - أعني : المتّصلةُ اللُّزوميَّة والاتفاقية ـ على السوالب ليس بحسب مفهوم اللغة، بل بحسب الاصطلاح، فهي مجازاتٌ لغويَّةٌ علاقتها : المشابهة فيما منه التركيب وحقائق اصطلاحية. قوله: (نَحْوُ : «لَوْ لَمْ يَخَفِ اللَّهَ لَمْ يَعْصِهِ» جعله السَّنوسي تبعاً للعقباني حديثاً، ومثله في المطول». وقيل : هو أثر عن عُمر بن الخطاب رضي الله تعالى عنه . وذكر بهاء الدين السبكي في شرح التلخيص، وكذا العراقي ووالده ابن زرعة والسيوطي : يوجد في شيء من كتب الحديث حديثاً، ولا أثراً مع الفحص عنه .
And to the accidental (ittifaqiyya), which is the one in which the companionship between its two sides is without a necessitator; such that their simultaneous truth in existence is mere coincidence; like: 'Whenever the sun rises, man is an animal.' For neither of the two sides necessitates the other, neither rationally, nor legally, nor habitually. And the 'accidental' may be interpreted more generally than what preceded, meaning: that its consequent is true, and its truth does not contradict the truth of the antecedent. Its benefit is to remove what is imagined as repugnance between two things; like: 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him.' It may be imagined that the absence of fear contradicts the absence of disobedience, and that whoever does not fear... His statement: (without a necessitator) meaning that the truth of one does not entail the other in any way. His statement: ('Whenever the sun rises...') meaning: the sunrise does not indicate the animality of man, nor necessitate it, neither rationally, legally, nor habitually. A similar example: 'If the sky is above the earth, then honey is sweet.' His statement: (more general) — it is more general because in the first definition the truth of the antecedent was considered, but here it is not considered; rather the connection is between the consequent and it, whether it is assumed true or not; as in your saying: 'If the sky is above the earth, then wine is intoxicating.' Then, applying these terms — i.e., the necessary and accidental conditional — to negative statements is not according to the linguistic concept, but according to terminology; they are linguistic metaphors with the relation of similarity in composition and terminological realities. His statement: (like: 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him') — al-Sanusi, following al-‘Uqbani, considered it a hadith, and similarly in al-Mutawwal. And it is said that it is a saying of ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him. And Bahauddin al-Subki in Sharh al-Talkhis, and also al-‘Iraqi, his father Ibn Zur‘a, and al-Suyuti mentioned that it is not found in any book of hadith as a hadith or even as a saying after investigation.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage defines the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) as one where there is no real connection between antecedent and consequent; their simultaneous truth is coincidental. The example 'Whenever the sun rises, man is an animal' illustrates that sunrise does not entail humanity. The commentary then presents a broader interpretation of ittifaqiyya: the consequent is simply true, and its truth does not negate the antecedent's truth, used to remove apparent repugnance, e.g., 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him.' The gloss explains that the broader definition is more general because it does not require the antecedent to be true. It then clarifies that applying the terms 'luzumiyya' and 'ittifaqiyya' to negative propositions is terminological, not linguistic; they are metaphors. Finally, the famous example 'Law lam yakhaf Allah lam ya‘sih' is discussed: some scholars considered it a hadith, but after tracing, it is not found in hadith books; it may be a saying of ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab or just a grammatical example.

مُقَدَّم
تَالِي
لُزُومِيَّة

Key Takeaway
The accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) is defined by the absence of any necessitation between antecedent and consequent, making their simultaneous truth coincidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Broader Definition and Application of the Accidental Conditional
Broader definition and application of the accidental conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the broader definition of the accidental conditional to remove apparent repugnance and explain its metaphorical use for negative statements.

Core Idea
The accidental conditional can be used to resolve apparent contradictions by showing that two statements can both be true without conflict.

After the specific definition, the author presents a broader interpretation of the accidental conditional. In this lesson, we explore this broader definition, its purpose of removing imagined repugnance, and the famous example 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him.' We also learn that the terms 'luzumiyya' and 'ittifaqiyya' apply to negative propositions only metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
State the broader definition
The broader definition of ittifaqiyya is: the consequent is true, and its truth does not contradict the truth of the antecedent. This is more general because it does not require the antecedent to be true.
The commentary notes that in the first definition the truth of the antecedent was considered, but here it is not.
2
Identify the purpose (fa'ida)
The purpose of this broader definition is to remove what might be imagined as repugnance (tanafur) between two statements. It shows that two seemingly conflicting truths can coexist.
The example 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him' is used to remove the apparent repugnance between lack of fear and lack of disobedience.
3
Analyze the example
The example shows that someone who lacks fear does not necessarily disobey; the conditional removes the imagined conflict by asserting that both can be true together.
The commentary discusses the provenance of this example, noting it is not a hadith but a statement of 'Umar or a grammatical example.
4
Understand the application to negatives
The terms 'luzumiyya' and 'ittifaqiyya' are applied to negative statements (sawalib) metaphorically, not linguistically. They are terminological realities based on similarity in composition.
This means that when we say 'If not A, then not B', we call it accidental if there is no necessary connection, but this is a technical usage.

وَإِلَى اتِّفَاقِيَّةِ، وَهِيَ : الَّتِي تَكُونُ الصُّحْبَةُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيْهَا لِغَيْرِ مُوجِبٍ؛ بِأَنِ اتَّفَقَ فَقَطْ صِدْقُهُمَا فِي الوُجُودِ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلَّمَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ ، فَالإِنْسَانُ حَيَوَانٌ، فَإِنَّ أَحَداً مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ لَا يَقْتَضِي الآخَرَ ، لَا عَقْلاً وَلَا شَرْعاً وَلَا عَادَةٌ . وَقَدْ تُفَسَّرُ «الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ) بِأَعَمَّ مِمَّا مَرَّ ، وَهِيَ : أَنْ يَكُونَ تَالِيهَا صَادِقاً، وَلَا يُنَافِي في صِدْقِهِ صِدْقَ المُقَدَّم»، وَفَائِدَتُهَا : رَفْعُ مَا يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنَ التَّنَافُرِ بَيْنَ أَمْرَيْنِ ؛ نَحْوُ : لَوْ لَمْ يَخَفِ اللَّهَ لَمْ يَعْصِهِ» (١) ، قَدْ تُتَوَهَّمُ مُنَافَاةُ عَدَمِ الخَوْفِ لِعَدَمِ المَعْصِيَةِ، وَأَنَّ مَنْ لَمْ يَخَفْ . قوله : (لِغَيْرِ مُوجِب أي بأن لا يقتضي صدق أحدهما الآخر بوجه. قوله : ( كُلَّمَا طَلَعَتِ الشَّمْسُ ... إلخ) أي : فإنَّ طلوع الشَّمس لا إشعار له بحيوانية الإنسان، ولا يقتضيها لا عقلاً، ولا شرعاً، ولا عادةً. ونظير المثال : إن كانتِ السَّماء فوق الأرض، فالعسل حلو . قوله : ( بِأَعَمَّ) إنَّما كانت أعم؛ لأنَّه اعتبر في الأولى صدق المقدَّم، ولم يعتبر في هذه، بل اللزوم بين التَّالي وبينه ؛ سواءٌ فُرِض صدقه أم لا ؛ كما في قولك: إن كانتِ السَّماء فوق الأرض، فالنبيذُ مسكر . ثمَّ إن إجراء هذه الأسامي - أعني : المتّصلةُ اللُّزوميَّة والاتفاقية ـ على السوالب ليس بحسب مفهوم اللغة، بل بحسب الاصطلاح، فهي مجازاتٌ لغويَّةٌ علاقتها : المشابهة فيما منه التركيب وحقائق اصطلاحية. قوله: (نَحْوُ : «لَوْ لَمْ يَخَفِ اللَّهَ لَمْ يَعْصِهِ» جعله السَّنوسي تبعاً للعقباني حديثاً، ومثله في المطول». وقيل : هو أثر عن عُمر بن الخطاب رضي الله تعالى عنه . وذكر بهاء الدين السبكي في شرح التلخيص، وكذا العراقي ووالده ابن زرعة والسيوطي : يوجد في شيء من كتب الحديث حديثاً، ولا أثراً مع الفحص عنه .
And to the accidental (ittifaqiyya), which is the one in which the companionship between its two sides is without a necessitator; such that their simultaneous truth in existence is mere coincidence; like: 'Whenever the sun rises, man is an animal.' For neither of the two sides necessitates the other, neither rationally, nor legally, nor habitually. And the 'accidental' may be interpreted more generally than what preceded, meaning: that its consequent is true, and its truth does not contradict the truth of the antecedent. Its benefit is to remove what is imagined as repugnance between two things; like: 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him.' It may be imagined that the absence of fear contradicts the absence of disobedience, and that whoever does not fear... His statement: (without a necessitator) meaning that the truth of one does not entail the other in any way. His statement: ('Whenever the sun rises...') meaning: the sunrise does not indicate the animality of man, nor necessitate it, neither rationally, legally, nor habitually. A similar example: 'If the sky is above the earth, then honey is sweet.' His statement: (more general) — it is more general because in the first definition the truth of the antecedent was considered, but here it is not considered; rather the connection is between the consequent and it, whether it is assumed true or not; as in your saying: 'If the sky is above the earth, then wine is intoxicating.' Then, applying these terms — i.e., the necessary and accidental conditional — to negative statements is not according to the linguistic concept, but according to terminology; they are linguistic metaphors with the relation of similarity in composition and terminological realities. His statement: (like: 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him') — al-Sanusi, following al-‘Uqbani, considered it a hadith, and similarly in al-Mutawwal. And it is said that it is a saying of ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him. And Bahauddin al-Subki in Sharh al-Talkhis, and also al-‘Iraqi, his father Ibn Zur‘a, and al-Suyuti mentioned that it is not found in any book of hadith as a hadith or even as a saying after investigation.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage defines the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) as one where there is no real connection between antecedent and consequent; their simultaneous truth is coincidental. The example 'Whenever the sun rises, man is an animal' illustrates that sunrise does not entail humanity. The commentary then presents a broader interpretation of ittifaqiyya: the consequent is simply true, and its truth does not negate the antecedent's truth, used to remove apparent repugnance, e.g., 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him.' The gloss explains that the broader definition is more general because it does not require the antecedent to be true. It then clarifies that applying the terms 'luzumiyya' and 'ittifaqiyya' to negative propositions is terminological, not linguistic; they are metaphors. Finally, the famous example 'Law lam yakhaf Allah lam ya‘sih' is discussed: some scholars considered it a hadith, but after tracing, it is not found in hadith books; it may be a saying of ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab or just a grammatical example.

تَالِي

سَالِب
لُزُومِيَّة

Grammar vs. Logic: Why a Conditional Remains Conditional
Author responds to objection that conditional becomes categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between grammatical form and logical meaning in conditional propositions and explain why a conditional with a subject pronoun remains logically conditional.

Core Idea
Logical primacy: quantifiers generalize or particularize, not change proposition type.

This passage addresses a confusion: does parsing a conditional with a subject pronoun turn it into a categorical? The author clarifies that logic prioritizes meaning over grammar. You will learn to separate these perspectives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that a conditional proposition parsed with a subject pronoun (ضمير الشأن) becomes categorical because it begins with a subject (مبتدأ).
Example: 'Zayd, if you come to him he honors you.'
2
Understand the grammatical perspective
Grammar requires a subject, so the pronoun is inserted. This is a grammatical convention (رعاية للصناعة النحوية).
3
Recognize the logical perspective
Logic focuses on meaning: quantifiers (أسوار) generalize or particularize the judgment, not change proposition type. The proposition remains conditional.
The intent of quantifiers is تعميم الحكم أو تبعيضه.
4
Apply the distinction
In Islamic logical analysis, prioritize the intended logical scope over grammatical parsing.
Example: In Usul al-Fiqh, conditional legal texts are interpreted by their logical structure, not just grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها
رِعَايَةٌ لِجَانِبِ الصِّنَاعَةِ النَّحَوِيَّةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ القَصْدُ مِنْ تِلْكَ الأَسْوَارِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ تَعْمِيمُ الحُكْمِ أَوْ تَبْعِيضُهُ، لَا مَا فِيهَا مِنَ النِّسْبَةِ.
قوله: (رِعَايَةٌ) لِجَانِبِ الصِّنَاعَةِ النَّحَوِيَّةِ أشار بهذا الكلام إلى جواب عن سؤال، وهو : أنَّ الشَّرطيَّة بذلك الإعراب تصير حمليَّة؛ لأنَّ الحمليَّة هي التي تصدر بمبتدأ، فتكون هذه حملية وقع محمولها شرطيَّة؛ كقولك : زيد أو كأن زيد إن جئته يكرمك.
والجواب : أنَّ ما ذكرناه مِنَ الإعراب رعاية لجانب الصناعة النحوية، ولم يقصد به وضع الاسم الذي هو ضمير الشَّأن؛ ليحمل عليه ما بعده حتّى يلزم ما ذكرت، بل اعتبار المعنى هو المراعى في المنطق، فما القصد من تلك الأسوار إلا بيان تعميم الحكم أو تبعيضه.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings
Consideration for the side of the grammatical art, even though the intent from those quantifiers is only the generalization of the judgment or its particularization, not what is in them of the relationship.
His saying: (Consideration) for the side of the grammatical art, he indicated by this speech a response to a question, namely: that the conditional proposition with that parsing becomes categorical, because the categorical is that which begins with a subject (mubtada'), so this would be a categorical whose predicate is conditional, like your saying: 'Zayd, or as if Zayd, if you come to him he honors you.'
And the answer: that what we mentioned of parsing is a consideration for the side of the grammatical art, and it was not intended by it to posit the noun which is the pronoun of the matter such that what follows is predicated on it so as to necessitate what you mentioned; rather, consideration of meaning is what is observed in logic. So the intent from those quantifiers is only to indicate the generalization of the judgment or its particularization.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the matn (perhaps from al-Sullam) that begins 'Consideration for the side of the grammatical art...' The commentator explains that this refers to a response to an objection: The conditional proposition, when parsed as having a subject pronoun (damir al-sha'n), might be considered a categorical proposition because a categorical proposition is typically defined as one that begins with a subject (mubtada'). This would then make the conditional proposition a categorical one with a conditional predicate (e.g., 'Zayd, if you come to him he honors you'). The objection is that this classification blurs the distinction between conditional and categorical propositions. The answer is that the parsing with a subject pronoun is only a grammatical consideration (ri'āyah li-jānib al-ṣinā'ah al-naḥwiyyah) and is not intended to change the logical nature of the proposition. In logic, the primary concern is the meaning (i'tibār al-ma'nā) – that is, the quantifiers (aswār) are used to generalize or particularize the judgment, not to indicate the type of proposition based on grammatical form. Thus, the conditional proposition remains conditional in its logical structure despite the grammatical appearance. The commentator emphasizes that the purpose of quantifiers is only to indicate the scope of the judgment (general or particular), not the relation within the proposition.

حمليَّة
شَرطيَّة

Key Takeaway
The logical nature of a proposition is determined by its meaning and the function of its quantifiers, not by its grammatical parsing.

When Proof Gives the Converse: Non-First Figure Syllogisms
Illustrates second figure syllogism yielding converse of sought

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a non-first figure categorical syllogism yields the converse of the sought conclusion and apply conversion to obtain the sought.

Core Idea
In non-first figures, the direct conclusion is often not the sought; you must convert it.

When reducing a syllogism from a non-first figure to the first figure, the conclusion may be the converse of what you originally wanted. This lesson uses a concrete second-figure example to show the process of recognizing and correcting this mismatch.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and figure
Examine the given syllogism: 'No stone is an animal' and 'Every human is an animal'. Note that the middle term (animal) is the predicate in both premises, which is characteristic of the second figure.
The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises.
2
Derive the direct conclusion
Apply the second figure rule: the conclusion is a universal negative (E) with the subject from the first premise and predicate from the second. Thus we get 'No stone is a human'.
This is the direct conclusion of the second figure syllogism.
3
Compare to the sought conclusion
The original desired conclusion (the sought) was likely 'No human is a stone'. Notice that the direct conclusion is the converse (subject-predicate swap) of the sought.
The sought is the converse of the direct conclusion.
4
Reduce to the first figure
To reduce the syllogism, convert the minor premise 'No stone is an animal' to its converse: 'No animal is a stone'. Then swap the premises: now 'Every human is an animal' becomes the minor, and 'No animal is a stone' the major. This gives a valid first figure syllogism: 'Every human is an animal, no animal is a stone, therefore no human is a stone'.
Conversion of the minor premise is key for reduction.
5
Obtain the sought by conversion
The first figure conclusion 'No human is a stone' is exactly the sought. Alternatively, one could simply convert the direct second figure conclusion 'No stone is a human' to obtain 'No human is a stone'.
Conversion of the direct conclusion yields the sought directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ : وَمِثَالُ المَظْلُوبِ الَّذِي يَقُومُ الدَّلِيلُ عَلَى عَكْسِهِ مَا يَقَعُ فِي الْأَشْكَالِ الثَّلَاثَةِ غَيْرِ الأَوَّلِ ، فَإِنَّهَا عِنْدَ رَدّهَا لِلأَوَّلِ قَدْ تُنْتِجُ غَيْرَ المَظْلُوبِ، عَلَى مَا ذَكَرُوهُ، وَمِثَالُهُ قَوْلُكَ فِي الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي: «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَانٍ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ يُنْتِجُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانِ، وَيُرَدُّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَجَعْلِهَا كُبْرَى، وَعَكْسُ النَّتِيجَةِ .
قوله : ( وَعَكْسُ النَّتِيجَةِ ) صوابه: «فتنعكس النتيجة) .
He said: An example of the sought for which the proof is established on its converse is what occurs in the three figures other than the first, for when they are reduced to the first they sometimes produce something other than the sought, as they mentioned. Its example is your statement in the second figure: 'No stone is an animal, and every human is an animal' produces 'No stone is a human'; and it is reduced to the first figure by converting the minor premise and making it major, and the conversion of the conclusion. His statement 'and the conversion of the conclusion' should read 'so the conclusion is converted'.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage explains the second case for studying conversion: when the proof aims at the converse of the sought. The author refers to the three figures other than the first (second, third, and fourth). When we reduce a syllogism in these figures to the first figure, we often obtain a conclusion that is not exactly the original sought but its converse. The example uses the second figure: 'No stone is an animal' and 'Every human is an animal' entail 'No stone is a human'. The original sought might have been 'No human is a stone' (the converse). To reduce this to the first figure, one converts the minor premise ('No animal is a human'? Actually, careful: the minor premise is 'No stone is an animal', its conversion is 'No animal is a stone'. Then that becomes the major, and the original major 'Every human is an animal' becomes the minor, yielding 'No human is a stone', which is the converse of the original conclusion. The commentator notes a textual correction: instead of 'wa ʿaks al-natīja', it should read 'fa tanʿakisu al-natīja' (so the conclusion is converted). This demonstrates the practical need for conversion in the art of demonstration.

Key Takeaway
In non-first figure syllogisms, the direct conclusion is often the converse of the sought; proper reduction uses conversion to obtain the desired conclusion.

Defining Qiyas: Genus, Components, and Conditions
Definition of qiyas with genus and differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus, differentia, and components in the definition of qiyas and explain why induction and analogy are not qiyas unless cast in syllogistic form.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a mental statement composed of at least two propositions that necessarily entails another by itself.

This lesson unpacks the classical definition of qiyas (syllogism) from al-Sullam al-Munawraq. You will learn why qiyas is the highest aim of logic, how its definition is structured into genus (qawl) and differentia (composed of propositions entailing another), and what it excludes. Understanding this definition is foundational for distinguishing valid syllogistic arguments from informal reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its importance
The passage states that qiyas is the utmost purpose of logic because it yields tasdiqat (assents), which are nobler than tasawwurat (concepts). Note the reasoning: concepts are only means to assents.
2
Extract the genus (al-qawl)
The definition says qiyas is a qawl (statement) composed of propositions. Understand that qawl here means mental composite, not spoken utterance. The commentator clarifies that the spoken qiyas is only qiyas insofar as it indicates the mental one.
In logic, qawl often means mental speech; in grammar and usul, it means spoken utterance.
3
Extract the differentia: composition and entailment
The definition has two differentiae: 'composed from propositions' excludes single propositions and incomplete composites like genitive constructions. 'Entailing by itself another statement' excludes statements that do not necessitate a conclusion, such as induction and analogy when not in syllogistic form.
'By itself' (bil-dhat) means the entailment is intrinsic, not dependent on external conditions.
4
Analyze the number of propositions
The phrase 'min qadaya' (from propositions) is plural. The commentator presents two interpretations: either the plural is used for the dual (two propositions) or 'propositions' is a genus encompassing any number. In either case, at least two premises are required for a simple syllogism, and compound syllogisms (more than two) are also included.
The term 'composed' (muʾallaf) restricts the definition to a specific arrangement of propositions.
5
Apply the definition to examples
Consider the grammatical rule 'al-fāʿil marfūʿ' (the subject is nominative). This is not qiyas because it is based on induction, not composed of propositions that entail another statement by itself. Contrast with a theological syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated.' This meets the definition.
Induction (istiqrāʾ) and analogy (tamthīl) become qiyas only when their premises are cast in syllogistic form and produce necessary entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابٌ فِي القِيَاسِ
هَذَا هُوَ المَقْصِدُ (۱) الأَقْصَى، وَالمَطْلَبُ الأَعْلَى مِنْ هَذَا الفَنِّ؛ إِذْ هُوَ العُمْدَةُ فِي تَحْصِيلِ المَطَالِبِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ. وَهُوَ فِي اللُّغَةِ مَصْدَرُ قَاسَ الشَّيْءَ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ أَيْ : قَدَّرَهُ بِقَدْرِهِ.
وَفِي العُرْفِ مَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ النَّاظِمُ بِقَوْلِهِ :
( ( (۷۳) إِنَّ القِيَاسَ مِنْ قَضَايَا صُوِّرًا مُسْتَلْزِماً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرَا
فَجُمْلَهُ صُوِّرًا مِنْ قَضَايَا فِي مَحَلِّ صِفَةٍ لِمُقَدَّرٍ ؛ أَيْ: قَوْلُ صُوِّرَ مِنْ قَضَايَا». و«القَوْلُ» جِنْسٌ لِلقِيَاسِ المَعْقُولِ، كَمَا أَنَّهُ جِنْسٌ لِلقِيَاسِ المَلْفُوظِ، وَالمُرَادُ هُنَا الأَوَّلُ ؛ لِقَوْلِ السَّيِّدِ إِنَّ إِطْلَاقَ القِيَاسِ عَلَى المَلْفُوظِ مَجَازٌ لِدَلَالَتِهِ عَلَى المَعْقُولِ .
قصارة.
بَابٌ فِي القِيَاسِ
قوله : (هُوَ المَقْصِدُ الأَقْصَى) أي: لأنَّ التَّصورات لا تطلب في العلوم إلا لكونها وسائل إلى التصديقات.
استقراء
قوله : (إِذْ هُوَ العُمْدَةُ فِي تَحْصِيلِ المَطَالِبِ ) أي : دون غيره مما يوصل إلى التصديق مين وتمثيل، فهو إذن مقصود أعظم في هذا الفنّ بالقياس إلى التصورات وبالقياس إلى سائر ما يوصل إلى التصديقات، ولذا جعلوا الاستقراء والتمثيل من لواحق القياس كما سيأتي .
ومسائلُ النَّحو مبنيّة على الاستقراء كقولهم: «الفاعل مرفوع».

قوله : ( وَالقَوْلُ» جنس ... إلخ) معناه : أنَّ القول المقدَّر يشمل المعقول والملفوظ؛ لأنَّ القول يُطلق عليهما، والقياس الملفوظ إنَّما هو قياسٌ مِن حيث إنَّه دالّ على معنى معقول، وهو جنس يتناول كل مركَّب، ولا يسمى غير المركَّب في عرفهم قولاً، ولا يصح جعل القول هنا شاملاً للنوعين؛ لأنَّ فيه جمع حقيقتين في تعريف، فلذا قال الشارح: «والمراد هنا ... إلخ».

وَقَوْلُهُ : مِنْ قَضَايَا يُخْرِجُ : القَضِيَّةَ الوَاحِدَةَ، وَالمُرَكَّبَ النَّاقِصَ كَالإِضَافِي، وَمُرَادُهُ : مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ فَأَكْثَرَ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ التَّنْنِيَةَ جَمْعٌ فِي المَعْنَى ، أَوِ المُرَادُ : مِنْ جِنْسِ القَضَايَا ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَهُوَ شَامِلٌ لِلقِيَاسِ البَسِيطِ، وَهُوَ مَا تَرَكَّبَ مِنْ قَضِيّتَيْنِ، وَلِلْمُرَكَّبِ وَهُوَ مَا تَرَكَّبَ مِنْ أَكْثَرَ ، وَسَيَذْكُرُهُ النَّاظِمُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَمِنْهُ مَا يَدْعُونَهُ مُرَكَّبًا ... إلخ». وَقَوْلُهُ: «مُسْتَلْزِماً» يُخْرِجُ : الأَقْوَالَ المُرَكَّبَةَ الَّتي لَا تَسْتَلْزِمُ قَوْلاً آخَرَ، وَلَوْ كَانَتْ تُسَمَّى : ( دَلِيلاً) كَالِاسْتِقْرَاءِ وَالتَّمْثِيلِ ؛ إِذَا لَمْ يُفْرَغَا فِي قَالَبِ القِيَاسِ المَنْطِقِيِّ، فَإِنَّهُمَا بَعْدَ تَسْلِيمٍ مُقَدِّمَاتِهِمَا لَا يُقْطَعُ بِلُزُومِ المَدْلُولِ لَهُمَا ، وَيَأْتِي بَيَانُهُمَا إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى فِي كَلَامِ النَّاظِمِ.
قصارة.
قوله : (بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ التَّنْنِيَةَ جَمْعٌ فِي المَعْنَى) أي : فيكون أطلق الجمع وأراد الثثنية، وكثيراً ما يُستعمل .

فإن قيل : إذا أُريد الجنس دخلت القضيَّة الواحدة، والإجماع على اشتراط اثنين فقط . فجوابه : أنَّ قوله : «صور» يرفع ذلك؛ لأنه بمعنى ألف، ولا يعني مطلق التأليف، بل تأليفاً مخصوصاً هو مِنَ القضايا كما أفاده تقييده بذلك .
Chapter on Qiyas. This is the utmost purpose and the highest aim of this art; for it is the mainstay in obtaining the assentive (tasdiqī) aims, which are nobler than the conceptual (tasawwurī). And it is in language the verbal noun of 'he measured something against something', i.e., he estimated it by its measure. And in conventional usage, it is what the versifier indicated by his saying: 'Indeed qiyas is from propositions composed, necessarily entailing by essence another statement.' So His phrase 'composed from propositions' is in the position of an adjective for an elided noun; i.e., 'a statement composed from propositions.' And 'statement' (al-qawl) is a genus for the mental qiyas, just as it is a genus for the spoken qiyas, and what is meant here is the first; due to the sayyid's saying that applying qiyas to the spoken is metaphorical because it indicates the mental. [Marginal note]. Chapter on Qiyas. His saying: 'It is the utmost purpose' means: because concepts are not sought in the sciences except as means to assents. Induction? His saying: 'For it is the mainstay in obtaining the aims' means: to the exclusion of other things that lead to assent, like induction and analogy, so it is therefore a greater purpose in this art relative to concepts and relative to all other things that lead to assents, and therefore they made induction and analogy as appendages to qiyas as will come. And the issues of grammar are based on induction like their saying: 'The subject is in the nominative case.' And analogy (tamthīl) is the uṣūlī qiyas, which is applying a known to a known due to its equality in the effective cause of its ruling according to the one who applies; like their saying: 'Nabidh is forbidden like wine, due to the common factor of intoxication.' [Footnote: In the Fez edition: 'al-maqṣūd' instead of 'al-maqṣid'.] His saying: 'And al-qawl as genus, etc.' its meaning: that the elided qawl includes the mental and the spoken; because qawl is applied to both, and the spoken qiyas is only a qiyas in that it indicates a mental meaning, and it is a genus encompassing every composite, and nothing other than composite is called qawl in their convention, and it is not correct to make qawl here inclusive of both types, because that would bring together two realities in a definition, so the commentator said: 'What is meant here, etc.' And His saying: 'from propositions' excludes: the single proposition and the incomplete composite like the genitive construction, and his intent is: from two or more propositions, based on the view that the dual is plural in meaning, or the intent is: from the genus of propositions, and in any case it includes the simple qiyas, which is composed of two propositions, and the compound, which is composed of more, and the versifier will mention it in his saying: 'And from it what they call compound, etc.' And His saying: 'entailing' excludes: the composite statements that do not entail another statement, even if they are called 'evidence', like induction and analogy, when they are not cast in the mold of logical qiyas, for they, after their premises are conceded, one is not certain of the entailment of the intended conclusion for them, and their clarification will come, God willing, in the words of the versifier. [Marginal note]. His saying: 'Based on the view that the dual is plural in meaning' means: so he used the plural and intended the dual, and it is often used. His saying: 'Or the intent is from the genus of propositions' means: so it is not restricted to what the word plural indicates of three or more, and this is like your saying: 'Speech (al-kalām) is composed of noun, verb, and particle', even if their togetherness is not required. Then if it is said: If the genus is intended, the single proposition would enter, and there is consensus on the condition of exactly two. So the answer: that His saying: 'composed' removes that; because it means 'composed', and it does not mean absolute composition, but a specific composition which is from propositions, as is indicated by its restriction by that.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the chapter opening of qiyas (syllogism) from al-Sullam al-Munawraq. The author first states the paramount importance of qiyas as the chief tool for acquiring tasdiqāt (assentive knowledge), which are more noble than tasawwurāt (concepts), since concepts are only sought as means to assents. He then gives the linguistic meaning of qiyas as 'measuring something against something' and the technical definition as per the versifier's line: 'Indeed qiyas is from propositions composed, necessarily entailing by essence another statement.' The commentator unpacks this definition: 'statement' (qawl) is the genus, and he clarifies that it refers to mental speech, not spoken utterance, because the latter only indicates the mental qiyas. He then explains each part: 'from propositions' excludes a single proposition and incomplete compounds, and it means at least two propositions, whether simple (two premises) or compound (more than two). 'Entailing' excludes other composite statements like induction and analogy that do not necessitate their conclusion unless cast in syllogistic form. The commentator also addresses an objection regarding the use of the plural 'propositions' — whether it means two specifically or the genus — and resolves it by noting that the term 'composed' restricts the composition to a proper arrangement of propositions.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in the passage, which of the following is NOT excluded by the phrase 'mustalzim bi al-dhat' (entailing by itself)?
A) A single proposition
B) An incomplete composite like the genitive construction
C) Induction and analogy when not in syllogistic form
D) A compound syllogism of three premises

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is defined as a mental statement composed of at least two propositions that necessarily entails another conclusion by itself, distinguishing it from single propositions, incomplete composites, and non-entailing argument forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a simple categorical syllogism from a known Islamic legal example (e.g., 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is intoxicating like wine') and verify that it meets the conditions of the definition: genus (statement), at least two propositions, and necessary entailment.
?
Explain why the grammatical rule 'al-fāʿil marfūʿ' (the subject is nominative) is not a qiyas but is based on induction, using the definition's exclusion of non-entailing composites.

The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
Definition of Qiyas and the Condition of Essential Entailment
Author defines qiyas with the condition that conclusion follows essentially, excluding material or extraneous consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (bi al-dhat) and accidental conclusions in syllogisms, identifying when a conclusion follows from the form versus from material or extraneous premises.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires the conclusion to follow from the premises themselves, not from specific terms or an added premise.

The definition of qiyas includes the condition 'bi al-dhat' (essentially). This crucial condition excludes conclusions that follow due to the specific material (madda) of the premises or that require an extraneous premise (muqaddima ghariba). Understanding this distinction separates rigorous logical proof from accidental truth. We will examine examples of each exclusion and clarify why the first figure is not dependent on any foreign premise.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the definiendum and condition
The term being defined is qiyas. The condition 'bi al-dhat' restricts the definition to syllogisms where the conclusion follows essentially from the premises themselves, not from external factors.
2
2. Understand the first exclusion – material dependence
The example 'No human is a stone, no stone is a neighing thing' yields a true conclusion, but if you replace 'neighing' with 'rational', the conclusion becomes false while premises remain true. This shows the entailment depends on the specific terms, not the logical form. Such conclusions are not essential.
The passage calls this 'due to specificity of material' (li-khusus al-madda).
3
3. Understand the second exclusion – extraneous premise
The 'syllogism of equality' (qiyas al-musawat) requires a foreign premise such as 'Everything equal to the equal of a thing is equal to that thing.' Without this premise, the conclusion does not follow. Therefore, the inference is not essential to the given premises.
The passage includes additional examples where the foreign premise is false, so no conclusion follows.
4
4. Recognize the objection about the first figure
One might object that the first figure also needs an extraneous premise: 'What falls under what falls under is falling under.' The answer: that premise is not foreign; it defines the syllogistic form itself, explaining why the conclusion necessarily follows in any material.
5
5. Apply the definition to classify syllogisms
The definition includes complete syllogisms (first figure) and incomplete ones (other figures) that require conversion but not a foreign premise. Distinguish: conversion is internal to the premises, while an extraneous premise is external.
This prepares the student for later discussion of reducing figures to the first.

Source Text

وَقَوْلُهُ: «بِالذَّاتِ» مُخْرِجٌ : لِلقَوْلِ المُؤَلَّفِ مِنْ قَضَايَا إِذَا لَزِمَ مِنْهُ قَوْلُ آخَرُ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ أَوْ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ غَرِيبَةٍ، فَالأَوَّلُ كَقَوْلِنَا : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِصَاهِلٍ ، فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُهُ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِصَاهِلٍ، وَهُوَ صَادِقٌ لَكِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ، لَا لِذَاتِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ؛ [بِدَلِيل أَنَّكَ لَوْ جَعَلْتَ النَّاطِقَ فِي الكُبْرَى بَدَلِ الصَّاهِلِ ، لَكَذَبَتِ النَّتِيجَةُ مَعَ صِدْقِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ] وَأَمَّا الثَّانِي فَالمُرَادُ بِالمُقَدِّمَةِ الغَرِيبَةِ أَنْ تَكُونَ أَجْنَبِيَّةٌ غَيْرَ لَازِمَةٍ لِإِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَي القِيَاسِ ؛ كَقِيَاسِ المُسَاوَاةِ، وَهُوَ مَا تَرَكَّبَ مِنْ قَضِيّتَيْنِ ... مُتَعَلَّقُ مَحْمُولٍ أَوْلَاهُمَا يَكُونُ مَوْضُوعَ الأُخْرَى ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : الإِنْسَانُ مُسَاوِ لِلنَّاطِقِ، وَالنَّاطِقُ مُسَاوِ لِلكَاتِبِ»، فَيَلْزَمُهُمَا : الإِنْسَانُ مُسَاوِ لِلكَاتِبِ، لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِمَا ، بَلْ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ ، وَهِيَ : «أَنَّ كُلَّ مُسَاوِ لِمُسَاوِي الشَّيْء، مُسَاوِ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء»، وَلِهَذَا لَمْ يَتَحَقَّقْ ذَلِكَ اللُّزُومُ إِلَّا حَيْثُ تَصْدُقُ هَذِهِ المُقَدِّمَةُ ، نَحْوُ : التَّغَيُّرُ مَلْزُومٌ لِلحُدُوثِ، وَالحُدُوتُ مَلْزُومٌ لِلاِفْتِقَارِ) يَسْتَلْزِمُ : «التَّغَيُّرُ مَلْزُومٌ لِلافْتِقَارِ) بِوَاسِطَةِ : أَنَّ مَلْزُومَ المَلْزُومِ مَلْزُومٌ». : وَأَمَّا إِذَا لَمْ تَصْدُقْ تِلْكَ المُقَدِّمَةُ الأَجْنَبِيَّةُ ، فَلَا يَحْصُلُ مِنْهُ شَيْءٌ ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : الإِنْسَانُ مُبَايِنُ لِلْفَرَسِ ، وَالفَرَسُ مُبَايِنُ لِلنَّاطِقِ فَلَا يَلْزَمُهُ : أَنَّ الإِنْسَانَ مُبَاينٌ لِلنَّاطِقِ»؛ لِعَدَم صِدْقِ : أَنَّ مُبَايِنَ المُبَايِنِ مُبَايِنٌ»، وَكَقَوْلِنَا : الثَّلَاثَةُ نِصْفُ سِتَّةٍ، وَالسِّنَّةُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَى عَشَرَ، فَلَا يَصْدُقُ : «الثَّلَاثَةُ نِصْفُ الإِثْنَي عَشَرَا؛ لِعَدَمِ صِدْقٍ: «أَنَّ نِصْفَ النّصْفِ نِصْفُ . فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : وَكَذَا الإِنْتَاجُ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ ، وَهِيَ : أَنَّ المُنْدَرِجَ تَحْتَ المُنْدَرِحِ مُنْدَرج . أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ أَجْنَبِيَّةٌ، بَلْ هِيَ مَفْهُومُ صُورَةِ القِيَاسِ، فَهِيَ بَيَانُ لِلزُومِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِصُورَةِ القِيَاسِ فِي أَيِّ مَادَّةٍ فُرِضَتْ . وَدَخَلَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ : القِيَاسُ الكَامِلُ كَالشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَغَيْرُ الكَامِل وَهُوَ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ إِنْتَاجُهُ عَلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ غَيْرِ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ ، بَلْ عَكْساً لإحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كَمَا يَأْتِي فِي رَدِّ الأَشْكَالِ غَيْرِ الأَوَّلِ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ.
And his statement: 'essentially' excludes the composite statement from premises when another statement follows from it not essentially, but due to specificity of material or through an extraneous premise. The first is like our saying: 'No human is a stone, and no stone is a neighing thing,' for it entails: 'No human is a neighing thing,' and it is true, but it is a consequence due to the specificity of the material, not due to the essence of the two premises; [evidence that if you made 'rational' in the major instead of 'neighing,' the conclusion would be false while the premises are true]. As for the second, what is meant by the extraneous premise is that it be foreign, not necessary to either of the two premises of the syllogism, like the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions... the attached of the predicate of the first becomes the subject of the second; like our saying: 'Human is equal to rational, and rational is equal to writer,' so they entail: 'Human is equal to writer,' but not essentially, rather through a foreign premise, which is: 'Everything equal to the equal of a thing is equal to that thing.' Therefore that entailment is only realized where this premise is true, such as: 'Change is necessary for temporal origination, and temporal origination is necessary for dependence' entails: 'Change is necessary for dependence' through the mediation that 'the necessary of the necessary is necessary.' As for when that foreign premise is not true, nothing results from it; like our saying: 'Human is separate from horse, and horse is separate from rational' does not entail: 'Human is separate from rational' because it is not true that 'the separate of the separate is separate'; and like our saying: 'Three is half of six, and six is half of twelve' does not yield: 'Three is half of twelve' because it is not true that 'half of a half is half.' If you say: 'Similarly, the production in the first figure is through a premise, which is: That which falls under what falls under is falling under,' the answer is: that it is not foreign; rather it is the understood meaning of the form of the syllogism, so it is an explanation of the entailment of the conclusion from the form of the syllogism in any material assumed. And included in the definition is the complete syllogism like the first figure, and the incomplete, which is that whose production depends on a premise that is not foreign, but rather a conversion of one of the two premises, as will come in reducing the other figures to the first.
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the definition of qiyas (syllogism) from the matn, focusing on the condition that the conclusion must follow 'bi al-dhat' (essentially). The author explains two categories of inference that do not satisfy this condition. First, those that follow due to specific material (madda) — the example uses 'No human is a stone, no stone is a neighing thing' which yields a true conclusion, but if we replace 'neighing' with 'rational' the conclusion becomes false, showing the entailment depends on the particular terms, not the syllogistic form. Second, those that follow through an extraneous premise (muqaddima ghariba), which is a foreign premise not implied by either of the original premises. The primary example is qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality), where the conclusion holds only if we supply an external premise like 'Everything equal to the equal of a thing is equal to that thing.' The author gives additional examples where the extraneous premise is false, so no conclusion follows. Then an objection is raised: in the first figure of the categorical syllogism, isn't there also an extraneous premise, namely 'that which falls under what falls under is falling under'? The answer is that this premise is not foreign; it is the very meaning of the syllogistic form, explaining why a conclusion necessarily follows from any material. Finally, the author notes that the definition includes both the complete syllogism (first figure, which requires no further proof) and the incomplete syllogisms (second and third figures) that need conversion to be reduced to the first figure.

Key Terms
بالذات
مقدمة غريبة
قياس المساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following conclusions follows essentially (bi al-dhat)?
A) 'No human is a stone, no stone is a neighing thing, therefore no human is a neighing thing.'
B) 'Human is equal to rational, rational is equal to writer, therefore human is equal to writer.'
C) 'Change is necessary for temporal origination, temporal origination is necessary for dependence, therefore change is necessary for dependence.'
D) 'All A are B, all B are C, therefore all A are C' (first figure syllogism).

Key Takeaway
Essential entailment means the conclusion follows from the logical form of the syllogism, not from the specific terms or an added premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following conclusion follows essentially or not: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, every nabidh is an intoxicant, therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Justify by checking if the conclusion would still follow if you replace 'intoxicant' with 'grape' and 'nabidh' with 'date-wine' while keeping the same logical form (i.e., both premises remain true).
?
The syllogism of equality 'A is equal to B, B is equal to C, therefore A is equal to C' requires an extraneous premise. Write down that extraneous premise and explain why it is foreign to the given premises, using an Islamic example such as 'Human is equal to rational, rational is equal to writer'.
