The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Condition of Lizatiha in Qiyas
Author explains lizatiha condition with external premise examples and defends first figure against a critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish whether a syllogism's conclusion follows by virtue of the premises themselves or relies on an external premise.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism must produce its conclusion solely from its premises.

This lesson explores the first essential condition of a syllogism: that the conclusion follows 'by virtue of the premises themselves' (lizatiha). The author provides examples where an external premise is needed, and addresses an objection about the first figure. Understanding this condition helps you identify genuine logical entailment.

Guided Path
1
Examine the definition of qiyas
Begin with the definition: a discourse composed of premises that, when granted, necessarily entail another statement by virtue of themselves. The key phrase is 'by virtue of themselves' (lizatiha).
2
Analyze examples with external premises
Look at the example: 'Man is equal to rational, rational is equal to writer' yields 'Man is equal to writer'. The author says this conclusion does NOT follow lizatiha, because it relies on an external premise: 'Everyone equal to what is equal to a thing is equal to that thing'. Identify the external premise.
This shows that some apparent syllogisms depend on an additional principle.
3
Test cases where external premise is false
Consider 'Man is separate from horse, horse is separate from rational'. The supposed conclusion 'Man is separate from rational' does not follow because the external premise 'The separate of the separate is separate' is false. Verify this by checking whether the external premise is universally true.
The falsity of the external premise blocks the implication.
4
Address the objection about the first figure
An objector claims that first figure syllogisms also depend on an external premise: 'That which falls under what falls under is falling under'. The response says this premise is not external; it is inherent in the form of the syllogism. Understand why it is considered part of the form, not an external addition.
This clarifies that the first figure's structure itself guarantees entailment.

باب في القياس. مُتَعَلَّقُ مَحْمُولٍ أَوْلَاهُمَا يَكُونُ مَوْضُوعَ الأُخْرَى؛ كَقَوْلِنَا: الإِنْسَانُ مُسَاوِ لِلنَّاطِقِ، وَالنَّاطِقُ مُسَاوِ لِلكَاتِبِ، فَيَلْزَمُهُمَا: الإِنْسَانُ مُسَاوِ لِلكَاتِبِ، لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِمَا، بَلْ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ، وَهِيَ: «أَنَّ كُلَّ مُسَاوِ لِمُسَاوِي الشَّيْءِ مُسَاوِ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ»، وَلِهَذَا لَمْ يَتَحَقَّقْ ذَلِكَ اللُّزُومُ إِلَّا حَيْثُ تَصْدُقُ هَذِهِ المُقَدِّمَةُ. نَحْوُ: التَّغَيُّرُ مَلْزُومٌ لِلحُدُوثِ، وَالحُدُوثُ مَلْزُومٌ لِلاِفْتِقَارِ، يَسْتَلْزِمُ: «التَّغَيُّرُ مَلْزُومٌ لِلافْتِقَارِ» بِوَاسِطَةِ: أَنَّ مَلْزُومَ المَلْزُومِ مَلْزُومٌ. وَأَمَّا إِذَا لَمْ تَصْدُقْ تِلْكَ المُقَدِّمَةُ الأَجْنَبِيَّةُ، فَلَا يَحْصُلُ مِنْهُ شَيْءٌ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا: الإِنْسَانُ مُبَايِنُ لِلْفَرَسِ، وَالفَرَسُ مُبَايِنُ لِلنَّاطِقِ فَلَا يَلْزَمُهُ: أَنَّ الإِنْسَانَ مُبَايِنٌ لِلنَّاطِقِ؛ لِعَدَمِ صِدْقِ: أَنَّ مُبَايِنَ المُبَايِنِ مُبَايِنٌ. وَكَقَوْلِنَا: الثَّلَاثَةُ نِصْفُ سِتَّةٍ، وَالسِّتَّةُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَى عَشَرَ، فَلَا يَصْدُقُ: «الثَّلَاثَةُ نِصْفُ الإِثْنَي عَشَرَ»؛ لِعَدَمِ صِدْقِ: «أَنَّ نِصْفَ النِّصْفِ نِصْفُ». فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: وَكَذَا الإِنْتَاجُ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ، وَهِيَ: أَنَّ المُنْدَرِجَ تَحْتَ المُنْدَرِجِ مُنْدَرِجٌ. أُجِيبَ: بِأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ أَجْنَبِيَّةٌ، بَلْ هِيَ مَفْهُومُ صُورَةِ القِيَاسِ، فَهِيَ بَيَانُ لِلُزومِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِصُورَةِ القِيَاسِ فِي أَيِّ مَادَّةٍ فُرِضَتْ. وَدَخَلَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ: القِيَاسُ الكَامِلُ كَالشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَغَيْرُ الكَامِلِ وَهُوَ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ إِنْتَاجُهُ عَلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ غَيْرِ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ، بَلْ عَكْساً لِإِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كَمَا يَأْتِي فِي رَدِّ الْأَشْكَالِ غَيْرِ الأَوَّلِ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ. قوله: (مُتَعَلَّقُ مَحْمُولِ أُوْلَاهُمَا يَكُونُ مَوْضُوعَ الأُخْرَى) بيانُهُ في المثال: أنَّ المتعلَّق هو الناطق، والمحمول هو مساو. قوله: (فَإِنْ قُلْتَ ... إلخ) أصل هذا الإيراد للمغيلي، والذي أجاب عنه بما ذكر الهلالي، وزاد: فإنَّ الاندراج المذكور مأخوذ مِنَ المقدمتين، لا من خارج. قوله: (القِيَاسُ الكَامِلُ) المراد بـ «الكامل»: ما لا يتوقف في إنتاجه على بيان، بل يكفي وضع مقدماته في تسليم نتيجته كالشَّكل الأوَّل. قوله: (كَمَا يَأْتِي فِي رَدِّ الأَشْكَالِ غَيْرِ الأَوَّلِ) أي: عند غير القائل بأنَّ إنتاج الثَّاني والثالث بيِّن في نفسه، غنيٌّ عنِ الرَّدِّ إلى الأوَّل، وأمَّا عنده فيكونان كاملين. وَقَوْلُهُ: قَوْلاً آخَرَ يُخْرِجُ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِاعْتِبَارِ اسْتِلْزَامِهِمَا لِإِحْدَاهِمَا اسْتِلْزَامَ الكُلِّ لِجُزْئِهِ، فَلَوْلَا هَذَا القَيْدُ لَدَخَلَ كُلَّ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَتَا عَلَى تَأْلِيفِ قِيَاسٍ مُنْتِجِ أَمْ لَا. قَالَ السَّعْدُ: وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ؛ لِأَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ عَنِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، فَإِنَّ مَعْنَى اللُّزُومِ عَنْهُمَا أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُمَا دَخْلٌ فِي ذَلِكَ، وَظَاهِرُ أَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الأُخْرَى لَا دَخْلَ لَهَا فِي ذَلِكَ. اهـ. وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ قَوْلُهُ: «آخَر» لِمُجَرَّدِ البَيَانِ، لَا لِلإِخْرَاجِ. وَأُوْرِدَ عَلَى اشْتِرَاطِ المُغَايَرَةِ: أَنَّا إِذَا قُلْنَا: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، فَإِنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ عَيْنَ الكُبْرَى. وَالجَوَابُ: أَنَّا نَمْنَعُ كَوْنَ هَذَا وَنَحْوِهِ قِيَاساً؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانِ إِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِقَضِيَّةٍ؛ إِذْ لَا بُدَّ فِي القَضِيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ مِنْ تَغَايُرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مَفْهُوماً، وَاتِّحَادِهِمَا مَصْدُوقاً، وَحَيْثُ كَانَا مُتَّحِدَيْنِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ بَطْلَ حَمْلُ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ؛ لِعَرَائِهِ عَنِ الفَائِدَةِ. وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ: القِيَاسَ الصَّادِقَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَغَيْرَهُ. قوله: (آخر) أي: مغاير وفيه إشارة إلى وجوب مغايرة النتيجة لكلِّ مِنَ المقدمتين؛ لأنَّ النَّتيجة غير مفروضة التسليم بخلاف المقدِّمتين، ولأنَّه لو لم تعتبر المغايرة، لزم أن يكون كلُّ قضيَّتين قياساً؛ كقولنا: كلُّ إنسان حيوان وكل حجر جماد، فإنَّهما يستلزمان إحداهما؛ ضرورة استلزام الكل للجزء، لكن إحداهما غير مغايرة فخرجت بقيد المغايرة كما أشار إلى ذلك الشارح بقوله: وقوله: قولاً آخر يخرج المقدمتين. قوله: (وَظَاهِرٌ أَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الأُخْرَى لَا دَخْلَ لَهَا فِي ذَلِكَ) أي بخلاف القياس، فإنَّ لكلِّ من مقدمتيه على الانفراد دخلاً في النتيجة؛ لأنَّ أخذ الجزأين مِنَ الصُّغرى، والآخر مِنَ الكبرى. قوله: (فَإِنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ عَيْنَ الكُبْرَى) أي: لا قولاً آخر مغايراً. قوله: (مِنْ تَغَايُرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مَفْهُوماً، وَاتِّحَادِهِمَا مَصْدُوقاً) أي: تغايرهما ذهناً واتحادهما خارجاً، وحيث اتحدا في الذهن وفي الخارج فليست بقضيَّة، كما يدلُّ عليه ما تقدم في مبحث القضايا. قوله: (الصَّادِقَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَغَيْرَهُ) فالصَّادق المقدّمات كقولنا: كلُّ إنسان حيوان، وكلُّ حيوان جسم»، وغيره هو القياس الكاذب المقدمات كقول القائل: كلُّ إنسان فرس، وكل فرس صهال»؛ لأنَّ القياس من حيث هو قياس إنَّما يجب أن يؤخذ بحيث يشمل البرهاني والجدلي ... إلخ، وذلك لأنَّ معنى لزوم الشَّيء للشَّيء هو كون الشَّيء بحيث لو وجد لازمه، وإن كان لا يوجد هو ولا لازمه.
Chapter on syllogism. The attachment of the predicate of the first of them is the subject of the other; as our saying: 'Man is equal to rational, and rational is equal to writer,' so it follows from them: 'Man is equal to writer,' but not by virtue of themselves, rather by means of an external premise, which is: 'Everyone equal to what is equal to a thing is equal to that thing.' And for this reason that implication is not realized except where this premise is true. An example: 'Change is a necessary concomitant of origination, and origination is a necessary concomitant of neediness' entails 'Change is a necessary concomitant of neediness' by means of: 'The necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is a necessary concomitant.' But if that external premise is not true, then nothing results from it; as our saying: 'Man is separate from horse, and horse is separate from rational,' so it does not follow that man is separate from rational, because 'the separate of the separate is separate' is not true. And as our saying: 'Three is half of six, and six is half of twelve,' so 'Three is half of twelve' is not true, because 'half of a half is half' is not true. If you say: Likewise production in the first figure is by means of a premise, namely: 'That which falls under what falls under is falling under.' The answer: That premise is not external; rather it is the concept of the form of the syllogism, so it is an explanation of the implication of the conclusion from the form of the syllogism in any matter assumed. And included in the definition is the complete syllogism, like the first figure, and the incomplete, which is that whose production depends on a premise that is not external, but rather the converse of one of the two premises, as will come in the reduction of the figures other than the first to the first. His saying: (The attachment of the predicate of the first of them is the subject of the other) its explanation in the example is that the attached is 'rational' and the predicate is 'equal'. His saying: (If you say... etc.) The origin of this objection is from al-Maghili, and al-Hilali answered it with what was mentioned, adding: The mentioned falling under is taken from the two premises, not from outside. His saying: (The complete syllogism) By 'complete' is meant that which does not depend in its production on explanation, rather it suffices to posit its premises for its conclusion to be conceded, like the first figure. His saying: (As will come in the reduction of the figures other than the first) i.e., according to one who does not hold that the production of the second and third is evident in itself, and thus in need of reduction to the first. According to him, they are complete. And his saying: 'another statement' excludes the two premises by considering that they imply one of them as the whole implies its part. For were it not for this condition, every two propositions would be included, whether they are in the composition of a productive syllogism or not. Sa'd said: There is consideration of this; for we do not grant that they follow from the two premises, for the meaning of implication from them is that they have a role in that, and it is apparent that the other premise has no role in that. Thus his saying 'another' is only for clarification, not for exclusion. An objection was raised against the requirement of difference: When we say 'Every man is a man, and every man is an animal,' it produces the very major premise. The answer: We deny that this and the like are syllogisms, because 'Every man is a man' is not a proposition, for in an affirmative proposition there must be a difference of the two terms in concept and their unity in reference, and when they are identical in every respect, predicating one of the other is invalid, due to its lack of benefit. And the definition includes the syllogism with true premises and others. His saying: (another) i.e., different, and it contains an indication of the necessity of the result being different from each of the two premises, because the result is not assumed to be conceded unlike the two premises, and because if difference were not considered, every two propositions would be a syllogism, as our saying: 'Every man is an animal and every stone is a lifeless thing,' for they imply one of them, necessarily by the whole implying the part, but one of them is not different, so it is excluded by the condition of difference, as the commentator pointed out by his saying: 'And his saying: another statement excludes the two premises.' His saying: (And it is apparent that the other premise has no role in that) i.e., unlike the syllogism, for each of its two premises individually has a role in the result, because one part is taken from the minor and the other from the major. His saying: (For it produces the very major premise) i.e., not another different statement. His saying: (From the difference of the two terms in concept and their unity in reference) i.e., their difference mentally and their unity externally. When they are identical mentally and externally, it is not a proposition, as indicated earlier in the section on propositions. His saying: (The syllogism with true premises and others) So the one with true premises like our saying: 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is a body,' and other is the syllogism with false premises like the saying of one who says: 'Every man is a horse, and every horse is a neighing being,' because the syllogism insofar as it is a syllogism must be taken so as to include the demonstrative and dialectical etc., and that is because the meaning of one thing's implication for another is that the thing is such that if its implicans exists, although neither it nor its implicans may exist.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the definition of qiyas (syllogism) and its essential conditions. The author begins by clarifying a key aspect of the syllogism: the relation between the subject and predicate in the premises. He illustrates with an example: 'Man is equal to rational, and rational is equal to writer,' which yields 'Man is equal to writer,' but not directly by virtue of the premises themselves; rather, an external premise is needed: 'Everyone equal to what is equal to a thing is equal to that thing.' This shows that the implication is not 'by virtue of the premises themselves' (lizatihima) because it depends on an external principle. The author then gives examples where such an external premise is false, such as 'Man is separate from horse, and horse is separate from rational' — here the conclusion does not follow because the principle 'the separate of the separate is separate' is untrue. Similarly, 'Three is half of six, and six is half of twelve' does not yield 'Three is half of twelve' because 'half of a half is half' is not generally true. The text then addresses an objection: is not the first figure's production also dependent on an external premise, namely 'that which falls under what falls under is falling under'? The answer is that this premise is not external; it is inherent in the form of the syllogism itself, serving as an explanation of how the conclusion follows from that form in any matter. The author then distinguishes between a complete syllogism (like the first figure) where the conclusion follows directly from the premises without further conversion, and an incomplete syllogism (like the second and third figures) where production depends on converting one premise or reducing to the first figure. The condition of 'another statement' (qawlan akhar) is then discussed: the conclusion must be a statement different from each premise. This excludes cases where two premises merely imply one of them (as the whole implies its part). Sa'd al-Din Taftazani is cited questioning this, arguing that the implication of one premise by the two premises is not a true implication because the other premise has no role. Hence the word 'another' is for clarification not exclusion. An objection is raised: suppose we say 'Every man is a man, and every man is an animal' — this yields the major premise itself, not a different statement. The answer is that 'Every man is a man' is not a genuine proposition because in an affirmative proposition the subject and predicate must differ conceptually while referring to the same thing; if they are identical in both concept and reference, the predication is useless. The definition is then said to include both syllogisms with true premises (like demonstrative) and false premises (like sophistical), because the definition considers the form, not the truth of the matter.

لذاتها
مقدمة أجنبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the argument 'Man is separate from horse, and horse is separate from rational' not yield 'Man is separate from rational'?
A) Because the external premise 'the separate of the separate is separate' is not universally true.
B) Because the premises are false.
C) Because the conclusion is not distinct from the premises.
D) Because the syllogism is not in the first figure.

Key Takeaway
A genuine syllogism must entail its conclusion by virtue of its own premises, not by relying on external assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Does the conclusion follow lizatiha (by virtue of the premises themselves) or does it depend on an external premise? Explain your reasoning.
?
Construct a two-premise argument of your own on a fiqh topic that appears to produce a conclusion but actually requires an external premise (like a transitivity principle). Then state the missing premise.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Condition of Maghayara: The Conclusion Must Be Distinct
Author explains the maghayara condition, responds to Sa'd's critique, and addresses an objection about identical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that the conclusion of a syllogism must be distinct from each premise to tests whether a given pair of propositions qualifies as a syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism's conclusion must be a new statement, not a repetition of a premise.

The second essential condition of a syllogism is that the conclusion must be 'another statement' (qawlan akhar), meaning it must differ from each premise in wording and meaning. This lesson examines why this condition is necessary, how it excludes trivial cases, and how scholars like Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani refined the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of maghayara
The definition of qiyas includes 'another statement' (qawlan akhar) to indicate that the conclusion must be different from each premise. Understand that this excludes cases where two premises simply imply one of them, as the whole implies a part.
The phrase 'another' explicitly requires difference.
2
Examine the argument for excluding trivial cases
Consider two propositions like 'Every man is an animal' and 'Every stone is a lifeless thing'. These two premises logically imply 'Every man is an animal' (the first premise) because the whole implies its part. But since that conclusion is not distinct from a premise, this pair does not form a syllogism. The condition of maghayara filters out such cases.
The whole-part implication is not sufficient for a syllogism.
3
Analyze Sa'd al-Din's objection
Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani questions whether the two premises truly imply one of them. He argues that implication from two premises requires both to have a role; but in the whole-part case, the other premise (e.g., 'Every stone is a lifeless thing') has no role in implying the first premise. So the phrase 'another' is just for clarification, not for exclusion.
This critique refines the understanding of what 'implication from premises' means.
4
Handle the objection about identical predication
An objector says: if we say 'Every man is a man, and every man is an animal', the conclusion is 'Every man is an animal', which is the major premise itself, so it's not a distinct statement. The answer is that 'Every man is a man' is not a genuine proposition because the subject and predicate are identical in concept and reference, making it useless. Thus, it does not count as a premise.
This shows that the condition of maghayara also guards against vacuous predication.

باب في القياس. مُتَعَلَّقُ مَحْمُولٍ أَوْلَاهُمَا يَكُونُ مَوْضُوعَ الأُخْرَى؛ كَقَوْلِنَا: الإِنْسَانُ مُسَاوِ لِلنَّاطِقِ، وَالنَّاطِقُ مُسَاوِ لِلكَاتِبِ، فَيَلْزَمُهُمَا: الإِنْسَانُ مُسَاوِ لِلكَاتِبِ، لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِمَا، بَلْ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ، وَهِيَ: «أَنَّ كُلَّ مُسَاوِ لِمُسَاوِي الشَّيْءِ مُسَاوِ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ»، وَلِهَذَا لَمْ يَتَحَقَّقْ ذَلِكَ وَالفَرَسُ مُبَايِنُ لِلنَّاطِقِ فَلَا يَلْزَمُهُ: أَنَّ الإِنْسَانَ مُبَايِنٌ لِلنَّاطِقِ؛ لِعَدَمِ صِدْقِ: أَنَّ مُبَايِنَ المُبَايِنِ مُبَايِنٌ. وَكَقَوْلِنَا: الثَّلَاثَةُ نِصْفُ سِتَّةٍ، وَالسِّتَّةُ نِصْفُ الاثْنَى عَشَرَ، فَلَا يَصْدُقُ: «الثَّلَاثَةُ نِصْفُ الإِثْنَي عَشَرَ»؛ لِعَدَمِ صِدْقِ: «أَنَّ نِصْفَ النِّصْفِ نِصْفُ». فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: وَكَذَا الإِنْتَاجُ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ، وَهِيَ: أَنَّ المُنْدَرِجَ تَحْتَ المُنْدَرِجِ مُنْدَرِجٌ. أُجِيبَ: بِأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ أَجْنَبِيَّةٌ، بَلْ هِيَ مَفْهُومُ صُورَةِ القِيَاسِ، فَهِيَ بَيَانُ لِلُزومِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِصُورَةِ القِيَاسِ فِي أَيِّ مَادَّةٍ فُرِضَتْ. وَدَخَلَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ: القِيَاسُ الكَامِلُ كَالشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَغَيْرُ الكَامِلِ وَهُوَ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ إِنْتَاجُهُ عَلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ غَيْرِ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ، بَلْ عَكْساً لِإِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كَمَا يَأْتِي فِي رَدِّ الْأَشْكَالِ غَيْرِ الأَوَّلِ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ. قوله: (مُتَعَلَّقُ مَحْمُولِ أُوْلَاهُمَا يَكُونُ مَوْضُوعَ الأُخْرَى) بيانُهُ في المثال: أنَّ المتعلَّق هو الناطق، والمحمول هو مساو. قوله: (فَإِنْ قُلْتَ ... إلخ) أصل هذا الإيراد للمغيلي، والذي أجاب عنه بما ذكر الهلالي، وزاد: فإنَّ الاندراج المذكور مأخوذ مِنَ المقدمتين، لا من خارج. قوله: (القِيَاسُ الكَامِلُ) المراد بـ «الكامل»: ما لا يتوقف في إنتاجه على بيان، بل يكفي وضع مقدماته في تسليم نتيجته كالشَّكل الأوَّل. قوله: (كَمَا يَأْتِي فِي رَدِّ الأَشْكَالِ غَيْرِ الأَوَّلِ) أي: عند غير القائل بأنَّ إنتاج الثَّاني والثالث بيِّن في نفسه، غنيٌّ عنِ الرَّدِّ إلى الأوَّل، وأمَّا عنده فيكونان كاملين. وَقَوْلُهُ: قَوْلاً آخَرَ يُخْرِجُ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِاعْتِبَارِ اسْتِلْزَامِهِمَا لِإِحْدَاهِمَا اسْتِلْزَامَ الكُلِّ لِجُزْئِهِ، فَلَوْلَا هَذَا القَيْدُ لَدَخَلَ كُلَّ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَتَا عَلَى تَأْلِيفِ قِيَاسٍ مُنْتِجِ أَمْ لَا. قَالَ السَّعْدُ: وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ؛ لِأَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ عَنِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، فَإِنَّ مَعْنَى اللُّزُومِ عَنْهُمَا أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُمَا دَخْلٌ فِي ذَلِكَ، وَظَاهِرُ أَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الأُخْرَى لَا دَخْلَ لَهَا فِي ذَلِكَ. اهـ. وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ قَوْلُهُ: «آخَر» لِمُجَرَّدِ البَيَانِ، لَا لِلإِخْرَاجِ. وَأُوْرِدَ عَلَى اشْتِرَاطِ المُغَايَرَةِ: أَنَّا إِذَا قُلْنَا: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، فَإِنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ عَيْنَ الكُبْرَى. وَالجَوَابُ: أَنَّا نَمْنَعُ كَوْنَ هَذَا وَنَحْوِهِ قِيَاساً؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ إِنْسَانِ إِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِقَضِيَّةٍ؛ إِذْ لَا بُدَّ فِي القَضِيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ مِنْ تَغَايُرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مَفْهُوماً، وَاتِّحَادِهِمَا مَصْدُوقاً، وَحَيْثُ كَانَا مُتَّحِدَيْنِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ بَطْلَ حَمْلُ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ؛ لِعَرَائِهِ عَنِ الفَائِدَةِ. وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ: القِيَاسَ الصَّادِقَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَغَيْرَهُ. قوله: (آخر) أي: مغاير وفيه إشارة إلى وجوب مغايرة النتيجة لكلِّ مِنَ المقدمتين؛ لأنَّ النَّتيجة غير مفروضة التسليم بخلاف المقدِّمتين، ولأنَّه لو لم تعتبر المغايرة، لزم أن يكون كلُّ قضيَّتين قياساً؛ كقولنا: كلُّ إنسان حيوان وكل حجر جماد، فإنَّهما يستلزمان إحداهما؛ ضرورة استلزام الكل للجزء، لكن إحداهما غير مغايرة فخرجت بقيد المغايرة كما أشار إلى ذلك الشارح بقوله: وقوله: قولاً آخر يخرج المقدمتين. قوله: (وَظَاهِرٌ أَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الأُخْرَى لَا دَخْلَ لَهَا فِي ذَلِكَ) أي بخلاف القياس، فإنَّ لكلِّ من مقدمتيه على الانفراد دخلاً في النتيجة؛ لأنَّ أخذ الجزأين مِنَ الصُّغرى، والآخر مِنَ الكبرى. قوله: (فَإِنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ عَيْنَ الكُبْرَى) أي: لا قولاً آخر مغايراً. قوله: (مِنْ تَغَايُرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مَفْهُوماً، وَاتِّحَادِهِمَا مَصْدُوقاً) أي: تغايرهما ذهناً واتحادهما خارجاً، وحيث اتحدا في الذهن وفي الخارج فليست بقضيَّة، كما يدلُّ عليه ما تقدم في مبحث القضايا. قوله: (الصَّادِقَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَغَيْرَهُ) فالصَّادق المقدّمات كقولنا: كلُّ إنسان حيوان، وكلُّ حيوان جسم»، وغيره هو القياس الكاذب المقدمات كقول القائل: كلُّ إنسان فرس، وكل فرس صهال»؛ لأنَّ القياس من حيث هو قياس إنَّما يجب أن يؤخذ بحيث يشمل البرهاني والجدلي ... إلخ، وذلك لأنَّ معنى لزوم الشَّيء للشَّيء هو كون الشَّيء بحيث لو وجد لازمه، وإن كان لا يوجد هو ولا لازمه.
Chapter on syllogism. The attachment of the predicate of the first of them is the subject of the other; as our saying: 'Man is equal to rational, and rational is equal to writer,' so it follows from them: 'Man is equal to writer,' but not by virtue of themselves, rather by means of an external premise, which is: 'Everyone equal to what is equal to a thing is equal to that thing.' And for this reason that implication is not realized except where this premise is true. An example: 'Change is a necessary concomitant of origination, and origination is a necessary concomitant of neediness' entails 'Change is a necessary concomitant of neediness' by means of: 'The necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is a necessary concomitant.' But if that external premise is not true, then nothing results from it; as our saying: 'Man is separate from horse, and horse is separate from rational,' so it does not follow that man is separate from rational, because 'the separate of the separate is separate' is not true. And as our saying: 'Three is half of six, and six is half of twelve,' so 'Three is half of twelve' is not true, because 'half of a half is half' is not true. If you say: Likewise production in the first figure is by means of a premise, namely: 'That which falls under what falls under is falling under.' The answer: That premise is not external; rather it is the concept of the form of the syllogism, so it is an explanation of the implication of the conclusion from the form of the syllogism in any matter assumed. And included in the definition is the complete syllogism, like the first figure, and the incomplete, which is that whose production depends on a premise that is not external, but rather the converse of one of the two premises, as will come in the reduction of the figures other than the first to the first. His saying: (The attachment of the predicate of the first of them is the subject of the other) its explanation in the example is that the attached is 'rational' and the predicate is 'equal'. His saying: (If you say... etc.) The origin of this objection is from al-Maghili, and al-Hilali answered it with what was mentioned, adding: The mentioned falling under is taken from the two premises, not from outside. His saying: (The complete syllogism) By 'complete' is meant that which does not depend in its production on explanation, rather it suffices to posit its premises for its conclusion to be conceded, like the first figure. His saying: (As will come in the reduction of the figures other than the first) i.e., according to one who does not hold that the production of the second and third is evident in itself, and thus in need of reduction to the first. According to him, they are complete. And his saying: 'another statement' excludes the two premises by considering that they imply one of them as the whole implies its part. For were it not for this condition, every two propositions would be included, whether they are in the composition of a productive syllogism or not. Sa'd said: There is consideration of this; for we do not grant that they follow from the two premises, for the meaning of implication from them is that they have a role in that, and it is apparent that the other premise has no role in that. Thus his saying 'another' is only for clarification, not for exclusion. An objection was raised against the requirement of difference: When we say 'Every man is a man, and every man is an animal,' it produces the very major premise. The answer: We deny that this and the like are syllogisms, because 'Every man is a man' is not a proposition, for in an affirmative proposition there must be a difference of the two terms in concept and their unity in reference, and when they are identical in every respect, predicating one of the other is invalid, due to its lack of benefit. And the definition includes the syllogism with true premises and others. His saying: (another) i.e., different, and it contains an indication of the necessity of the result being different from each of the two premises, because the result is not assumed to be conceded unlike the two premises, and because if difference were not considered, every two propositions would be a syllogism, as our saying: 'Every man is an animal and every stone is a lifeless thing,' for they imply one of them, necessarily by the whole implying the part, but one of them is not different, so it is excluded by the condition of difference, as the commentator pointed out by his saying: 'And his saying: another statement excludes the two premises.' His saying: (And it is apparent that the other premise has no role in that) i.e., unlike the syllogism, for each of its two premises individually has a role in the result, because one part is taken from the minor and the other from the major. His saying: (For it produces the very major premise) i.e., not another different statement. His saying: (From the difference of the two terms in concept and their unity in reference) i.e., their difference mentally and their unity externally. When they are identical mentally and externally, it is not a proposition, as indicated earlier in the section on propositions. His saying: (The syllogism with true premises and others) So the one with true premises like our saying: 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is a body,' and other is the syllogism with false premises like the saying of one who says: 'Every man is a horse, and every horse is a neighing being,' because the syllogism insofar as it is a syllogism must be taken so as to include the demonstrative and dialectical etc., and that is because the meaning of one thing's implication for another is that the thing is such that if its implicans exists, although neither it nor its implicans may exist.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the definition of qiyas (syllogism) and its essential conditions. The author begins by clarifying a key aspect of the syllogism: the relation between the subject and predicate in the premises. He illustrates with an example: 'Man is equal to rational, and rational is equal to writer,' which yields 'Man is equal to writer,' but not directly by virtue of the premises themselves; rather, an external premise is needed: 'Everyone equal to what is equal to a thing is equal to that thing.' This shows that the implication is not 'by virtue of the premises themselves' (lizatihima) because it depends on an external principle. The author then gives examples where such an external premise is false, such as 'Man is separate from horse, and horse is separate from rational' — here the conclusion does not follow because the principle 'the separate of the separate is separate' is untrue. Similarly, 'Three is half of six, and six is half of twelve' does not yield 'Three is half of twelve' because 'half of a half is half' is not generally true. The text then addresses an objection: is not the first figure's production also dependent on an external premise, namely 'that which falls under what falls under is falling under'? The answer is that this premise is not external; it is inherent in the form of the syllogism itself, serving as an explanation of how the conclusion follows from that form in any matter. The author then distinguishes between a complete syllogism (like the first figure) where the conclusion follows directly from the premises without further conversion, and an incomplete syllogism (like the second and third figures) where production depends on converting one premise or reducing to the first figure. The condition of 'another statement' (qawlan akhar) is then discussed: the conclusion must be a statement different from each premise. This excludes cases where two premises merely imply one of them (as the whole implies its part). Sa'd al-Din Taftazani is cited questioning this, arguing that the implication of one premise by the two premises is not a true implication because the other premise has no role. Hence the word 'another' is for clarification not exclusion. An objection is raised: suppose we say 'Every man is a man, and every man is an animal' — this yields the major premise itself, not a different statement. The answer is that 'Every man is a man' is not a genuine proposition because in an affirmative proposition the subject and predicate must differ conceptually while referring to the same thing; if they are identical in both concept and reference, the predication is useless. The definition is then said to include both syllogisms with true premises (like demonstrative) and false premises (like sophistical), because the definition considers the form, not the truth of the matter.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'Every man is a man' not considered a valid premise in a syllogism?
A) Because it is false.
B) Because its subject and predicate are identical in concept and reference, making the predication useless.
C) Because it is a tautology that is always true.
D) Because it is not an affirmative proposition.

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism must produce a conclusion that is genuinely new and distinct from both premises, ensuring that logical inference adds knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair of propositions: 'Every Muslim must pray' and 'Every living thing is mortal.' Does this pair constitute a valid syllogism? Apply the condition of maghayara to explain why or why not.
?
Give an example of a supposed syllogism where the conclusion is identical to one of the premises (like the 'Every man is a man' example). Explain why it fails the maghayara condition and why the problematic premise is not a genuine proposition.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Condition of a New Conclusion in Qiyas
Commentator defines qiyas and explains the condition that conclusion must be a new statement distinct from premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that the conclusion of a syllogism must be distinct from each premise and apply it to exclude trivial syllogisms.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism produces a new statement, not a repetition of a premise.

In defining qiyas (syllogism), the logicians specify that the conclusion must be 'another statement' distinct from the premises. This subtle condition ensures that trivial combinations of propositions are not considered syllogisms. The commentary by al-Bajuri, with a noted objection by al-Sa'd al-Taftazani, refines this condition and defends it against counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qiyas
The passage defines qiyas as a composition of premises that entail a conclusion distinct from each premise. Find the phrase 'قولاً آخر' (another statement) that marks the condition.
2
Understand the condition of distinctness
The condition excludes cases where the conclusion is the same as one premise. For example, if the premises are 'Every intoxicant is haram' and 'This is intoxicating', the conclusion 'This is haram' is new. But if one premise is 'Every human is a human', that premise cannot even be used because it is not a proper proposition (its terms are identical in concept and referent).
The passage uses 'Every human is a human' as an example of an invalid premise.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objector might say: if we say 'Every human is a human' and 'Every human is an animal', the conclusion 'Every human is an animal' is exactly the major premise. The answer: 'Every human is a human' is not a valid proposition because its subject and predicate are identical in both concept and referent, making the predication futile. Thus, the syllogism fails at the premise level.
This shows that the condition of a proper affirmative proposition requires conceptual difference between subject and predicate.
4
Appreciate the scope of the definition
The definition covers all types of syllogisms: demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, poetical, and sophistical, regardless of truth of premises.
This inclusiveness is important because it means the definition applies to all five logical arts.

وَقَوْلُهُ: قَوْلاً آخَرَ يُخْرِجُ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِاعْتِبَارِ اسْتِلْزَامِهِمَا لِإِحْدَاهِمَا اسْتِلْزَامَ الكُلِّ لِجُزْئِهِ، فَلَوْلَا هَذَا القَيْدُ لَدَخَلَ كُلَّ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَتَا عَلَى تَأْلِيفِ قِيَاسٍ مُنْتِجِ أَمْ لَا . قَالَ السَّعْدُ : وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ ؛ لِأَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ عَنِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ، فَإِنَّ مَعْنَى اللُّزُومِ عَنْهُمَا أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُمَا دَخْلٌ فِي ذَلِكَ، وَظَاهِرُ أَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الأُخْرَى لَا دَخْلَ لَهَا فِي ذَلِكَ . اهـ . وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ قَوْلُهُ: «آخَر» لِمُجَرَّدِ البَيَانِ، لَا لِلإِخْرَاجِ. وَأُوْرِدَ عَلَى اشْتِرَاطِ المُغَايَرَةِ : أَنَّا إِذَا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ»، فَإِنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ عَيْنَ الكُبْرَى. وَالجَوَابُ : أَنَّا نَمْنَعُ كَوْنَ هَذَا وَنَحْوِهِ قِيَاساً ؛ لِأَنَّ كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ إِنْسَانُ لَيْسَ بِقَضِيَّةٍ ؛ إِذْ لَا بُدَّ فِي القَضِيَّةِ المُوجَبَةِ مِنْ تَغَايُرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مَفْهُوماً ، وَاتِّحَادِهِمَا مَصْدُوقاً، وَحَيْثُ كَانَا مُتَّحِدَيْنِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهِ بَطْلَ حَمْلُ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ؛ لِعَرَائِهِ عَنِ الفَائِدَةِ. وَشَمَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ : القِيَاسَ الصَّادِقَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَغَيْرَهُ، البُرْهَانِيَّ، وَالجَدَلِيَّ، وَالخِطَابِيَّ، وَالشَّعْرِيَّ، وَالسُّوفُسْطَاني.
And his statement: 'another statement' excludes the two premises by virtue of their entailment of one of them, like the entailment of the whole for its part. For were it not for this condition, every two propositions would enter [into the definition], whether they are in the composition of a productive syllogism or not. Al-Sa'd said: There is consideration in this, because we do not concede that it necessarily follows from the two premises, for the meaning of 'following from them' is that they both have a part in that, and it is apparent that the other premise has no part in that. End. And then his statement 'another' is for mere clarification, not for exclusion. And an objection was raised against the condition of difference: that if we say 'Every human is a human' and 'Every human is an animal', it produces exactly the major premise. And the answer: We deny that this and the like is a syllogism, because 'Every human is a human' is not a proposition, since in an affirmative proposition the two terms must differ in concept and be identical in referent, and when they are identical in every respect, predicating one of the other is invalid because it is devoid of benefit. And the definition includes the syllogism with true premises and other than it: the demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, poetical, and sophistical.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qiyas' (syllogism) and explains a key condition: the conclusion must be a new statement distinct from each premise. The commentator begins by glossing the matn's phrase 'another statement' (قولاً آخر), which ensures that the two premises themselves are not considered the result of entailment. Without this condition, any two propositions would count as a syllogism, since one necessarily implies itself (the whole implies its part). Al-Sa'd al-Taftazani objects: the entailment is not from both premises; rather, the conclusion is entailed by one premise alone, so the condition is not truly excluding but merely clarifying. The commentator then discusses an objection about the condition of difference (مغايرة). If one says 'Every human is a human' and 'Every human is an animal', the supposed conclusion 'Every human is an animal' is exactly the major premise, not a new statement. The answer is that 'Every human is a human' is not a valid proposition because its subject and predicate are identical in concept and referent, making the predication useless. In a proper affirmative proposition, the terms must differ in concept (ذهنا) but be identical in referent (خارجا). Finally, the definition of qiyas is said to encompass both true and false premises, and thus includes the five arts: demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, poetical, and sophistical. This passage is typical of a sharh expanding on the matn's definition, refining the conditions and defending them against objections.

Key Terms
قياس
مغايرة
لزوم
قضية موجبة

Checkpoint

A) Because it is not a categorical statement.
B) Because its subject and predicate are identical in concept and referent, making the predication futile.
C) Because it is negative in form.
D) Because it is not about universals.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism requires that the conclusion be a new statement, meaning it must differ in wording and meaning from each premise; otherwise, it is not a valid inference.

The Three Names of the Conclusion
Author explains three stages of naming the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the stage of an argument based on whether the conclusion is called da'wa, matlub, or nati'a.

Core Idea
One proposition, three names: claim, demand, result.

In Islamic logic, the same statement changes its technical name as the argument progresses. Recognizing these names helps you follow the flow of reasoning in debates and proofs. This passage explains the three terms and their contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition in question
The conclusion is a single proposition (e.g., 'the world is created'). Note that it remains the same content throughout.
2
Check the temporal stage
Before any proof is given, the proposition is a 'da'wa' (claim). Once the speaker starts arguing, it becomes 'matlub' (demanded). After the argument is complete, it becomes 'nati'a' (result) or 'hujja' (proof).
The stage is determined by the progress of the argument, not the speaker's intention.
3
Apply to a concrete example
Take the proposition 'Nabidh is intoxicating'. If I assert it without proof, it's a da'wa. If I then provide evidence (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' ...), it becomes matlub. After the syllogism is finished, it is nati'a.
This classification is used in jadal to track what has and hasn't been established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ تُسَمَّى قَبْلَ الشُّرُوعِ فِي الاِسْتِدْلَالِ : دَعْوَى، وَبَعْدَ الشُّرُوعِ فِيهِ : مَظْلُوباً، وَبَعْدَ تَكْمِيلِهِ : نَتِيجَةٌ. قوله : (دَعْوَى) أي : أنَّ النتيجَةَ التي هي الحكم المطلوب ثبوته تسمى قبل الشروع في الاستدلال على ثبوتها : دعوى؛ أي: لأنَّ المتكلّم ادَّعى ثبوتها بلا إقامة دليل، وبعد أن يشرع الاستدلال وقبل تمامه تسمى : مطلوباً؛ لأنَّها لمَّا سيق الدليل على ثبوتها، صارت مطلوبة الثبوت، وبعد أن يكمل الاستدلال ويفرغ منه تسمى : نتيجة، و : حجة؛ لأنَّ مَن تمسك بها حجَّ خصمه؛ أي: غلبه.
Know that the conclusion is called 'claim' (da'wa) before embarking on the argument, 'demanded' (matlub) after embarking on it, and 'result' (nati'a) after its completion. His statement: ('da'wa')—that is, the conclusion, which is the judgment whose proof is sought, is called 'claim' before embarking on its proof, because the speaker claimed its truth without presenting a proof. After beginning the argument and before its completion, it is called 'demanded' because when the proof for its truth is adduced, it becomes demanded of proof. After completing the argument and finishing from it, it is called 'result' and 'proof' (hujja) because whoever holds to it refutes his opponent, i.e., overcomes him.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage introduces the terminology used for the conclusion of a syllogism in different stages. The commentator notes that the same proposition—the conclusion—has three names depending on when it is considered. Before the argument begins, it is called 'da'wa' (claim), because the speaker asserts it without having yet provided evidence. Once the argument starts, it becomes 'matlub' (demanded or sought), since now the speaker is actively trying to prove it. After the argument is completed, it is called 'nati'a' (result) and also 'hujja' (proof), because it now serves to convince or overcome the opponent. This terminological distinction is important for understanding the logical process and the rhetorical context of jadal (dialectic). The commentary clarifies that the word 'da'wa' comes from the speaker's claim without evidence, while 'hujja' indicates that the established conclusion refutes the adversary.

Key Terms
دعوى
مطلوب
نتيجة
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only in dialectical syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion's name changes with the argument's progress: da'wa (claim), matlub (demanded), nati'a (result/ proof).

The Syllogism

The Twofold Division of Syllogism: Iqtirani and Istithna'i
Author divides syllogism into conjunctive and exceptive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i) syllogisms based on whether the conclusion is present actually or potentially.

Core Idea
A syllogism either contains its conclusion actually (istithna'i) or only potentially (iqtirani).

All syllogisms must entail their conclusions, but the manner of inclusion differs. This lesson clarifies the foundational division of qiyas into two types, a classification that underpins how later logicians analyze arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the necessity of inclusion
The author states that a syllogism must include the conclusion or its contradictory, or else it would not be self-entailing. Why is this condition essential for any valid syllogism?
Think about what would happen if a syllogism did not contain the conclusion even potentially—it would not lead to any necessary result.
2
Identify the two modes of inclusion
Inclusion can be either actual (bi'l-fi'l) or potential (bi'l-quwwa). Which mode corresponds to each division? Iqtirani includes potentially; istithna'i includes actually.
The passage only defines iqtirani, but the division is binary.
3
Match the divisions to the definitions
Iqtirani syllogisms contain the conclusion's matter (terms) but not its form (propositional structure). Istithna'i syllogisms contain the conclusion or its contradictory as an explicit proposition.
This distinction is crucial for determining whether a conclusion is directly stated or must be derived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ القِيَاسُ عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ: فَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِ: الاقْتِرَانِي (٧٥) وَهُوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَتِيجَةِ بِقُوَّةِ وَاخْتَصَّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ اعْلَمْ أَنَّ القِيَاسَ قِسْمَانِ افْتِرَانِيُّ، وَاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ، وَذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَشْتَمِلَ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ الَّذِي هُوَ النَّتِيجَةُ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَكُنْ مُسْتَلْزِماً لَهَا بِذَاتِهِ، فَإِنِ اسْتَمَلَ عَلَيْهَا يَعْنِي: أَوْ عَلَى نَقِيضِهَا بِالقُوَّةِ دُونَ الفِعْلِ؛ بِأَنِ اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى مَادَّتِهَا دُونَ صُورَتِهَا، فَهُوَ الِاقْتِرَانِيُّ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : كُلُّ جِسْم مُؤَلَّفٌ، وَكُلُّ مُؤلَّفٍ حَادِثُ، يَنْتُجُ : كُلُّ جِسْمِ حَادِفٌ، فَهَذَا القِيَاسُ لَمْ تُذْكَرْ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ بِصُورَتِهَا أَي: تَرْكِيهَا الخَاصٌ لَكِنْ بِمَادَّتِهَا، وَهِيَ طَرَفَاهَا: الجِسْمُ مَذْكُورٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى، وَالحَادِثُ، مَذْكُورٌ فِي الكُبْرَى، وَالشَّيْءُ يُوجَدُ مَعَ مَادَّتِهِ بِالقُوَّةِ قَبْلَ حُصُولِ الصُّوْرَةِ.
Then the syllogism according to them is of two types: from it is what is called 'conjunctive' (iqtirani), and it is that which indicates the conclusion by potency and is specific to categorical [propositions]. Know that the syllogism is of two types: conjunctive and exceptive. That is because it must include the demand which is the conclusion, otherwise it would not be self-entailing. If it includes it—meaning, or its contradictory—potentially not actually, by including its matter without its form, then it is the conjunctive; like our saying: 'Every body is composite, and every composite is originated,' producing 'Every body is originated.' This syllogism does not mention the conclusion in its form, i.e., its specific composition, but only its matter, which is its two terms: 'body' is mentioned in the minor premise and 'originated' is mentioned in the major premise. And a thing exists with its matter potentially before the acquisition of form.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogisms into two primary types: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The conjunctive syllogism is defined as one that implies the conclusion potentially, not actually; i.e., it contains the matter (terms) of the conclusion but not its form (the specific propositional structure). The example given is 'Every body is composite, every composite is originated, so every body is originated,' which is a categorical syllogism. In this example, the two terms of the conclusion ('body' and 'originated') appear in the minor and major premises respectively, but the conclusion statement itself is not explicitly written in the syllogism; it is only implied. This is why the syllogism is called 'conjunctive' (iqtirani): the premises are conjoined without the conclusion being stated. The passage emphasizes that without the syllogism's inclusion of the conclusion potentially, it would not entail it necessarily. The exceptive syllogism, by contrast, includes the conclusion or its contradictory actually, but that is not detailed here. This classification is foundational for understanding the structure of syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
اقتراني (iqtirānī): A syllogism that implies the conclusion potentially, containing its matter but not its form; typically categorical. [Note: In some later texts, iqtirānī may be restricted to only certain moods; here it is the general term for non-exceptive syllogism.]
استثنائي (istithnāʾī): A syllogism that contains the conclusion or its contradictory actually, such as conditional syllogisms. [Note: No significant shift.]
مادة (mādda): The terms (subject and predicate) of the conclusion; the content as opposed to the logical form. [Note: In later logic, 'mādda' can also refer to the subject matter of a discipline; here it is strictly the terms.]
صورة (ṣūra): The specific formal arrangement of the conclusion; its actual propositional structure. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the example 'Every body is composite, every composite is originated' considered a conjunctive (iqtirani) syllogism?
A) Because it uses conditional propositions.
B) Because the conclusion 'Every body is originated' is not stated but its terms are present in the premises.
C) Because it has two premises.
D) Because it is productive.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism must include its conclusion either actually (istithna'i) or potentially (iqtirani); this division is fundamental for analyzing arguments in Islamic logic.

Matter and Form in the Iqtirani Syllogism
Author divides syllogism into conjunctive and exceptive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the matter (mādda) and form (ṣūra) in a given categorical iqtirani syllogism and explain why the syllogism contains only the matter.

Core Idea
In an iqtirani syllogism, the conclusion's terms are present as matter, but its propositional form is absent.

The passage unpacks the concepts of 'matter' and 'form' to explain how the iqtirani syllogism implies its conclusion. This lesson focuses on applying these concepts to the example provided.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of iqtirani
Find the phrase 'indicates the conclusion by potency' and the condition of including matter without form. Write down the exact wording.
This definition is the key to understanding the rest.
2
Examine the example provided
The author gives: 'Every body is composite, every composite is originated.' Identify the two premises and the implied conclusion.
The conclusion is 'Every body is originated,' but it is not written in the syllogism.
3
Identify the matter (mādda)
The matter is the two terms of the conclusion: 'body' (subject) and 'originated' (predicate). Verify that these appear in the premises: 'body' in the minor, 'originated' in the major.
The matter is the raw material of the conclusion.
4
Identify the form (ṣūra)
The form is the specific proposition 'Every body is originated' with its particular structure (subject-copula-predicate, universal affirmative). This form is not present in the syllogism; it is only implied.
The form is what makes the conclusion explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ القِيَاسُ عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ: فَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِ: الاقْتِرَانِي (٧٥) وَهُوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَتِيجَةِ بِقُوَّةِ وَاخْتَصَّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ اعْلَمْ أَنَّ القِيَاسَ قِسْمَانِ افْتِرَانِيُّ، وَاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ، وَذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَشْتَمِلَ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ الَّذِي هُوَ النَّتِيجَةُ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَكُنْ مُسْتَلْزِماً لَهَا بِذَاتِهِ، فَإِنِ اسْتَمَلَ عَلَيْهَا يَعْنِي: أَوْ عَلَى نَقِيضِهَا بِالقُوَّةِ دُونَ الفِعْلِ؛ بِأَنِ اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى مَادَّتِهَا دُونَ صُورَتِهَا، فَهُوَ الِاقْتِرَانِيُّ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : كُلُّ جِسْم مُؤَلَّفٌ، وَكُلُّ مُؤلَّفٍ حَادِثُ، يَنْتُجُ : كُلُّ جِسْمِ حَادِفٌ، فَهَذَا القِيَاسُ لَمْ تُذْكَرْ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ بِصُورَتِهَا أَي: تَرْكِيهَا الخَاصٌ لَكِنْ بِمَادَّتِهَا، وَهِيَ طَرَفَاهَا: الجِسْمُ مَذْكُورٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى، وَالحَادِثُ، مَذْكُورٌ فِي الكُبْرَى، وَالشَّيْءُ يُوجَدُ مَعَ مَادَّتِهِ بِالقُوَّةِ قَبْلَ حُصُولِ الصُّوْرَةِ.
Then the syllogism according to them is of two types: from it is what is called 'conjunctive' (iqtirani), and it is that which indicates the conclusion by potency and is specific to categorical [propositions]. Know that the syllogism is of two types: conjunctive and exceptive. That is because it must include the demand which is the conclusion, otherwise it would not be self-entailing. If it includes it—meaning, or its contradictory—potentially not actually, by including its matter without its form, then it is the conjunctive; like our saying: 'Every body is composite, and every composite is originated,' producing 'Every body is originated.' This syllogism does not mention the conclusion in its form, i.e., its specific composition, but only its matter, which is its two terms: 'body' is mentioned in the minor premise and 'originated' is mentioned in the major premise. And a thing exists with its matter potentially before the acquisition of form.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogisms into two primary types: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The conjunctive syllogism is defined as one that implies the conclusion potentially, not actually; i.e., it contains the matter (terms) of the conclusion but not its form (the specific propositional structure). The example given is 'Every body is composite, every composite is originated, so every body is originated,' which is a categorical syllogism. In this example, the two terms of the conclusion ('body' and 'originated') appear in the minor and major premises respectively, but the conclusion statement itself is not explicitly written in the syllogism; it is only implied. This is why the syllogism is called 'conjunctive' (iqtirani): the premises are conjoined without the conclusion being stated. The passage emphasizes that without the syllogism's inclusion of the conclusion potentially, it would not entail it necessarily. The exceptive syllogism, by contrast, includes the conclusion or its contradictory actually, but that is not detailed here. This classification is foundational for understanding the structure of syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
مادة (mādda): The terms (subject and predicate) of the conclusion; the content as opposed to the logical form. [Note: In later logic, 'mādda' can also refer to the subject matter of a discipline; here it is strictly the terms.]
صورة (ṣūra): The specific formal arrangement of the conclusion; its actual propositional structure. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Every body is composite, every composite is originated, therefore every body is originated,' what is the matter (madda)?
A) The two premises.
B) The terms 'body' and 'originated'.
C) The term 'composite'.
D) The conclusion itself.

Key Takeaway
The iqtirani syllogism contains the conclusion's matter (terms) potentially, but not its form; the conclusion is derived when the form is applied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the iqtirani syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden.' Identify the matter (two terms) and the form (the exact conclusion). Explain why this syllogism is iqtirani.
?
Construct your own iqtirani categorical syllogism on a fiqh topic (e.g., zakat, prayer) and identify which terms constitute the matter.

The Two Types of Qiyas: Iqtirani and Istithna'i
Definition and division of qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i) syllogism based on whether the conclusion is contained potentially or explicitly.

Core Idea
A conjunctive syllogism implies its conclusion; an exceptive syllogism states it.

Before diving into the mechanics of syllogisms, logicians distinguish two fundamental types: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The key difference lies in how the conclusion appears—whether it is present only in potential (matter) or explicitly stated (form). This lesson unpacks that distinction using a classical example from kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions of qiyas
Find the sentence where the author divides qiyas into two kinds. Note the names and the defining characteristic given for each.
2
Understand the temporal terminology
The passage explains that the conclusion is called da'wa (claim) before proof, matlub (sought) during, and natija (result) after completion. Recognize how this sets the stage for the discussion.
3
Analyze the example of a conjunctive syllogism
Look at the example: 'Every body is composed; every composed thing is temporally originated; therefore every body is temporally originated.' Notice that the conclusion's terms (body, temporally originated) appear in the premises but not joined together as a complete statement.
4
Distinguish matter from form
The passage says the conjunctive contains the 'matter' of the conclusion but not its 'form'. Matter means the subject and predicate terms; form means the specific propositional structure. In the example, the conclusion's matter is present, but its form is not stated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ تُسَمَّى قَبْلَ الشُّرُوعِ فِي الاِسْتِدْلَالِ : دَعْوَى، وَبَعْدَ الشُّرُوعِ فِيهِ : مَظْلُوباً، وَبَعْدَ تَكْمِيلِهِ : نَتِيجَةٌ. ثُمَّ القِيَاسُ عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ: فَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِالاقْتِرَانِيِّ وَهُوَ الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَتِيجَةِ بِقُوَّةٍ وَاخْتَصَّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ. وَذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَشْتَمِلَ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ الَّذِي هُوَ النَّتِيجَةُ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَكُنْ مُسْتَلْزِماً لَهَا بِذَاتِهِ، فَإِنِ اشْتَمَلَ عَلَيْهَا يَعْنِي: أَوْ عَلَى نَقِيضِهَا بِالقُوَّةِ دُونَ الفِعْلِ؛ بِأَنِ اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى مَادَّتِهَا دُونَ صُورَتِهَا، فَهُوَ الِاقْتِرَانِيُّ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا: كُلُّ جِسْمٍ مُؤَلَّفٌ، وَكُلُّ مُؤَلَّفٍ حَادِثٌ، يَنْتُجُ: كُلُّ جِسْمٍ حَادِثٌ، فَهَذَا القِيَاسُ لَمْ تُذْكَرْ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ بِصُورَتِهَا أَيْ: تَرْكِيبِهَا الخَاصِّ لَكِنْ بِمَادَّتِهَا، وَهِيَ طَرَفَاهَا: الجِسْمُ مَذْكُورٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى، وَالحَادِثُ مَذْكُورٌ فِي الكُبْرَى، وَالشَّيْءُ يُوجَدُ مَعَ مَادَّتِهِ بِالقُوَّةِ قَبْلَ حُصُولِ الصُّوْرَةِ. وَسُمِّيَ: اقْتِرَانِيَّاً لِاقْتِرَانِ الحُدُودِ فِيهِ بَعْضُهَا بِبَعْضٍ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَصْلٍ بِأَدَاةِ اسْتِثْنَاءٍ. وَظَاهِرُ قَوْلِهِ: وَاخْتَصَّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ أَنَّ الِاقْتِرَانِيَّ لَا يَكُونُ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ أَيْضاً. وَاعْتَرَضُوهُ بِمَا ذَكَرَهُ ابْنُ سِيْنَا وَغَيْرُهُ مِنْ أَنَّهُ يُوجَدُ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ كَالحَمْلِيَّاتِ؛ وَمِثَالُهُ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ جِسْماً كَانَ مُؤَلَّفاً، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ مُؤَلَّفاً كَانَ حَادِثاً يَنْتُجُ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ جِسْماً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَهِيَ مَذْكُورَةٌ فِي القِيَاسِ بِمَادَّتِهَا فَقَط؛ لِأَنَّ مُقَدَّمَهَا مَذْكُورٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى، وَتَالِيهَا مَذْكُورٌ فِي الكُبْرَى. وَأُجِيبَ عَنْهُ: بِأَنَّ مَعْنَى قَوْلِهِ: وَاخْتَصَّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ أَنَّ الِاقْتِرَانِيَّ اخْتَصَّ عَنْ قَسِيمِهِ الَّذِي هُوَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ؛ أَيْ: بِتَرْكِيبِهِ مِنَ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةِ الصِّرْفَةِ، بِخِلَافِ الاسْتِثْنَائِي فَلَيْسَ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا وَحْدَهَا أَبَداً. وَعَلَيْهِ فَالبَاءُ فِي بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ: دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، لَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ عَلَيْهِ.
Know that the result is called before embarking on the proof: a claim; after embarking on it: a sought conclusion; and after its completion: a result. Then, qiyas according to them is of two kinds: among it is that which is called the conjunctive, and it is that which indicates the result potentially and is specific to categoricals. That is because it must include the sought conclusion which is the result; otherwise it would not entail it by itself. If it includes it—meaning its contradictory—potentially not actually, such that it includes its matter without its form, then it is the conjunctive, like our saying: 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing is temporally originated, yielding: every body is temporally originated.' So this qiyas does not mention the result in its form, i.e., its specific composition, but rather in its matter, which are its two extremes: 'body' is mentioned in the minor premise, and 'temporally originated' is mentioned in the major premise, and a thing exists with its matter potentially before the acquisition of form. And it is called 'conjunctive' because of the conjunction of the terms in it, some with others, without separation by an exceptive particle. And the apparent meaning of his statement 'and is specific to categoricals' is that the conjunctive does not occur in conditionals, and this is also apparent from Ibn al-Hajib. They objected to it by what Ibn Sina and others mentioned, that it occurs in conditionals just as in categoricals; an example: 'Whenever a thing is a body, it is composed; and whenever it is composed, it is temporally originated, yielding: whenever a thing is a body, it is temporally originated.' And it is mentioned in the qiyas only in its matter, because its antecedent is mentioned in the minor premise and its consequent in the major. And it was answered: that the meaning of his statement 'and is specific to categoricals' is that the conjunctive is specific in comparison to its counterpart, which is the exceptive, to categoricals, i.e., to being composed solely of categorical propositions, unlike the exceptive, which is never composed solely of them. Accordingly, the 'ba'' in 'bi-l-hamliyya' enters upon the restricted thing, not upon that to which it is restricted.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition and division of qiyas (syllogism). The commentator, likely Bannani, begins by clarifying terminologically that the conclusion of a qiyas is called a 'claim' (da'wa) before the proof is begun, a 'sought conclusion' (matlub) after the proof is started but before completion, and a 'result' (natija) after the proof is finished. This sets the stage for discussing the components of qiyas. The author then states that qiyas is of two types according to the logicians: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The focus here is on the conjunctive. It is defined as a qiyas that indicates the conclusion potentially (bi-quwwa), meaning it contains the matter (madda) of the conclusion but not its form (sura). The matter consists of the subject and predicate of the conclusion, which are distributed across the premises. The example given is the categorical syllogism: 'Every body is composed; every composed thing is temporally originated; therefore every body is temporally originated.' The conclusion's subject 'body' appears in the minor premise, and its predicate 'temporally originated' appears in the major premise, but the combined form 'Every body is temporally originated' is not explicitly stated; it is implied by the premises. This is what it means to 'indicate potentially.' The commentator then explains the name 'iqtiŘĀni' as derived from the conjunction (iqtiran) of the three terms (minor, middle, major) without the intervention of an exceptive particle like 'illa' or 'lākin'. He then addresses the statement that iqtirani is specific to categorical propositions (hamliyya). This is interpreted by some, including Ibn al-Hajib, to mean that iqtirani cannot be composed of conditional propositions (shartiyya). An objection is raised, attributed to Ibn Sina and others, pointing out that iqtirani can indeed be formed from conditionals, as in the example: 'Whenever something is a body, it is composed; whenever it is composed, it is temporally originated; therefore whenever something is a body, it is temporally originated.' In this example, the antecedent of the conclusion is found in the minor premise, and the consequent in the major, just as with categoricals. The answer provided refines the meaning: the specification to categoricals means that iqtirani, unlike istithna'i, is composed solely of categorical propositions; the istithna'i can never be composed solely of categoricals because it requires an exceptive particle. Therefore, the iqtirani can be composed of conditionals as well, but its definitional essence is that it does not include the conclusion explicitly, which applies to both categorical and conditional iqtirani. The conclusion of the objection's example is indeed only present potentially, so it is still iqtirani. The 'ba'' in 'bi-l-hamliyya' indicates restriction: iqtirani is restricted to being composed of categoricals alone in contrast to istithna'i, but this does not mean it cannot occur in conditionals as a variant.

Key Terms
القياس
الاقتراني
الاستثنائي
الحملية
المادة

Key Takeaway

The Scope of Iqtirani: Categoricals and Conditionals
Debate on the restriction of iqtirani to categoricals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the correct interpretation of the statement that iqtirani is 'specific to categoricals' and resolve the apparent conflict with conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Iqtirani is 'specific to categoricals' because it is composed solely of categoricals, not because it cannot be conditional.

A seemingly restrictive claim—that iqtirani is specific to categoricals—sparked debate among logicians. Ibn Sina and others pointed out that iqtirani can also be formed from conditional propositions. The resolution lies in a subtle grammatical reinterpretation that preserves the original insight while accommodating conditionals. This lesson walks through that debate and its elegant solution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
Locate the statement: 'اختص بالحملية' (is specific to categoricals). Note that this appears in the definition of iqtirani.
2
Find the objection
Ibn Sina and others objected that iqtirani can be formed from conditionals, giving an example: 'Whenever a thing is a body, it is composed; whenever it is composed, it is temporally originated; therefore whenever a thing is a body, it is temporally originated.' This too has the conclusion potentially.
3
Analyze the response
The answer reinterprets 'specific to categoricals' (اختص بالحملية) as meaning that iqtirani is composed solely of categorical propositions, unlike istithna'i. The 'ba' (ب) in 'bi-l-hamliyya' indicates restriction on the thing itself, not on what it is restricted from.
4
Apply the refined definition
Test the refined definition: iqtirani is a syllogism where the conclusion is not explicitly stated, regardless of whether the premises are categorical or conditional. Both categorical and conditional iqtirani satisfy this.

Source Text

بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ أَنَّ الِاقْتِرَانِيَّ اخْتَصَّ عَنْ قَسِيمِهِ الَّذِي هُوَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ؛ أَيْ: بِتَرْكِيبِهِ مِنَ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةِ الصِّرْفَةِ، بِخِلَافِ الاسْتِثْنَائِي فَلَيْسَ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا وَحْدَهَا أَبَداً. وَعَلَيْهِ فَالبَاءُ فِي بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ: دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، لَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ عَلَيْهِ.
Know that the result is called before embarking on the proof: a claim; after embarking on it: a sought conclusion; and after its completion: a result. Then, qiyas according to them is of two kinds: among it is that which is called the conjunctive, and it is that which indicates the result potentially and is specific to categoricals. That is because it must include the sought conclusion which is the result; otherwise it would not entail it by itself. If it includes it—meaning its contradictory—potentially not actually, such that it includes its matter without its form, then it is the conjunctive, like our saying: 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing is temporally originated, yielding: every body is temporally originated.' So this qiyas does not mention the result in its form, i.e., its specific composition, but rather in its matter, which are its two extremes: 'body' is mentioned in the minor premise, and 'temporally originated' is mentioned in the major premise, and a thing exists with its matter potentially before the acquisition of form. And it is called 'conjunctive' because of the conjunction of the terms in it, some with others, without separation by an exceptive particle. And the apparent meaning of his statement 'and is specific to categoricals' is that the conjunctive does not occur in conditionals, and this is also apparent from Ibn al-Hajib. They objected to it by what Ibn Sina and others mentioned, that it occurs in conditionals just as in categoricals; an example: 'Whenever a thing is a body, it is composed; and whenever it is composed, it is temporally originated, yielding: whenever a thing is a body, it is temporally originated.' And it is mentioned in the qiyas only in its matter, because its antecedent is mentioned in the minor premise and its consequent in the major. And it was answered: that the meaning of his statement 'and is specific to categoricals' is that the conjunctive is specific in comparison to its counterpart, which is the exceptive, to categoricals, i.e., to being composed solely of categorical propositions, unlike the exceptive, which is never composed solely of them. Accordingly, the 'ba'' in 'bi-l-hamliyya' enters upon the restricted thing, not upon that to which it is restricted.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition and division of qiyas (syllogism). The commentator, likely Bannani, begins by clarifying terminologically that the conclusion of a qiyas is called a 'claim' (da'wa) before the proof is begun, a 'sought conclusion' (matlub) after the proof is started but before completion, and a 'result' (natija) after the proof is finished. This sets the stage for discussing the components of qiyas. The author then states that qiyas is of two types according to the logicians: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The focus here is on the conjunctive. It is defined as a qiyas that indicates the conclusion potentially (bi-quwwa), meaning it contains the matter (madda) of the conclusion but not its form (sura). The matter consists of the subject and predicate of the conclusion, which are distributed across the premises. The example given is the categorical syllogism: 'Every body is composed; every composed thing is temporally originated; therefore every body is temporally originated.' The conclusion's subject 'body' appears in the minor premise, and its predicate 'temporally originated' appears in the major premise, but the combined form 'Every body is temporally originated' is not explicitly stated; it is implied by the premises. This is what it means to 'indicate potentially.' The commentator then explains the name 'iqtiŘĀni' as derived from the conjunction (iqtiran) of the three terms (minor, middle, major) without the intervention of an exceptive particle like 'illa' or 'lākin'. He then addresses the statement that iqtirani is specific to categorical propositions (hamliyya). This is interpreted by some, including Ibn al-Hajib, to mean that iqtirani cannot be composed of conditional propositions (shartiyya). An objection is raised, attributed to Ibn Sina and others, pointing out that iqtirani can indeed be formed from conditionals, as in the example: 'Whenever something is a body, it is composed; whenever it is composed, it is temporally originated; therefore whenever something is a body, it is temporally originated.' In this example, the antecedent of the conclusion is found in the minor premise, and the consequent in the major, just as with categoricals. The answer provided refines the meaning: the specification to categoricals means that iqtirani, unlike istithna'i, is composed solely of categorical propositions; the istithna'i can never be composed solely of categoricals because it requires an exceptive particle. Therefore, the iqtirani can be composed of conditionals as well, but its definitional essence is that it does not include the conclusion explicitly, which applies to both categorical and conditional iqtirani. The conclusion of the objection's example is indeed only present potentially, so it is still iqtirani. The 'ba'' in 'bi-l-hamliyya' indicates restriction: iqtirani is restricted to being composed of categoricals alone in contrast to istithna'i, but this does not mean it cannot occur in conditionals as a variant.

الحملية
الشرطية
الاستثنائي

Defining the Conjunctive Syllogism and Its Restriction to Categoricals
Author defines conjunctive syllogism and rebuts Ibn Sina's objection about conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining feature of a conjunctive syllogism and explain why it is restricted to categorical propositions, using the resolution of Ibn Sina's objection.

Core Idea
The conjunctive syllogism is defined by the absence of an exceptive particle, not by the proposition type.

This lesson clarifies the definition of the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) and addresses a common misunderstanding: does the phrase 'restricted to categoricals' mean it cannot use conditional premises? We'll see how the author resolves Ibn Sina's objection by distinguishing form from content.

Guided Path
1
Understand the etymology of 'iqtirani'
The term comes from 'iqtiran' (conjunction) because the terms are joined without an exceptive particle. This is the defining feature.
2
Identify the apparent meaning of 'ikhtassa bi-l-hamliyya'
The matn says the conjunctive is restricted to categoricals. What does this seem to imply? It appears to exclude conditionals entirely.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's counterexample
He gives a syllogism with conditional premises: 'Whenever a thing is a body, it is composed; whenever it is composed, it is originated; therefore whenever a thing is a body, it is originated.' This looks conjunctive in form.
4
Analyze the author's response
The restriction means the conjunctive is composed only of purely categorical propositions (al-qadaya al-hamliyya al-sirfa), unlike the exceptive which always includes an exceptive element. This does not forbid conditional premises in conjunctive form.
5
Apply the distinction
Check whether a given syllogism is conjunctive or exceptive based on the presence of an exceptive particle, not the proposition type.

وَسُمِّيَ: «اقْتِرَانِيَّا»؛ لِاقْتِرَانِ الحُدُودِ فِيهِ بَعْضُهَا بِبَعْضٍ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَصْلٍ بِأَدَاةِ اسْتِثْنَاءِ. وَظَاهِرُ قَوْلِهِ : «وَاخْتَصَّ بِالحَمْلِيَّهُ : أَنَّ الاِقْتِرَانِيَّ لَا يَكُونُ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ أَيْضاً. وَاعْتَرَضُوهُ : بِمَا ذَكَرَهُ ابْنُ سِيْنَا وَغَيْرُهُ مِنْ : أَنَّهُ يُوجَدُ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ كَالحَمْلِيَّاتِ ؛ وَمِثَالُهُ : «كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ جِسْماً كَانَ مُؤَلَّفاً ، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ مُؤلَّفاً كَانَ حَادِثاً» يَنْتُجُ : «كُلَّمَا كَانَ الشَّيْءُ جِسْماً كَانَ حَادِثاً»، وَهِيَ مَذْكُورَةٌ فِي القِيَاسِ بِمَادَّتِهَا فَقَط ؛ لِأَنَّ مُقَدَّمَهَا مَذْكُورٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى، وَتَالِيهَا مَذْكُورٌ فِي الكُبْرَى. وَأُجِيبَ عَنْهُ : بِأَنَّ مَعْنَى قَوْلِهِ : وَاخْتَصَّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ أَنَّ الِاقْتِرَانِيَّ اخْتَصَّ عَنْ قَسِيمِهِ الَّذِي هُوَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ ؛ أَيْ: بِتَرْكِيبِهِ مِنَ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةِ الصِّرْفَةِ، بِخِلَافِ الاسْتِثْنَائِي فَلَيْسَ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا وَحْدَهَا أَبَداً . وَعَلَيْهِ فَـ «البَاءُ فِي بِالحَمْلِيَّةِ : دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، لَا عَلَى المَقْصُورِ عَلَيْهِ، وَإِنَّمَا إِنِ اشْتَمَلَ القِيَاسُ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ أَوْ عَلَى نَقِيضِهَا بِالفِعْلِ بِأَنْ ذُكِرَتْ فِيهِ بِمَادَّتِهَا وَصُورَتِهَا ، فَهُوَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ ، وَسَيَأْتِي كَلَامُهُ .
And it is called 'conjunctive' due to the conjunction of the terms in it, some with others, without separation by a tool of exception. And the apparent meaning of his statement 'and it is restricted to the categorical' is that the conjunctive [syllogism] does not occur in conditionals, and this is also apparent from Ibn al-Hajib. And they objected to it by what Ibn Sina and others mentioned: that it is found in conditionals just as in categoricals; its example: 'Whenever a thing is a body, it is composed, and whenever it is composed, it is originated' yields 'Whenever a thing is a body, it is originated', and this [conclusion] is mentioned in the syllogism by its matter only because its antecedent is mentioned in the minor and its consequent is mentioned in the major. And it was answered: that the meaning of his statement 'and it is restricted to the categorical' is that the conjunctive is distinguished from its counterpart which is the exceptive by the categorical, i.e., by its composition from purely categorical propositions, contrary to the exceptive [syllogism] which is never composed from them alone. Accordingly, the 'bi' in 'bi-l-hamliyya' enters upon the restricted [thing], not upon that to which it is restricted. Rather, if the syllogism includes the conclusion or its opposite actually, such that it is mentioned in it with its matter and form, then it is the exceptive [syllogism], and his discussion will come.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage defines the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) and clarifies its relation to categorical and conditional propositions. The name 'conjunctive' comes from the conjunction of the three terms without an exceptive tool (like 'illa' or 'istithna'). The author points out that the matn's statement 'wa-ikhtassa bi-l-hamliyya' apparently means the conjunctive syllogism does not occur in conditional propositions, a view also held by Ibn al-Hajib. However, Ibn Sina and others objected, giving an example of a syllogism composed of conditional premises: 'Whenever a thing is a body, it is composed; whenever it is composed, it is originated; therefore whenever a thing is a body, it is originated.' In such a syllogism, the conclusion's antecedent is mentioned in the minor premise and the consequent in the major, so the syllogism is conjunctive in form even though the premises are conditional. The response explains that the restriction to categorical refers to the composition of the conjunctive syllogism from purely categorical propositions (al-qadaya al-hamliyya al-sirfa), unlike the exceptive syllogism which always involves an exceptive premise. The 'bi' in 'bi-l-hamliyya' indicates that the categorical is the domain of the conjunctive, not that it is exclusive to categorical content. If a syllogism explicitly includes its conclusion or its opposite, it becomes exceptive. This distinction preserves the technical definition while accounting for Ibn Sina's counterexample.
