The Three Terms of a Syllogism: Asghar, Akbar, and Middle
Author explains the need for a middle term and names the three terms of the syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the asghar (minor), akbar (major), and middle term in any categorical syllogism and explain their roles.

Core Idea
The three terms are the skeleton of every valid syllogism.

Now that we understand the need for a syllogism to prove unknown relations, we must learn its inner structure. A syllogism contains exactly three terms, even though it appears to have four. In this lesson, we will name and define these three terms based on their positions in the conclusion and their function in linking the premises.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the need for a middle term
The relation between the subject and predicate of the conclusion is unknown. To discover it, we introduce a third term—the middle—whose relation to each is known. This middle term appears once with the subject (asghar) in one premise, and once with the predicate (akbar) in the other.
2
Identify the two terms of the conclusion
The subject of the conclusion is called 'asghar' (smaller) because it is usually more specific (fewer individuals). The predicate of the conclusion is called 'akbar' (larger) because it is usually more general (more individuals). In the example 'The world is created', 'world' is asghar and 'created' is akbar.
3
Locate the repeated term as the middle
The term that appears in both premises, but not in the conclusion, is the middle term (hadd wasat). It mediates between asghar and akbar. Even if it does not appear medially in wording, it is still the middle in judgment.
4
Understand why three terms are needed
Although each premise has two terms, giving four in wording, one term (the middle) is repeated, so there are only three distinct terms in meaning. This repetition creates the logical connection between premises.
5
Practice with a real syllogism
Take the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore, khamr is haram.' Identify asghar (khamr), akbar (haram), and middle (intoxicant).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَهُ فَرَكْبَا مُقَدِّمَـاتِـهِ عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَانْظُرَا صَحِيحَهَا مِنْ فَاسِدٍ مُخْتَبِرًا فَإِنَّ لَازِمَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ بِـحَـــــبِ الـــمُـــــدمـــاتِ آتِ لَمَّا كَانَتِ النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيِ المَظْلُوبِ مَجْهُولَةٌ، احْتِيجَ فِي القِيَاسِ إِلَى أَمْرٍ ثَالِثٍ يَكُونُ مَعْلُومَ النِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كِلَيْهِمَا ، فَنَشَاتْ مِنْ رَبْطِهِ بِمَوْضُوعِ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ مُقَدَّمِهِ قَضِيَّةٌ، وَمِنْ رَبْطِهِ بِمَحْمُولِهِ أَوْ تَالِيهِ أُخْرَى، فَكَانَ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ مُشْتَمِلَتَيْنِ عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَطْرَافٍ فِي اللَّفْظِ، وَهِيَ ثَلَاثَةٌ فِي المَعْنَى. وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّتَانِ : مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الاِسْتِدْلَالُ بِهِمَا. وَأَحَدُ طَرَفَيْ إِحْدَاهُمَا هُوَ مَوْضُوعُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ مُقَدَّمُهُ وَيُسَمَّى : «الأَصْغَرَ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ غَالِباً أَخَصُّ مِنَ الطَّرَفِ الآخَرِ مِنَ المَطْلُوبِ، وَالأَخَصُّ أَقَلُّ أَفْرَاداً . وَأَحَدُ طَرَفِي المُقَدِّمَةِ الأُخْرَى هُوَ مَحْمُولُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ تَالِيهِ، وَيُسَمَّى : «الأَكْبَرَ»؛ لأَنَّهُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ غَالِباً ، فَهُوَ أَكْثَرُ أَفْرَاداً . وَالطَّرَفُ الآخَرُ لِكُلٍّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ هُوَ الأَمْرُ الثَّالِثُ المَعْلُومُ النِّسْبَةِ إِلَى طَرَفَيِ المَظْلُوبِ، وَيُسَمَّى: «الحَدَّ الوَسَطِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ وَاسِطَةٌ فِي الحُكْمِ بِالأَكْبَرِ عَلَى الأَصْغَرِ فِي المَطْلُوبِ؛ سَوَاءٌ تَوَسَّطَ فِي اللَّفْظِ كَمَا فِي الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ، أَمْ لَا، وَيُسَمَّى أَيْضاً بِـ: «المُكَرَّرِ»؛ لِإِعَادَتِهِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ، وَبِتَكَرُّرِهِ فِيهَا حَصَلَ الِارْتِبَاطُ بَيْنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ.
If you desire its composition, then compose its premises as is necessary, and arrange the premises and examine, testing, their sound from their unsound, for the necessary consequent of the premises comes by their connection. Since the relation between the two terms of the sought [conclusion] is unknown, there is need in the syllogism for a third thing whose relation to both of them is known. Then from its connection to the subject of the sought or its antecedent arises a proposition, and from its connection to its predicate or its consequent arises another [proposition]. Thus there are two propositions containing four terms in wording, while they are three in meaning. And the two propositions are called 'premises' from the perspective of using them for inference. And one of the two terms of one of them is the subject of the sought or its antecedent, and it is called 'the smaller' because it is usually more specific than the other term of the sought, and the more specific has fewer instances. And one of the two terms of the other premise is the predicate of the sought or its consequent, and it is called 'the larger' because it is usually more general than the first, so it has more instances. And the other term of each of the two premises is the third thing whose relation to the two terms of the sought is known, and it is called 'the middle term' because it is an intermediary in the judgment of the larger about the smaller in what is sought, whether it is medial in wording as in the first figure or not. And it is also called 'the repeated' because it is repeated in the second [premise], and through its repetition in it the connection between the two premises occurs.
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage explains the structure of the syllogism using the poet's lines from al-Sullam. The author begins by noting that when we want to construct a syllogism, we must arrange its premises appropriately. The purpose is to discover a relation between the two terms of the desired conclusion that is initially unknown. To do this, we introduce a third term whose relation to both is known. This third term is connected to the subject (or antecedent) of the conclusion in one premise, and to the predicate (or consequent) in another premise, yielding two premises. Although these premises have four terms in wording, they actually have only three terms in meaning because the third term appears twice (once in each premise). The two premises are called 'muqaddimatayn' (two premises) because they are used for inference. The author then names the terms: the term that is the subject of the conclusion is called 'asghar' (smaller) because it is typically more specific (has fewer individuals) than the predicate term; the term that is the predicate of the conclusion is called 'akbar' (larger) because it is usually more general (has more individuals); and the repeated term (the third) is called 'hadd wasat' (middle term) because it mediates between the smaller and larger in the judgment, whether or not it appears medially in the wording (e.g., in the first figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major; in other figures, it may be positioned differently). The middle term is also called 'al-mukarrar' (the repeated) because its repetition in both premises creates the connection between them. This foundational explanation of the three terms is essential for understanding all syllogistic reasoning, as every valid categorical syllogism relies on this structure.

Key Terms
أصغر
أكبر
حد وسط
مقدمة

Key Takeaway
Every valid categorical syllogism has exactly three terms: asghar (minor), akbar (major), and hadd wasat (middle), which links the premises.

The Syllogism
beginner
8 min
The Three Terms of a Syllogism: Minor, Major, and Middle
Defining the three terms of a syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism and explain the role of the middle term.

Core Idea
Every syllogism has exactly three terms; the middle term is the essential link that makes inference possible.

The commentator unpacks why a syllogism cannot be constructed with just two terms. Since the relationship between subject and predicate of the desired conclusion is unknown, a third term is introduced—the middle—which is known relative to both. This middle term appears in both premises, connecting them and revealing the sought conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sought conclusion
Find the proposition you aim to prove (the matlub). This will have two extremes: a subject and a predicate.
Example: In 'Nabidh is haram', 'nabidh' is the subject, 'haram' is the predicate.
2
Determine the minor and major terms
The subject of the conclusion is the minor term (asghar), usually more specific; the predicate is the major term (akbar), usually more general.
3
Locate the middle term
Look for a term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term (hadd wasat).
It is repeated (al-mukarrar) and creates the connection between premises.
4
Verify the tripartite structure
Check that the two premises have four terms in expression but only three distinct terms in meaning: minor, middle, major.

باب في القياس . قَوْلُهُ : (٧٦) فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَهُ فَرَكْبَا مُقَدِّمَـاتِـهِ عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا (۷۷) وَرَتِّبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ وَانْظُرَا صَحِيحَهَا مِنْ فَاسِدٍ مُخْتَبِرًا (۷۸) فَإِنَّ لَازِمَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ بِحَسْبِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ آتِ لَمَّا كَانَتِ النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيِ المَظْلُوبِ مَجْهُولَةٌ، احْتِيجَ فِي القِيَاسِ إِلَى أَمْرٍ ثَالِثٍ يَكُونُ مَعْلُومَ النِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كِلَيْهِمَا ، فَنَشَاتْ مِنْ رَبْطِهِ بِمَوْضُوعِ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ مُقَدَّمِهِ قَضِيَّةٌ، وَمِنْ رَبْطِهِ بِمَحْمُولِهِ أَوْ تَالِيهِ أُخْرَى، فَكَانَ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ مُشْتَمِلَتَيْنِ عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَطْرَافٍ فِي اللَّفْظِ ، وَهِيَ ثَلَاثَةٌ فِي المَعْنَى. وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّتَانِ : مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الاِسْتِدْلَالُ بِهِمَا. وَأَحَدُ طَرَفَيْ إِحْدَاهِمَا هُوَ مَوْضُوعُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ مُقَدَّمُهُ وَيُسَمَّى : «الأَصْغَرَ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ غَالِباً أَخَصُّ مِنَ الطَّرَفِ الآخَرِ لِلْمَطْلُوبِ، وَالأَخَصُّ أَقَلُّ أَفْرَاداً . وَأَحَدُ طَرَفِي المُقَدِّمَةِ الأُخْرَى هُوَ مَحْمُولُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ تَالِيهِ، وَيُسَمَّى : «الأَكْبَرَ» ؛ لأَنَّهُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ غَالِباً ، فَهُوَ أَكْثَرُ أَفْرَاداً . وَالطَّرَفُ الآخَرُ لِكُلٍّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ هُوَ الأَمْرُ الثَّالِثُ المَعْلُومُ النِّسْبَةِ إِلَى طَرَفَيِ المَظْلُوبِ، وَيُسَمَّى: «الحَدَّ الوَسَطِ» ; لِأَنَّهُ وَاسِطَةٌ فِي الحُكْمِ بِالأَكْبَرِ عَلَى الأَصْغَرِ فِي المَطْلُوبِ؛ سَوَاءٌ تَوَسَّطَ فِي اللَّفْظِ كَمَا فِي الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ، أَمْ لَا، وَيُسَمَّى أَيْضاً بِـ: «المُكَرَّرِ » ; لِإِعَادَتِهِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ، وَبِتَكَرُّرِهِ فِيهَا حَصَلَ الِارْتِبَاطُ بَيْنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ . قوله : (فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَهُ ... إلخ) : قوله : (مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ) جمع : «مقدمة» - بالكسر ـ عند الجم الغفير، وقد تفتح ; بناءً على تعدي فعلها ولزومه، فمن كسر جعلها مأخوذة من: «قدم»; بمعنى: تقدَّم»، ومنه قوله تعالى: وَلَا نُقَدِّمُوا ﴾ [الحجرات (١) ، ومَن فتح أخذها من: «قدم» المتعدِّي . وهي عند المناطقة: «القضيَّة المجعولة جزء الدَّليل أي: القياس، سميت بذلك; لتقدمها أمام المطلوب; أخذاً لها من مقدمة الجيش للجماعة المتقدمة منه . قوله : (مَجْهُولَةٌ) أي : إذ لو كانت معلومةٌ لم تحتج إلى استدلال وقياس. وَتُسَمَّى الأَطْرَافُ الثَّلَاثَةُ بـ : «الحُدُودِ» ; لِأَنَّ حَدَّ الشَّيْءِ وَطَرَفَهُ مُنْتَهَاهُ، وَهُوَ مُنْتَهَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، فَهَذِهِ كَيْفِيَّةُ تَرْكِيبِهِ المُشَارُ إِلَيْهَا بِقَوْلِهِ : فَرَكَّبَا) . . مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا». . وَالأَلِفُ فِي رَكْبَا»: بَدَلٌ مِنْ نُونِ التَّوْكِيدِ الخَفِيفَةِ، وَكَذَا فِي قَوْلِهِ : «وَانْظُرَا»، وَأَرَادَ «المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَقَلَّ الجَمْع وَهُوَ اثْنَانِ، وَهَذَا القَدْرُ فِي القِيَاسِ البَسِيطِ كَافٍ، وَسَيَأْتِي الكَلَامُ عَلَى المُرَكَّبِ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَرْتِيبِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ، وَاخْتِبَارِ الصَّحِيحِ مِنْهُمَا مِنَ الفَاسِدِ ؛ إِذْ لَا تَصِحُ النَّتِيجَةُ لُزُوماً إِلَّا بَعْدَ صِحَّةِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ لَهُمَا ، وَلَازِمُ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِحَسْبِهِمَا ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقُهُ إِلَّا بِصِدْقِهِمَا قَالَ ابْنُ سِيْنَا : الحَقُّ أَنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ القِيَاسُ صَحِيحُ التَّألِيفِ صَادِقُ المُقَدِّمَاتِ، وَجَبَ أَنْ تَكُونَ النَّتِيجَةُ صَادِقَةٌ . اهـ . فَأَمَّا إِنْ كَذَبَتَا أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا فَقَدْ تَكْذُبُ النَّتِيجَةُ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَجَرٌ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جَمَادٌ، وَقَدْ تَصْدُقُ ، نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ صَهَالٌ، وَكُلُّ صَهَالٍ حَيَوَان . قوله : (وَتُسَمَّى الأَطْرَافُ الثَّلَاثَةُ بـ: «الحُدُودِ») وبذلك سمَّاها صاحب «الخريدة»، فقال: حُدُودُهُ ثَلَاثَةٌ فَمَا حُكِمَ عَلَيْهِ فِي المَظْلُوبِ أَصْغَرٌ عُلِمْ وَمَا بِهِ حُكِمَ أَكْبَرُ وَمَا جَاءَ لِجَمْعِ وَسَطاً قَدْ عُلِمَا قوله : (فَلَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقُهُ إِلَّا بِصِدْقِهِمَا ) أي : لأنَّ صدق الملزوم يُوجب صدق اللازم، ومما يناسب هنا ما قيل في التهنئة بولادة ابن دقيق العيد : هيئت بِالبَرِّ التَّقِي وَمَنْ يَكُنْ بَرًّا تَقِيَّا مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ يُنْتِجُ إِنَّ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ مَهُمَا كَانَتَا صِدْقاً فَمِثْلُهُمَا النَّتِيجَةُ تَخْرُجُ قوله : (وَقَدْ تَصْدُقُ) أي : لأنَّ النتيجة لازم للمقدِّمتين، وكذبُ الملزوم لا يوجب كذب اللازم، بخلاف صدق الملزوم فإنه يوجب صدق اللازم كما تقدم.
Chapter on the syllogism. His statement: (76) If you intend its composition, then compose its premises as required. (77) And arrange the premises and examine, testing, the sound from the corrupt. (78) For the necessary consequence of the premises follows according to the premises. Since the relation between the two extremes of the sought [conclusion] is unknown, there is need in the syllogism for a third thing whose relation to both of them is known. So there arose from linking it to the subject of the sought or its antecedent a proposition, and from linking it to its predicate or its consequent another proposition. Thus there are two propositions containing four terms in expression, though they are three in meaning. The two propositions are called 'premises' from the perspective of using them for inference. One of the two extremes of one of them is the subject of the sought or its antecedent, and it is called the 'minor' because it is generally more specific than the other extreme of the sought, and the more specific has fewer individuals. And one of the two extremes of the other premise is the predicate of the sought or its consequent, and it is called the 'major' because it is generally more general than the first, so it has more individuals. And the other extreme of each of the two premises is the third thing whose relation to the two extremes of the sought is known, and it is called the 'middle term' because it is an intermediary in the judgment of the major upon the minor in the sought, whether it is intermediate in expression as in the first figure or not, and it is also called the 'repeated' because it is repeated in the second [premise], and by its repetition the connection between the two premises is obtained. His statement: (if you intend its composition... etc.): His statement: (its premises) is the plural of 'muqaddima' with kasra according to the great multitude, and it can be with fatha based on the transitivity and intransitivity of its verb; whoever uses kasra takes it from 'qadima' meaning 'to precede', and from that is His statement: 'and do not precede' [Hujurat 49:1]; and whoever uses fatha takes it from 'qadama' transitive. And according to the logicians it is 'the proposition made part of the proof i.e. the syllogism', so named because it precedes the sought, taken from the vanguard of the army for the advanced group. His statement: (unknown) i.e. if it were known, there would be no need for inference and syllogism. And the three extremes are called 'terms' because the end or limit of something is its extremity, and it is the extremity of the two premises. Such is the manner of its composition referred to by his statement: 'compose its premises as required.' The alif in 'rukba' is a substitute for the light nun of emphasis, and similarly in his statement: 'and examine.' And he meant by 'premises' the minimum plural which is two, and this amount is sufficient for the simple syllogism, and the discussion on the compound will come. And the ordering of the two premises is necessary, and examining the sound from the corrupt among them, since the conclusion is not valid necessarily except after the soundness of the two premises, because it is necessary for them, and the necessary of the two premises is according to them, so its truth is not entailed except by their truth. Ibn Sina said: The truth is that if the syllogism is valid in composition and its premises are true, then the conclusion must be true. End. As for if both or one of them is false, then the conclusion may be false, like: every human is a stone, and every stone is inanimate; and it may be true, like: every human is a neighing being, and every neighing being is an animal. His statement: (And the three extremes are called 'terms') and by that the author of al-Kharida named them; he said: its terms are three: what is judged upon in the sought is the minor, known; what is judged by it is the major; and what comes to combine is the middle, known. His statement: (So its truth is not entailed except by their truth) i.e. because the truth of the entailed necessitates the truth of the necessary. And appropriate here is what was said in congratulation of the birth of Ibn Daqiq al-'Id: You are congratulated with the righteous piety; whoever is righteous and pious, such produces. Indeed, the two premises whenever they are true, so like them the conclusion emerges. His statement: (and it may be true) i.e. because the conclusion is the necessary of the premises, and the falsehood of the entailed does not necessitate the falsehood of the necessary, unlike the truth of the entailed which does necessitate the truth of the necessary as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on lines 76–78 of the Sullam al-Munawraq, explaining both the structure of a syllogism and the condition for a valid conclusion. The commentator begins by restating the poem, then unpacks the logic behind the syllogism's composition. Since the relation between subject and predicate of the desired conclusion is unknown, we need a middle term. Linking this middle term to the subject yields one premise, and linking it to the predicate yields a second. Though the two premises have four terms in expression (subject, middle, middle again, predicate), there are only three distinct terms in meaning. The subject of the sought is the 'minor' (usually more specific); its predicate is the 'major' (usually more general); the repeated term is the 'middle'. The two premises are the building blocks of inference. The commentator then explains the poet's command 'compose its premises' and 'examine', noting that the minimum is two premises for a simple syllogism. Crucially, the conclusion follows necessarily only if both premises are sound; the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises. Ibn Sina is quoted to affirm that a valid syllogism with true premises yields a true conclusion. However, false premises may yield false or even true conclusions—as illustrated by the examples: 'every human is a stone, every stone is inanimate' gives a false conclusion; 'every human is a neighing being, every neighing being is an animal' gives a true one. The commentator then cites al-Kharida's poetic definition of the three terms, and reinforces the point that the truth of the entailed (premises) necessitates the truth of the necessary (conclusion), while false premises do not necessitate a false conclusion—a point illustrated with a congratulatory poem about Ibn Daqiq al-'Id. For an Islamic example: if we take 'every khamr is haram' and 'every nabidh is khamr' (true premises), the conclusion 'every nabidh is haram' is true; but if we change to 'every khamr is halal' (false) and 'every nabidh is khamr' (true), the conclusion 'every nabidh is halal' is false—showing the dependence on premise truth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a categorical syllogism, the term that is repeated in both premises but not in the conclusion is called:
A) The minor term (الأصغر)
B) The major term (الأكبر)
C) The middle term (الحد الوسط)
D) The predicate (المحمول)

Key Takeaway
A syllogism has exactly three distinct terms: the minor (subject of conclusion), the major (predicate of conclusion), and the middle (repeated term that connects the premises).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following argument: 'Every khamr is haram; every nabidh is khamr; therefore, every nabidh is haram.' Identify the minor, major, and middle terms.
?
Given the conclusion 'The world is temporally originated', construct a simple categorical syllogism with a middle term (e.g., 'Everything changeable is temporally originated; the world is changeable'), then identify the three terms.

The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
The Truth Condition: True Premises Yield a True Conclusion
Explaining the relationship between premise truth and conclusion necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the truth condition to determine whether a syllogism's conclusion is guaranteed to be true, and recognize that false premises do not guarantee a false conclusion.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism with true premises necessarily produces a true conclusion; false premises can still accidentally yield a true conclusion.

The commentator emphasizes that the conclusion is a necessary consequence of the premises. For the conclusion to be mandatory, both premises must be true. Ibn Sina is quoted to affirm that a valid syllogism with true premises guarantees a true conclusion. However, if any premise is false, the conclusion may be false or true—as shown by illustrative examples from everyday language and a congratulatory poem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
List the two premises and the conclusion of the syllogism.
2
Check the syllogism's validity
Ensure the syllogism is logically valid (correct mood and figure). This lesson does not cover validity assessment, but it is a prerequisite.
Assume validity for the purpose of truth condition.
3
Assess the truth of each premise
Determine whether each premise is factually true or false. The truth of the conclusion depends on this.
4
Apply the rule
If both premises are true and the syllogism is valid, the conclusion must be true. If any premise is false, the conclusion may be false or true; no guarantee.
The passage gives examples: false premises can lead to false or even true conclusions.

أَطْرَافٍ فِي اللَّفْظِ ، وَهِيَ ثَلَاثَةٌ فِي المَعْنَى. وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّتَانِ : مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الاِسْتِدْلَالُ بِهِمَا. وَأَحَدُ طَرَفَيْ إِحْدَاهِمَا هُوَ مَوْضُوعُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ مُقَدَّمُهُ وَيُسَمَّى : «الأَصْغَرَ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ غَالِباً أَخَصُّ مِنَ الطَّرَفِ الآخَرِ لِلْمَطْلُوبِ، وَالأَخَصُّ أَقَلُّ أَفْرَاداً . وَأَحَدُ طَرَفِي المُقَدِّمَةِ الأُخْرَى هُوَ مَحْمُولُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ تَالِيهِ، وَيُسَمَّى : «الأَكْبَرَ» ؛ لأَنَّهُ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ غَالِباً ، فَهُوَ أَكْثَرُ أَفْرَاداً . وَالطَّرَفُ الآخَرُ لِكُلٍّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ هُوَ الأَمْرُ الثَّالِثُ المَعْلُومُ النِّسْبَةِ إِلَى طَرَفَيِ المَظْلُوبِ، وَيُسَمَّى: «الحَدَّ الوَسَطِ» ; لِأَنَّهُ وَاسِطَةٌ فِي الحُكْمِ بِالأَكْبَرِ عَلَى الأَصْغَرِ فِي المَطْلُوبِ؛ سَوَاءٌ تَوَسَّطَ فِي اللَّفْظِ كَمَا فِي الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ، أَمْ لَا، وَيُسَمَّى أَيْضاً بِـ: «المُكَرَّرِ » ; لِإِعَادَتِهِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ، وَبِتَكَرُّرِهِ فِيهَا حَصَلَ الِارْتِبَاطُ بَيْنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ . قوله : (فَإِنْ تُرِدْ تَرْكِيبَهُ ... إلخ) : قوله : (مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ) جمع : «مقدمة» - بالكسر ـ عند الجم الغفير، وقد تفتح ; بناءً على تعدي فعلها ولزومه، فمن كسر جعلها مأخوذة من: «قدم»; بمعنى: تقدَّم»، ومنه قوله تعالى: وَلَا نُقَدِّمُوا ﴾ [الحجرات (١) ، ومَن فتح أخذها من: «قدم» المتعدِّي . وهي عند المناطقة: «القضيَّة المجعولة جزء الدَّليل أي: القياس، سميت بذلك; لتقدمها أمام المطلوب; أخذاً لها من مقدمة الجيش للجماعة المتقدمة منه . قوله : (مَجْهُولَةٌ) أي : إذ لو كانت معلومةٌ لم تحتج إلى استدلال وقياس. وَتُسَمَّى الأَطْرَافُ الثَّلَاثَةُ بـ : «الحُدُودِ» ; لِأَنَّ حَدَّ الشَّيْءِ وَطَرَفَهُ مُنْتَهَاهُ، وَهُوَ مُنْتَهَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، فَهَذِهِ كَيْفِيَّةُ تَرْكِيبِهِ المُشَارُ إِلَيْهَا بِقَوْلِهِ : فَرَكَّبَا) . . مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا». . وَالأَلِفُ فِي رَكْبَا»: بَدَلٌ مِنْ نُونِ التَّوْكِيدِ الخَفِيفَةِ، وَكَذَا فِي قَوْلِهِ : «وَانْظُرَا»، وَأَرَادَ «المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَقَلَّ الجَمْع وَهُوَ اثْنَانِ، وَهَذَا القَدْرُ فِي القِيَاسِ البَسِيطِ كَافٍ، وَسَيَأْتِي الكَلَامُ عَلَى المُرَكَّبِ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَرْتِيبِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ، وَاخْتِبَارِ الصَّحِيحِ مِنْهُمَا مِنَ الفَاسِدِ ؛ إِذْ لَا تَصِحُ النَّتِيجَةُ لُزُوماً إِلَّا بَعْدَ صِحَّةِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ لَهُمَا ، وَلَازِمُ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِحَسْبِهِمَا ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقُهُ إِلَّا بِصِدْقِهِمَا قَالَ ابْنُ سِيْنَا : الحَقُّ أَنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ القِيَاسُ صَحِيحُ التَّألِيفِ صَادِقُ المُقَدِّمَاتِ، وَجَبَ أَنْ تَكُونَ النَّتِيجَةُ صَادِقَةٌ . اهـ . فَأَمَّا إِنْ كَذَبَتَا أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا فَقَدْ تَكْذُبُ النَّتِيجَةُ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَجَرٌ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جَمَادٌ، وَقَدْ تَصْدُقُ ، نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ صَهَالٌ، وَكُلُّ صَهَالٍ حَيَوَان . قوله : (وَتُسَمَّى الأَطْرَافُ الثَّلَاثَةُ بـ: «الحُدُودِ») وبذلك سمَّاها صاحب «الخريدة»، فقال: حُدُودُهُ ثَلَاثَةٌ فَمَا حُكِمَ عَلَيْهِ فِي المَظْلُوبِ أَصْغَرٌ عُلِمْ وَمَا بِهِ حُكِمَ أَكْبَرُ وَمَا جَاءَ لِجَمْعِ وَسَطاً قَدْ عُلِمَا قوله : (فَلَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقُهُ إِلَّا بِصِدْقِهِمَا ) أي : لأنَّ صدق الملزوم يُوجب صدق اللازم، ومما يناسب هنا ما قيل في التهنئة بولادة ابن دقيق العيد : هيئت بِالبَرِّ التَّقِي وَمَنْ يَكُنْ بَرًّا تَقِيَّا مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ يُنْتِجُ إِنَّ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ مَهُمَا كَانَتَا صِدْقاً فَمِثْلُهُمَا النَّتِيجَةُ تَخْرُجُ قوله : (وَقَدْ تَصْدُقُ) أي : لأنَّ النتيجة لازم للمقدِّمتين، وكذبُ الملزوم لا يوجب كذب اللازم، بخلاف صدق الملزوم فإنه يوجب صدق اللازم كما تقدم.
Chapter on the syllogism. His statement: (76) If you intend its composition, then compose its premises as required. (77) And arrange the premises and examine, testing, the sound from the corrupt. (78) For the necessary consequence of the premises follows according to the premises. Since the relation between the two extremes of the sought [conclusion] is unknown, there is need in the syllogism for a third thing whose relation to both of them is known. So there arose from linking it to the subject of the sought or its antecedent a proposition, and from linking it to its predicate or its consequent another proposition. Thus there are two propositions containing four terms in expression, though they are three in meaning. The two propositions are called 'premises' from the perspective of using them for inference. One of the two extremes of one of them is the subject of the sought or its antecedent, and it is called the 'minor' because it is generally more specific than the other extreme of the sought, and the more specific has fewer individuals. And one of the two extremes of the other premise is the predicate of the sought or its consequent, and it is called the 'major' because it is generally more general than the first, so it has more individuals. And the other extreme of each of the two premises is the third thing whose relation to the two extremes of the sought is known, and it is called the 'middle term' because it is an intermediary in the judgment of the major upon the minor in the sought, whether it is intermediate in expression as in the first figure or not, and it is also called the 'repeated' because it is repeated in the second [premise], and by its repetition the connection between the two premises is obtained. His statement: (if you intend its composition... etc.): His statement: (its premises) is the plural of 'muqaddima' with kasra according to the great multitude, and it can be with fatha based on the transitivity and intransitivity of its verb; whoever uses kasra takes it from 'qadima' meaning 'to precede', and from that is His statement: 'and do not precede' [Hujurat 49:1]; and whoever uses fatha takes it from 'qadama' transitive. And according to the logicians it is 'the proposition made part of the proof i.e. the syllogism', so named because it precedes the sought, taken from the vanguard of the army for the advanced group. His statement: (unknown) i.e. if it were known, there would be no need for inference and syllogism. And the three extremes are called 'terms' because the end or limit of something is its extremity, and it is the extremity of the two premises. Such is the manner of its composition referred to by his statement: 'compose its premises as required.' The alif in 'rukba' is a substitute for the light nun of emphasis, and similarly in his statement: 'and examine.' And he meant by 'premises' the minimum plural which is two, and this amount is sufficient for the simple syllogism, and the discussion on the compound will come. And the ordering of the two premises is necessary, and examining the sound from the corrupt among them, since the conclusion is not valid necessarily except after the soundness of the two premises, because it is necessary for them, and the necessary of the two premises is according to them, so its truth is not entailed except by their truth. Ibn Sina said: The truth is that if the syllogism is valid in composition and its premises are true, then the conclusion must be true. End. As for if both or one of them is false, then the conclusion may be false, like: every human is a stone, and every stone is inanimate; and it may be true, like: every human is a neighing being, and every neighing being is an animal. His statement: (And the three extremes are called 'terms') and by that the author of al-Kharida named them; he said: its terms are three: what is judged upon in the sought is the minor, known; what is judged by it is the major; and what comes to combine is the middle, known. His statement: (So its truth is not entailed except by their truth) i.e. because the truth of the entailed necessitates the truth of the necessary. And appropriate here is what was said in congratulation of the birth of Ibn Daqiq al-'Id: You are congratulated with the righteous piety; whoever is righteous and pious, such produces. Indeed, the two premises whenever they are true, so like them the conclusion emerges. His statement: (and it may be true) i.e. because the conclusion is the necessary of the premises, and the falsehood of the entailed does not necessitate the falsehood of the necessary, unlike the truth of the entailed which does necessitate the truth of the necessary as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on lines 76–78 of the Sullam al-Munawraq, explaining both the structure of a syllogism and the condition for a valid conclusion. The commentator begins by restating the poem, then unpacks the logic behind the syllogism's composition. Since the relation between subject and predicate of the desired conclusion is unknown, we need a middle term. Linking this middle term to the subject yields one premise, and linking it to the predicate yields a second. Though the two premises have four terms in expression (subject, middle, middle again, predicate), there are only three distinct terms in meaning. The subject of the sought is the 'minor' (usually more specific); its predicate is the 'major' (usually more general); the repeated term is the 'middle'. The two premises are the building blocks of inference. The commentator then explains the poet's command 'compose its premises' and 'examine', noting that the minimum is two premises for a simple syllogism. Crucially, the conclusion follows necessarily only if both premises are sound; the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises. Ibn Sina is quoted to affirm that a valid syllogism with true premises yields a true conclusion. However, false premises may yield false or even true conclusions—as illustrated by the examples: 'every human is a stone, every stone is inanimate' gives a false conclusion; 'every human is a neighing being, every neighing being is an animal' gives a true one. The commentator then cites al-Kharida's poetic definition of the three terms, and reinforces the point that the truth of the entailed (premises) necessitates the truth of the necessary (conclusion), while false premises do not necessitate a false conclusion—a point illustrated with a congratulatory poem about Ibn Daqiq al-'Id. For an Islamic example: if we take 'every khamr is haram' and 'every nabidh is khamr' (true premises), the conclusion 'every nabidh is haram' is true; but if we change to 'every khamr is halal' (false) and 'every nabidh is khamr' (true), the conclusion 'every nabidh is halal' is false—showing the dependence on premise truth.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator and Ibn Sina, which of the following is true?
A) If both premises are false, the conclusion must be false.
B) If the syllogism is valid and both premises are true, the conclusion must be true.
C) If one premise is false, the conclusion may be false but never true.
D) The truth of the conclusion is independent of the truth of the premises.

Key Takeaway
In a valid syllogism, truth of premises necessitates truth of conclusion; false premises do not necessitate falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' If the first premise is true (given the Quran and hadith) but the second is false (some nabidh may not be intoxicating), can the conclusion be true? Explain using the truth condition from this lesson.
?
Construct a syllogism with false premises that nonetheless yields a true conclusion (similar to the 'neighing being' example). Identify that the truth of the conclusion is accidental, not necessary.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Three Terms and the Truth Condition of Syllogisms
Commentator defines the three terms and explains the truth condition of syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three terms (minor, major, middle) in a categorical syllogism and determine whether the conclusion is guaranteed true based on the truth of the premises.

Core Idea
True premises and valid form guarantee truth; false premises can accidentally yield a true conclusion.

In any valid categorical syllogism, there are exactly three terms that function as the endpoints of the premises. The commentator explains why they are called 'limits' and emphasizes that the conclusion is a necessary consequent of the premises. Understanding both the structure and the truth condition is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three terms
Identify the conclusion of the syllogism. The subject of the conclusion is the minor term, the predicate is the major term. Find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion; that is the middle term.
The three terms are also called the minor (al-aṣghar), major (al-akbar), and middle (al-awsaṭ).
2
Verify the number and order of premises
Ensure the syllogism has exactly two premises (the smallest plural). The premises must be properly ordered and composed according to the rules of syllogistic form.
The commentator notes that two premises suffice for a simple syllogism; compound syllogisms will be discussed later.
3
Assess the truth of each premise
Using Islamic sources (Qur'an, Sunnah, consensus, or rational necessity), determine whether each premise is actually true. This step requires external knowledge; the logical tool does not provide truth but checks necessity.
Ibn Sina's principle: 'If the syllogism is sound in composition and true in premises, the conclusion must be true.'
4
Apply the truth condition
If both premises are true and the form is valid, the conclusion is necessarily true. If at least one premise is false, the conclusion may be either true or false; it is not necessitated.
Example: 'Every human is a stone; every stone is inert' yields a false conclusion. But 'Every human is an equine; every equine is an animal' yields a true conclusion despite false premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَتُسَمَّى الأَطْرَافُ الثَّلَاثَةُ بـ : الحُدُودِ» ؛ لِأَنَّ حَدَّ الشَّيْءِ وَطَرَفَهُ مُنْتَهَاهُ، وَهُوَ مُنْتَهَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، فَهَذِهِ كَيْفِيَّةُ تَرْكِيبِهِ المُشَارِ إِلَيْهَا بِقَوْلِهِ : فَرَكَّبَا) . . مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ عَلَى مَا وَجَبَا». . وَالأَلِفُ فِي رَكْبَا»: بَدَلٌ مِنْ نُونِ التَّوْكِيدِ الخَفِيفَةِ، وَكَذَا فِي قَوْلِهِ : «وَانْظُرَا»، وَأَرَادَ «المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَقَلَّ الجَمْع وَهُوَ اثْنَانِ، وَهَذَا القَدْرُ فِي القِيَاسِ البَسِيطِ كَافٍ، وَسَيَأْتِي الكَلَامُ عَلَى المُرَكَّبِ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَرْتِيبِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ، وَاخْتِبَارِ الصَّحِيحِ مِنْهُمَا مِنَ الفَاسِدِ ؛ إِذْ لَا تَصِحُ النَّتِيجَةُ لُزُوماً إِلَّا بَعْدَ صِحَّةِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا لَازِمَةٌ لَهُمَا ، وَلَازِمُ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِحَسْبِهِمَا ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقُهُ إِلَّا بِصِدْقِهِمَا قَالَ ابْنُ سِيْنَا : الحَقُّ أَنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ القِيَاسُ صَحِيحُ التَّألِيفِ صَادِقُ المُقَدِّمَاتِ، وَجَبَ أَنْ تَكُونَ النَّتِيجَةُ صَادِقَةٌ . اهـ . فَأَمَّا إِنْ كَذَبَتَا أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا فَقَدْ تَكْذُبُ النَّتِيجَةُ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَجَرٌ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جَمَادٌ، وَقَدْ تَصْدُقُ ، نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ صَهَالٌ، وَكُلُّ صَهَالٍ حَيَوَان . قوله : (وَتُسَمَّى الأَطْرَافُ الثَّلَاثَةُ بـ: «الحُدُودِ») وبذلك سمَّاها صاحب «الخريدة»، فقال: حُدُودُهُ ثَلَاثَةٌ فَمَا حُكِمْ عَلَيْهِ فِي المَظْلُوبِ أَصْغَرٌ عُلِمْ وَمَا بِهِ حُكِمَ أَكْبَرُ وَمَا جَاءَ لِجَمْعِ وَسَطاً قَدْ عُلِمَا قوله : (فَلَا يَلْزَمُ صِدْقُهُ إِلَّا بِصِدْقِهِمَا ) أي : لأنَّ صدق الملزوم يُوجب صدق اللازم، ومما يناسب هنا ما قيل في التهنئة بولادة ابن دقيق العيد : هیئت بِالبَرِّ التَّقِي وَمَنْ يَكُنْ بَرًّا تَقِيَّا مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ يُنْتِجُ إِنَّ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ مَهُمَا كَانَتَا صِدْقاً فَمِثْلُهُمَا النَّتِيجَةُ تَخْرُجُ قوله : (وَقَدْ تَصْدُقُ) أي : لأنَّ النتيجة لازم للمقدِّمتين، وكذبُ الملزوم لا يوجب كذب اللازم، بخلاف صدق الملزوم فإنه يوجب صدق اللازم كما تقدم.
And the three terms are called 'limits' because the limit of a thing and its extremity is its end, and it is the end of the two premises. So this is the manner of its composition indicated by his saying: 'they composed its premises as required.' And the alif in 'rakkaba' is a substitute for the light nun of emphasis, and likewise in his saying 'wa-nẓurā'. He meant 'the premises' as the smallest plural, which is two, and this amount suffices in the simple syllogism, and discussion of the compound will come. There must be ordering of the two premises and testing the sound from the corrupt, because the conclusion is not necessary except after the soundness of the two premises, for it is a necessary consequent of them, and the necessary consequent of the two premises depends on them, so its truth is not necessitated except by their truth. Ibn Sina said: 'The truth is that if the syllogism is sound in composition and true in premises, the conclusion must be true.' So if both or one of them is false, sometimes the conclusion is false, e.g., 'Every human is a stone, and every stone is inert'; and sometimes it is true, e.g., 'Every human is an equine, and every equine is an animal.' His saying: 'And the three terms are called 'limits'' – by that the author of al-Kharida named them, saying: 'Its limits are three: what is judged upon in the sought is known as the smaller, and that by which judgment is made is the greater, and what comes for combination is the middle, known.' His saying: 'So its truth is not necessitated except by their truth' means that the truth of the necessary ground necessitates the truth of the necessary consequent. Suitable here is what was said in congratulation for the birth of Ibn Daqiq al-'Id: 'You are characterized by pure piety, and whoever is purely pious like that produces; indeed the two premises, if they are both true, the conclusion emerges similar to them.' His saying: 'And sometimes it is true' means that the conclusion is a necessary consequent of the premises, and falsehood of the necessary ground does not necessitate falsehood of the necessary consequent, unlike truth of the necessary ground, for it necessitates truth of the necessary consequent as previously stated.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's definition of the three terms (hudud) of a syllogism. The commentator begins by explaining why they are called 'limits': because the limit of a thing is its endpoint, and these terms are the endpoints of the two premises. He then clarifies the structure of a simple syllogism, emphasizing that it requires exactly two premises (the smallest plural) and that they must be properly ordered and checked for soundness. The core logical point is that the conclusion is a necessary consequent (lazim) of the premises, so its truth depends on their truth. To support this, he quotes Ibn Sina, affirming that a syllogism with sound composition and true premises yields a necessarily true conclusion. However, he notes that if premises are false, the conclusion may be false (e.g., 'Every human is a stone; every stone is inert') or true (e.g., 'Every human is an equine; every equine is an animal'), because false premises do not necessitate a false conclusion. The commentator then cites verses from al-Kharida to reinforce the identification of the three terms (minor, major, middle). Finally, he elaborates on the relationship between truth of premises and truth of conclusion using the principle of necessary ground (malzum) and consequent (lazim): truth of the ground necessitates truth of the consequent, but falsehood of the ground does not necessitate falsehood. The poetic lines about Ibn Daqiq al-'Id serve as an illustration.

According to the passage, if both premises of a valid syllogism are true, the conclusion must be:
A) True
B) False
C) Either true or false
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism with true premises guarantees a true conclusion; false premises do not guarantee a false conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following syllogism from kalām: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a creator; therefore the world has a creator.' Identify the three terms (minor, major, middle) and then check whether the premises are true. Based on that, is the conclusion guaranteed true?
?
In a fiqh qiyās, a scholar argues: 'Every sale involving gharar (uncertainty) is invalid; this contract involves gharar; therefore this contract is invalid.' Identify the middle term and determine if the syllogism satisfies the truth condition (i.e., are both premises true?).

The Syllogism
intermediate

Identifying Sterile Moods in First Figure Syllogisms
Author presents and refutes two objections to the truth condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify sterile moods in first figure categorical syllogisms by checking for negative minor or particular major premises.

Core Idea
Not every syllogism with true premises is valid; formal defects like negative minor or particular major produce sterility.

The principle that true premises guarantee a true conclusion applies only to valid syllogisms. Two objections seem to contradict this, but on inspection they involve formal defects that make them sterile (uqm). By learning to detect these defects, you can defend the principle and avoid being misled by apparent counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two objections
Examine the two syllogisms presented as objections: (1) 'Human alone is rational; every rational is an animal' concluding 'Human alone is animal'. (2) 'Four is a number; every number is either even or odd' concluding 'Four is either even or odd'. Both premises are assumed true, yet the conclusion is claimed false. Note the structure of each.
The first objection uses 'alone' which makes the minor premise compound; the second uses a disjunctive major.
2
Analyze the first objection for sterility
Break down the minor premise 'Human alone is rational' into an affirmative ('Human is rational') and a negative ('Nothing other than human is rational'). In the first figure, if the minor premise is negative, the syllogism does not produce a valid conclusion. Thus the first objection is sterile because it violates the rule that the minor must be affirmative in the first figure.
This is a formal defect, not a problem with truth of premises.
3
Analyze the second objection for sterility
The major premise 'Every number is either even or odd' is a disjunction that has the force of two particular propositions: 'Some number is even' and 'Some number is odd'. In the first figure, the major premise must be universal; a particular major yields a sterile mood. Hence the second objection also fails.
Even though both premises are true, the form is invalid.
4
Generalize the rule
For a first figure categorical syllogism to produce a valid conclusion, the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise must be universal. Violating either condition results in a sterile mood (uqm).
This is a key condition for syllogistic validity in the first figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد على لزوم صدق النتيجة عند صدق مقدماتها صورتان : الأولى : إذا قلت: «الإنسان وحده ناطق، وكلُّ ناطق حيوان ينتج : «الإنسان وحده حيوان»، وهو كذب، مع صدق مقدمتيه. والثانية : إذا قلت: «الأربعة عدد، وكلُّ عدد إمَّا زوج وإما فرده فإنَّه ينتج: الأربعة : إما زوج وإما فرد»، وهو كذب أيضاً، مع صدق المقدمتين . قلت: أجيب عن الأولى : بأنَّها مِنَ الضُّرُوب العقيمة التي لا تنتج، بيانُه : أَنَّ الصُّغرى مركَّبة من قضيتين: إحداهما موجبة، وهي: «الإنسان ناطق، والأخرى سالبة وهي ليس غـيـر الإنسان بناطق عبر عنها بقوله : «وحده، ومتى كانتِ الصُّغرى سالبة في الشكل الأول لم ينتج. وعن الأخرى أنَّها أيضاً عقيمة، وبيانُه : أَنَّ الكبرى في قوَّة قضيَّتين جزئيتين: إحداهما : بعض العدد زوج ، والأخرى : بعض العدد فرد عبر عنهما بـ : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد»، ومتى كانتِ الكبرى جزئيَّة في الشكل الأوَّل لم تنتج .
And two objections were raised against the necessity of the conclusion being true when its premises are true: First: if you say 'Human alone is rational, and every rational is an animal' it yields 'Human alone is an animal', which is false, despite the truth of its premises. Second: if you say 'Four is a number, and every number is either even or odd, it yields 'Four is either even or odd', which is also false, despite the truth of its premises. I say: The first is answered by saying it is from the sterile moods that do not produce, its explanation: the minor premise is composite of two propositions: one affirmative, namely 'Human is rational', and the other negative, namely 'Nothing other than human is rational', expressed by his saying 'alone', and whenever the minor is negative in the first figure, it does not produce. And the second is also sterile, its explanation: the major premise has the force of two particular propositions: one is 'Some number is even', and the other is 'Some number is odd', expressed by 'Every number is either even or odd', and whenever the major is particular in the first figure, it does not produce.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage defends the principle that a valid syllogism with true premises yields a true conclusion against two counterexamples. The first objection: 'Human alone is rational; every rational is an animal' seems to have true premises and a false conclusion ('Human alone is animal') because, e.g., animals other than humans are also rational? Actually, the premise 'Human alone is rational' is false in reality, but the objection assumes it is true. The commentator explains that this syllogism is sterile (uqm) because the minor premise 'Human alone is rational' is not a simple categorical proposition but a compound of an affirmative ('Human is rational') and a negative ('Nothing other than human is rational'). When the minor premise is negative, the first figure syllogism fails to produce a conclusion. The second objection: 'Four is a number; every number is either even or odd' seems true, but the conclusion 'Four is either even or odd' is true, but the objection claims it is false? Wait, the text says 'وهو كذب أيضاً' meaning it is also false. Actually, 'Four is either even or odd' is true, but the objection argues it is false? Perhaps the intended meaning is that the conclusion is not the same as 'Four is even' or 'Four is odd'? But the commentator treats it as a false conclusion because the major premise 'Every number is either even or odd' is a disjunction that includes both possibilities, but the conclusion 'Four is either even or odd' is true; however, the objection likely assumes that the conclusion should be a specific determination, not a disjunction. The commentator explains that this syllogism is also sterile because the major premise has the force of two particular propositions ('Some number is even', 'Some number is odd') which are both true, but in the first figure, a particular major does not yield a universal conclusion. Thus, neither objection undermines the principle.

الشكل الأول

The Necessity of the Minor's Inclusion in the Major
Commentator explains the necessity of the minor premise being included under the major, and clarifies the linguistic form of 'sughra' and 'kubra'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the minor premise must be included under the major premise for a valid categorical syllogism in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Inclusion ensures that the conclusion's subject is linked to its predicate via the middle term.

In a valid categorical syllogism, the minor premise (sughra) must be included under the major premise (kubra). This inclusion is not merely a formal condition but the logical mechanism that guarantees the conclusion. The commentator explains this necessity by referring to the relationship between particular and general, and he contrasts it with a simpler formulation from Ibn al-Hajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
The minor premise contains the minor term (subject of the conclusion), and the major premise contains the major term (predicate of the conclusion). For example: 'The world is temporally originated (minor), every temporally originated thing requires a creator (major).'
The minor term is 'the world', the major term is 'requires a creator', and the middle term is 'temporally originated'.
2
Understand the concept of inclusion (indiraj)
The minor term is included under the middle term in the minor premise, and the middle term is included under the major term in the major premise. This two-step inclusion yields the conclusion.
Inclusion means that the minor term is an instance of the middle term, and the middle term is an instance of the major term.
3
Trace Ibn al-Hajib's explanation
Ibn al-Hajib says the minor is particular and the major is general, so inclusion of the particular in the general is necessary. This means the minor premise is more specific, and its subject falls under the major's predicate through the middle term.
The terms 'particular' and 'general' refer to the logical scope of the premises, not necessarily their quantified form.
4
Apply to an Islamic syllogism
Take the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden.' The minor (wine) is included under the middle (intoxicant), and the middle is included under the major (forbidden). The conclusion follows.
Check the inclusion condition to verify validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القياس ثُمَّ قَالَ : (۷۹) وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى فَيَجِبُ انْدِرَاجُهَا فِي الكُبْرَى» يَعْنِي: أَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الصُّغْرَى وَهِيَ ذَاتُ الحَدِّ الأَصْغَرِ مُنْدَرِجَةٌ فِي الكُبْرَى وَهِيَ ذَاتُ الحَدِّ الأَكْبَرِ، وَفِي الحَقِيقَةِ الأَصْغَرُ هُوَ المُنْدَرجُ تَحْتَ الأَوْسَطِ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ بِالأَكْبَرِ كَمَا يَأْتِي ؛ لِيَلْزَمَ الحُكْمُ بِالأَكْبَرِ عَلَى الأَصْغَرِ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ، فَلَوِ اسْتَغْنَى بِقَوْلِهِ الآتي: «وَأَصْغَرٌ فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ عَنْ هَذَا البَيْتِ كَانَ أَوْلَى . وَلَفْظُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ : وَوَجْهُ الدَّلَالَةِ فِي المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى خُصُوص، وَالكُبْرَى عُمُومٌ، فَيَجِبُ انْدِرَاجُ الخُصُوصِ فِي العُمُومِ، فَيَلْتَقِي) مَوْضُوعُ الصُّغْرَى وَمَحْمُولُ الكُبْرَى، وَهُوَ النَّتِيجَةُ . اهـ . قول الناظم : (وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى».. البيت) ليس صغرى وكبرى بلحن؛ إذ لم يريدوا بالأصغر والأكبر التفضيل على معنى من بل على معنى فاعل فصحت المطابقة، ولو لم يحل تال ولم يضف ؛ قال ابن هاني: كَانَ صُغْرَى وَكُبْرَى مِنْ فَقَاقِعِهَا حَصْبَاءُ دُرِّ عَلَى أَرْضِ مِنَ الذَّهَبِ وكما يقول التَّحويون : جملة صغرى وكبرى والعروضيُّون : «فاصلة كبرى وصغرى».
Chapter on syllogism. Then he said: (79) 'And from the premises is the minor, so its inclusion in the major is necessary.' He means that the minor premise, which is the one possessing the minor term, is included in the major premise, which is the one possessing the major term. In reality, the minor term is what is included under the middle term, being judged by the major term, as will come, so that the judgment of the major term on the minor term becomes necessary, and that is the desired conclusion. So if he had sufficed with his later saying 'And the minor is that which has inclusion' instead of this verse, it would have been better. And the wording of Ibn al-Hajib: 'The manner of indication in the two premises is that the minor is particular and the major is general, so inclusion of the particular in the general is necessary, and then the subject of the minor and the predicate of the major meet, which is the conclusion.' The author's saying (the verse 'And from the premises is the minor...') is not 'sughra' and 'kubra' with a vowel (lahn) because they did not intend by 'smaller' and 'greater' the comparative meaning of 'than', but rather the meaning of a participle (fa'il), so the agreement is correct, even if it is not declined and not annexed. Ibn Hani said: 'Its sughra and kubra are like pearls scattered on a golden ground.' And as the grammarians say: 'a sentence small and large' and the prosodists say: 'a major and minor pause'.
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line (number 79) that states the necessity of the minor premise's inclusion in the major premise. The commentator explains that the minor premise is the one containing the minor term, and the major premise contains the major term. In reality, the minor term is included under the middle term, and the middle term is judged by the major term. This inclusion ensures that the judgment about the major term applies to the minor term, which is the desired conclusion. The commentator suggests that a later line in the matn ('And the minor is that which has inclusion') could have sufficed instead of this verse. He then quotes Ibn al-Hajib, who articulates the logical basis: the minor premise is particular, the major is general, so the particular must be included under the general, and then the subject of the minor (minor term) and the predicate of the major (major term) meet in the conclusion. The passage ends with a linguistic note: the words 'sughra' and 'kubra' (meaning minor and major) are not comparative forms (like 'asghar' and 'akbar' meaning smaller and larger) but rather active participles (like 'fa'il'), so they are indeclinable and not annexed. The commentator quotes Ibn Hani and mentions analogous usage in grammar ('small and large sentence') and prosody ('major and minor pause').

Key Terms

الصغرى
الكبرى

Key Takeaway
For a categorical syllogism to conclude, the minor premise must be included under the major premise, ensuring that the minor term is subsumed under the middle term, which is in turn subsumed under the major term.

The Grammatical Status of 'Sughra' and 'Kubra' in Logical Terminology
Commentator explains the necessity of the minor premise being included under the major, and clarifies the linguistic form of 'sughra' and 'kubra'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the grammatical form of 'sughra' and 'kubra' (active participles) from comparative adjectives and explain why they are indeclinable and not annexed.

Core Idea
'Sughra' and 'kubra' are not comparatives but active participles, making them invariable in logical terminology.

The passage includes a linguistic clarification that the terms 'sughra' (minor premise) and 'kubra' (major premise) are not the comparative adjectives 'asghar' or 'akbar'. Instead, they are active participles (like 'fa'il') and therefore indeclinable and not annexed. This grammatical point prevents a common misunderstanding about their meaning in syllogistic logic and shows consistency with usage in other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the common misunderstanding
Many students assume 'sughra' and 'kubra' are comparatives (meaning 'smaller' and 'larger'), which would require a 'than' (min) construction. The commentator clarifies they are intended as active participles, so no comparison is implied.
The verse says 'sughra' not 'asghar', which would be the comparative form.
2
Identify the grammatical form
These words are on the pattern of active participles (fa'la? They are actually nouns that function like participles). They mean 'the one that is minor' and 'the one that is major', not 'the smaller one' or 'the larger one'.
In Arabic, the pattern fu'la and fa'la can be active participles or adjectives. Here they are participles.
3
Understand the consequences for declension and annexation
Because they are not comparatives, they are indeclinable (do not change form for case) and are not annexed (do not take a genitive complement). This is a technical point in Arabic grammar.
The commentator says 'fasaḥat al-muṭābaqah, wa law lam yuḥall tāl wa lam yuḍaf' meaning the agreement is correct even without declension or annexation.
4
See analogous usage in other sciences
The passage mentions grammarians use 'jumla ṣughrā wa kubrā' (small and large sentence) and prosodists use 'fāṣila kubrā wa ṣughrā' (major and minor pause). This shows the same active participle usage across disciplines.
This reinforces that the logical terms are not comparative but descriptive.

Condition of Inclusion of Minor in Major Premise
Explains the requirement that the minor premise must be included in the major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the requirement that the minor term must be included under the middle term in a categorical syllogism, and apply it to verify syllogistic validity.

Core Idea
For a valid syllogism, the minor term must be a particular included under the universal of the major premise.

This passage from the commentary on al-Bajuri explains a crucial condition for any valid categorical syllogism: the minor premise must be 'included' in the major premise. Understanding this condition is key to ensuring that the conclusion necessarily follows from the premises. Let's break down exactly what inclusion means and how to apply it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
Locate the minor term (subject of conclusion), major term (predicate of conclusion), and middle term (appears in both premises but not conclusion). In a valid syllogism, the minor term must be included under the middle term in the minor premise.
The minor premise states a particular fact about the minor term, e.g., 'Khamr is an intoxicant.'
2
Check the relationship in the major premise
The major premise must predicate the major term of the middle term in a universal or particular way. For inclusion to work, the major premise should express a general rule that applies to the middle term, e.g., 'Every intoxicant is prohibited.'
The major term is the predicate of the conclusion; the middle term is the subject of the major premise.
3
Verify that the minor term falls under the middle term
The minor premise must show that the minor term is an instance of the middle term. This ensures that the rule (major term) applies to the minor term. For example, 'Khamr is an intoxicant' places khamr under the category 'intoxicant'.
This is the essence of 'indiraj' (inclusion).
4
Derive the conclusion
Once inclusion is established, the conclusion that the major term applies to the minor term follows necessarily. The conclusion should match the subject of the minor and predicate of the major.
E.g., 'Therefore khamr is prohibited.' This is the desired conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ قَالَ : (وَمَا مِنَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ صُغْرَى فَيَجِبُ انْدِرَاجُهَا فِي الكُبْرَى) يَعْنِي: أَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الصُّغْرَى وَهِيَ ذَاتُ الحَدِّ الأَصْغَرِ مُنْدَرِجَةٌ فِي الكُبْرَى وَهِيَ ذَاتُ الحَدِّ الأَكْبَرِ، وَفِي الحَقِيقَةِ الأَصْغَرُ هُوَ المُنْدَرجُ تَحْتَ الأَوْسَطِ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ بِالأَكْبَرِ كَمَا يَأْتِي ; لِيَلْزَمَ الحُكْمُ بِالأَكْبَرِ عَلَى الأَصْغَرِ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ، فَلَوِ اسْتَغْنَى بِقَوْلِهِ الآتي: «وَأَصْغَرٌ فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ عَنْ هَذَا البَيْتِ كَانَ أَوْلَى . وَلَفْظُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ : وَوَجْهُ الدَّلَالَةِ فِي المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى خُصُوص، وَالكُبْرَى عُمُومٌ، فَيَجِبُ انْدِرَاجُ الخُصُوصِ فِي العُمُومِ، فَيَلْتَقِي مَوْضُوعُ الصُّغْرَى وَمَحْمُولُ الكُبْرَى، وَهُوَ النَّتِيجَةُ . اهـ .
Then he said: 'And from the premises, the minor must be included in the major.' Meaning: the minor premise, which possesses the minor term, is included in the major premise, which possesses the major term. In reality, the minor term is what falls under the middle term, upon which the major term is predicated, as will come, so that the judgment of the major upon the minor is necessitated, and that is the desired conclusion. So if he sufficed with his upcoming saying: 'And the minor is the one included,' he would have been better than this line. The wording of Ibn al-Hajib: 'The aspect of indication in the two premises is that the minor is a particular and the major is universal, so the particular must be included in the universal, so the subject of the minor and the predicate of the major meet, and that is the conclusion.' End.
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the condition for a valid syllogism. The author states that the minor premise must be included in the major premise, meaning the minor term (subject of the conclusion) must fall under the middle term, which is the subject of the major premise. This ensures that the major term is predicated of the minor term in the conclusion. The commentator notes that the poet could have omitted this line and sufficed with a later line. He then quotes Ibn al-Hajib, who explains that the minor premise is particular and the major premise is universal, so the particular (minor term) is included in the universal (middle term), resulting in the conclusion being the meeting of the subject of the minor and predicate of the major.

A valid categorical syllogism requires that the minor premise (containing the minor term) be included in the major premise, which is fundamentally the inclusion of the particular under the universal.

Analyzing Invalid First-Figure Syllogisms
Refutes two examples of syllogisms that produce false conclusions despite true premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a first-figure categorical syllogism fails when the minor premise is negative or the major premise is particular.

Core Idea
A valid first-figure syllogism requires both premises universal and the minor affirmative.

This passage exposes two seemingly valid syllogisms that produce false conclusions. The first uses a compound minor premise containing a negative; the second uses a disjunctive major premise equivalent to two particulars. Understanding these hidden rule violations sharpens your ability to check syllogisms in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two syllogisms
The passage critiques two syllogisms. The first is implied: 'Man is rational, and only man is rational' (i.e., nothing else is rational). The second is explicit: 'Four is a number, and every number is either even or odd.'
The first syllogism is reconstructed from the author's answer.
2
Analyze the first syllogism's minor premise
The minor premise 'Man is rational, and only man is rational' is compound: one affirmative ('Man is rational') and one negative ('Nothing other than man is rational'). In the first figure, a negative minor premise is invalid.
The author says: 'Whenever the minor premise is negative in the first figure, it does not produce.'
3
Analyze the second syllogism's major premise
The major premise 'Every number is either even or odd' is disjunctive. In categorical logic, it is equivalent to two particular propositions: 'Some number is even' and 'Some number is odd.' In the first figure, the major premise must be universal; a particular major invalidates the syllogism.
The author says the major is 'in the strength of two particular propositions.'
4
Conclude why the examples are sterile
Both syllogisms violate essential rules of the first figure: the first has a negative minor, the second has a particular major. Hence they produce no necessary conclusion, even though the individual premises are true.
The conclusions are false in reality; the logical flaw prevents them from being valid proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانية : إذا قلت: «الأربعة عدد، وكلُّ عدد إمَّا زوج وإما فرده فإنَّه ينتج: الأربعة : إما زوج وإما فرد»، وهو كذب أيضاً، مع صدق المقدمتين . قلت: أجيب عن الأولى : بأنَّها مِنَ الضُّروب العقيمة التي لا تنتج، بيانُه : أَنَّ الصُّغرى مركَّبة من قضيتين: إحداهما موجبة، وهي: «الإنسان ناطق، والأخرى سالبة وهي ليس غـيـر الإنسان بناطق عبر عنها بقوله : «وحده، ومتى كانتِ الصُّغرى سالبة في الشكل الأول لم ينتج. وعن الأخرى أنَّها أيضاً عقيمة، وبيانُه : أَنَّ الكبرى في قوَّة قضيَّتين جزئيتين: إحداهما : بعض العدد زوج ، والأخرى : بعض العدد فرد عبر عنهما بـ : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد»، ومتى كانتِ الكبرى جزئيَّة في الشكل الأوَّل لم تنتج .
And the second: If you say: 'Four is a number, and every number is either even or odd,' then it produces: 'Four is either even or odd.' That is also false, despite the truth of the two premises. I say: The first is answered: that it is among the sterile modes that produce nothing. Its explanation: The minor premise is composed of two propositions: one affirmative: 'Man is rational,' and the other negative: 'Nothing other than man is rational,' expressed by his saying 'alone.' And whenever the minor premise is negative in the first figure, it does not produce. And about the other: it is also sterile. Its explanation: The major premise is in the strength of two particular propositions: one: 'Some number is even,' and the other: 'Some number is odd,' expressed by them as: 'Number is either even or odd.' And whenever the major premise is particular in the first figure, it does not produce.
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage refutes two invalid syllogisms that have true premises but false conclusions. The first example is: 'Man is rational, and only man is rational' (meaning 'Nothing other than man is rational') which is a compound minor premise containing a negative. In the first figure, a negative minor premise does not yield a valid conclusion. The second example is: 'Four is a number, and every number is either even or odd' which seems to produce 'Four is either even or odd,' but this conclusion is false because four is actually even. The problem is that the major premise 'Every number is either even or odd' is disjunctive and equivalent to two particular propositions: 'Some number is even' and 'Some number is odd.' In the first figure, if the major premise is particular, no valid conclusion follows. Thus both examples are sterile ( عقيمة ) modes.

Key Takeaway
A first-figure syllogism requires the minor premise to be affirmative and the major premise to be universal; otherwise it is sterile.

Indiraj in the First Figure and Coextensive Terms
Explains that indiraj is specific to the first figure but extended, and justifies inclusion for coextensive terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the condition of indiraj applies to the first figure and justify its validity when the middle term is coextensive with the minor term, using the distinction between essence and concept.

Core Idea
Inclusion holds even for coextensive terms because the minor's essence is more specific than the middle's concept.

In this lesson, we explore the principle of indiraj, which requires the minor term to be included under the middle term in a first-figure categorical syllogism. The author notes that although indiraj is specific to the first figure, it is extended to other figures because they reduce to the first. More importantly, we address a potential problem: what happens when the middle term is coextensive with the minor term, as in 'Every human is rational'? The commentator resolves this by distinguishing between the essence (individuals) of the minor and the concept (meaning) of the middle, showing that inclusion still holds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about indiraj
The passage states that indiraj is specific to the first figure. This means that in a first-figure syllogism, the minor term must be included under the middle term. Locate this claim in the Arabic.
2
Understand the extension to other figures
The commentator explains that because all other figures are reducible to the first figure, the condition of indiraj is generalized to them. Notice the phrase 'rājiʿan ilayhi' indicating reduction.
3
Analyze the two cases of inclusion
The passage presents two cases: when the middle term is more general than the minor (e.g., 'Arab' under 'human') and when they are coextensive (e.g., 'human' and 'rational'). Recognize that both are valid for different reasons.
4
Apply the essence-concept distinction
For coextensive terms, the justification relies on the fact that the minor term is considered by its essence (its individuals), while the middle term is considered by its concept. The individuals of the minor are more specific than the meaning of the middle, so inclusion holds. Verify this with the example given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ هَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجُ خَاصٌ بِالشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، لَكِنْ لَمَّا كَانَ غَيْرُهُ مِنَ الْأَشْكَالِ رَاجِعاً فِي بَيَانِهِ إِلَيْهِ ، أَطْلَقَ النَّاظِمُ فِيهِ، وَلَا فَرْقَ فِي هَذَا الاِنْدِارَجِ بَيْنَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الوَسَطُ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الأَصْغَرِ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ عَرَبِيِّ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ، أَوْ مُسَاوِياً لَهُ؛ نَحْوُ : « كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ حَيَوَانٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهُمَا وَإِنْ تَسَاوَيَا فِي الصِّدْقِ، لَكِنَّ الأَصْغَرَ مُرَادُ مِنْهُ ذَاتُهُ، وَالأَوْسَطُ مَفْهُومُهُ، وَذَاتُ الأَصْغَرِ أَخَصُّ مِنْ مَنْهُومِ الْأَوْسَطِ، فَصَحَ الاِنْدِرَاجُ . وَبِهَذَا يُجَابُ عَنِ الِانْدِرَاجِ فِي نَحْوِ : «العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرٌ ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرٍ حَادِثٌ»؛ لِأَنَّ العَالَمَ وَالمُتَغَيِّرَ مُسْتَوِيَانِ فِي الصِّدْقِ . وَقِيلَ : إِنَّ المُتَغَيِّرَ أَعَمُّ ؛ لِقِصْرِ العَالَم عَلَى المَوْجُودِ المُمْكِنِ، وَزِيَادَةِ المُتَغَيِّرِ بِالأَحْوَالِ الحَادِثَةِ كَكَوْنِ الشَّيْءِ أَبْيَضَ أَوْ أَسْوَدَ ، وَالإِضَافَاتُ الاعْتِبَارِيَّةُ كَالأُبُوَّةِ وَالبُنُوَّةِ ؛ لأَنَّهَا مِنَ المُتَغَيّرِ ، وَلَيْسَتْ بِمَوْجُودَةٍ، وَالاِنْدِرَاجُ حِينَئِذٍ ظَاهِرٌ .
Then this inclusion is specific to the first figure, but since other figures in their explanation refer back to it, the versifier made it general. And there is no distinction in this inclusion between whether the middle term is more general than the minor term, like: 'Every Arab is a human, and every human is an animal' — which is obvious — or equal to it, like: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is an animal'; because even if they are equal in truth (extension), the minor term refers to its essence, while the middle term is its concept, and the essence of the minor is more specific than the concept of the middle, so inclusion is valid. And with this, the inclusion in something like 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated' is answered; because the world and changing are equal in truth (extension). And it is said that changing is more general, because 'world' is restricted to possible existents, while changing includes more, such as accidents like whiteness and blackness, and relative attributes like paternity and sonship, as these are changing but not existent. Then the inclusion is obvious.
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage discusses that the condition of indiraj (inclusion of the minor in the major) is strictly required only in the first figure. However, because all other figures are reducible to the first, the author generalizes this condition. The commentator then clarifies that indiraj is valid both when the middle term is more general than the minor term and when it is coextensive. For example, 'Every human is rational, and every rational is an animal' — here 'human' and 'rational' are coextensive, but since the minor term is considered by its essence (individuals) and the middle term by its concept (meaning), the individuals of human are a subset of the concept of rational, so inclusion holds. This also explains why 'The world is changing, everything changing is temporally originated' is valid — 'world' and 'changing' are coextensive in terms of external existence, but some argue that 'changing' is broader because it includes non-existent attributes. In any case, inclusion is justified.

Key Terms
شكل أول
اندراج
مفهوم

B) Because the minor term's essence is more specific than the middle term's concept
C) Because both terms are equal in extension
D) Because the major term is broader

Key Takeaway
Indiraj holds in the first figure even when the middle and minor are coextensive, because the minor term is taken by its essence (individuals) which are more specific than the middle term's concept.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Three Terms and Two Premises of a Syllogism
Defines the minor, major, and middle terms and the corresponding premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism, and label the corresponding premises as minor or major.

Core Idea
The three terms are the skeleton of every syllogism; the premises are their containers.

In every valid categorical syllogism, exactly three terms appear. Two of them appear in the conclusion (subject and predicate), and one appears only in the premises (the middle term). The premise containing the subject of the conclusion is the minor premise; the one containing the predicate is the major premise. This lesson fixes these definitions so you can analyse any syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Find the conclusion (natija)
The conclusion is the claim being proved. Look for words like 'therefore'. Identify its subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول).
2
Identify the minor term (أصغر)
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion. Write it down.
3
Identify the major term (أكبر)
The major term is the predicate of the conclusion. Write it down.
4
Find the middle term (وسط)
Look for a term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. That is the middle term.
5
Label the premises
The premise that contains the minor term is the minor premise (صغرى); the premise that contains the major term is the major premise (كبرى).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ قَالَ : (۸۰) وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرٍ: «صُغْرَاهُمَا وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَكْبَرٍ: «كُبْرَاهُمَا» (۸۱) وَأَصْغَرٌ» فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ وَوَسَطُ» يُلْـغَـى لَدَى الإِنْتَاجِ قَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّ حُدُودَ القِيَاسِ ثَلَاثَةٌ : ١ - أَحَدُهَا : الأَصْغَرُ، وَهُوَ مَوْضُوعُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ مُقَدَّمُهُ، وَالمُقَدِّمَةُ المُشْتَمِلَةُ عَلَيْهِ تُسَمَّى : «الصُّغْرَى». - وَالثَّانِي : الأَكْبَرُ، وَهُوَ مَحْمُولُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ تَالِيهِ، وَالمُقَدِّمَةُ المُشْتَمِلَةُ عَلَيْهِ تُسَمَّى : «الكُبْرَى».
Then he said: (80) 'And the possessor of a minor term is its minor premise, and the possessor of a major term is its major premise.' (81) 'And the minor is that which is included, and the middle is disregarded in producing the conclusion.' It has already been mentioned that the terms of the syllogism are three: One: the minor, which is the subject of the desired conclusion or its antecedent, and the premise containing it is called the 'minor premise.' And the second: the major, which is the predicate of the desired conclusion or its consequent, and the premise containing it is called the 'major premise.'
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental components of a syllogism. The verse states that the premise containing the minor term is the minor premise, and the premise containing the major term is the major premise. The minor term is the one that is included (under the middle), and the middle term is set aside (not appearing) in the conclusion. The commentator then reiterates: the three terms are the minor (subject of the conclusion), the major (predicate of the conclusion), and the middle (appearing in both premises but not in the conclusion). The minor premise contains the minor, the major premise contains the major.

Key Terms
أصغر
أكبر
وسط
صغرى
كبرى

Key Takeaway
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion, the major term is the predicate, and the middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion; the premise containing the minor is the minor premise, and the one containing the major is the major premise.

The Syllogism
intermediate
