Understanding Inclusion and the Three Terms of the Syllogism
Explains inclusion and defines three syllogistic terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the condition of inclusion of the minor under the middle term in a categorical syllogism and identify the three syllogistic terms (minor, major, middle) in an example from kalam.

Core Idea
Inclusion holds even when terms are co-extensive because the minor's instances are more specific than the middle's concept.

This lesson unpacks a subtle but crucial condition for a valid first-figure syllogism: the minor term must be 'included' under the middle term. The commentator clarifies that inclusion does not require the middle to be broader in extension; it is sufficient that the instances of the minor are more specific than the mere concept of the middle. We also solidify the definitions of the three syllogistic terms—minor, major, and middle—and see how they function in a classic kalam argument for the temporal origination of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim about inclusion
The passage states inclusion is specific to the first figure, but other figures reduce to it. Inclusion is necessary for a valid first-figure syllogism.
Inclusion means the minor term falls under the middle in extension or essence.
2
Distinguish two cases of inclusion
The middle can be more general (e.g., 'Every Arab is a human': 'human' broader than 'Arab') or equal in extension (e.g., 'Every human is rational': co-extensive). In both, inclusion holds because the minor's extension is more specific than the middle's intension.
Equal extension does not negate inclusion; the key is the minor's essence is more specific.
3
Address the apparent counterexample
The syllogism 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated' seems to have co-extensive terms. The commentator explains that 'changing' is actually broader because it includes accidental states and relations, so inclusion is clear.
This resolves a common doubt about the kalam proof for the world's origination.
4
Define the three syllogistic terms
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion (e.g., 'world'), the major term is the predicate of the conclusion (e.g., 'temporally originated'), and the middle term connects them and is omitted in the conclusion (e.g., 'changing'). Each term gives its name to the premise containing it.
The minor premise contains the minor term as subject; the major premise contains the major term as predicate.
5
Apply to a new example
Use the example 'Every Arab is a human; every human is an animal' to identify terms: minor = Arab, major = animal, middle = human. Check inclusion: 'human' is more general than 'Arab', so inclusion is obvious.
This reinforces the concept with a different example from the passage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ هَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجُ خَاصٌ بِالشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، لَكِنْ لَمَّا كَانَ غَيْرُهُ مِنَ الْأَشْكَالِ رَاجِعاً فِي بَيَانِهِ إِلَيْهِ ، أَطْلَقَ النَّاظِمُ فِيهِ، وَلَا فَرْقَ فِي هَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجِ بَيْنَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الوَسَطُ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الأَصْغَرِ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ عَرَبِيٍّ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ، أَوْ مُسَاوِياً لَهُ؛ نَحْوُ : « كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ حَيَوَانٌ» ؛ لِأَنَّهُمَا وَإِنْ تَسَاوَيَا فِي الصِّدْقِ، لَكِنَّ الأَصْغَرَ مُرَادُ مِنْهُ ذَاتُهُ، وَالأَوْسَطُ مَفْهُومُهُ، وَذَاتُ الأَصْغَرِ أَخَصُّ مِنْ مَفْهُومِ الْأَوْسَطِ، فَصَحَّ الاِنْدِرَاجُ . وَبِهَذَا يُجَابُ عَنِ الِانْدِرَاجِ فِي نَحْوِ : «العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرٌ ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرٍ حَادِثٌ»؛ لِأَنَّ العَالَمَ وَالمُتَغَيِّرَ مُسْتَوِيَانِ فِي الصِّدْقِ . وَقِيلَ : إِنَّ المُتَغَيِّرَ أَعَمُّ ؛ لِقِصْرِ العَالَمِ عَلَى المَوْجُودِ المُمْكِنِ، وَزِيَادَةِ المُتَغَيِّرِ بِالأَحْوَالِ الحَادِثَةِ كَكَوْنِ الشَّيْءِ أَبْيَضَ أَوْ أَسْوَدَ ، وَالإِضَافَاتُ الاعْتِبَارِيَّةُ كَالأُبُوَّةِ وَالبُنُوَّةِ ؛ لأَنَّهَا مِنَ المُتَغَيّرِ ، وَلَيْسَتْ بِمَوْجُودَةٍ، وَالاِنْدِرَاجُ حِينَئِذٍ ظَاهِرٌ . ثُمَّ قَالَ : (٨٠) وَذَاتُ حَدٍ أَصْغَرٍ: «صُغْرَاهُمَا» وَذَاتُ حَدٍ أَكْبَرٍ: «كُبْرَاهُمَا» (٨١) وَأَصْغَرٌ فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ وَوَسَطٌ يُلْغَى لَدَى الإِنْتَاجِ قَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّ حُدُودَ القِيَاسِ ثَلَاثَةٌ : ١ - أَحَدُهَا : الأَصْغَرُ، وَهُوَ مَوْضُوعُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ مُقَدَّمُهُ، وَالمُقَدِّمَةُ المُشْتَمِلَةُ عَلَيْهِ تُسَمَّى : «الصُّغْرَى». - وَالثَّانِي : الأَكْبَرُ، وَهُوَ مَحْمُولُ المَظْلُوبِ أَوْ تَالِيهِ، وَالمُقَدِّمَةُ المُشْتَمِلَةُ عَلَيْهِ تُسَمَّى : «الكُبْرَى». - وَالثَّالِثُ : الحَدُّ الوَسَطُ الجَامِعُ بَيْنَهُمَا ، وَفِيهِ يَنْدَرِجُ الأَصْغَرُ، وَعِنْدَ الإِنْتَاجِ يُحْمَلُ الأَكْبَرُ عَلَى الأَصْغَرِ، وَيُلْغَى الوَسَطُ؛ نَحْوُ : «العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرٌ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرٍ حَادِثٌ» نَتِيجَتُهُ : العَالَمُ حَادِثٌ، وَذَلِكَ وَاضِحٌ.
Then this inclusion is specific to the first figure, but since other figures are referred back to it in their explanation, the author [of the poem] used it absolutely. And there is no difference in this inclusion between the middle being more general than the minor, such as 'Every Arab is a human, and every human is an animal' – which is obvious – or equal to it, such as 'Every human is rational, and every rational is an animal'; because although they are equal in extension (truth), the minor is intended for its essence (extension), and the middle is its concept (intension), and the essence of the minor is more specific than the concept of the middle, so the inclusion holds. And by this one answers about the inclusion in examples like 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated' because the world and the changing are equal in extension. And it was said that the changing is more general, due to the restriction of the world to possible existent, and the addition of changing by temporal states like a thing being white or black, and relational accidents like fatherhood and sonhood, because they are of the changing and not existent; and then the inclusion is obvious. Then he said: (80) 'And the essence of the minor is the minor premise, and the essence of the major is the major premise, (81) and the minor is that which has inclusion, and the middle is cancelled in the production.' It has preceded that the bounds of the syllogism are three: 1 – one of them is the minor, which is the subject of the desired [conclusion] or its antecedent, and the premise containing it is called the minor premise. 2 – and the second is the major, which is the predicate of the desired or its consequent, and the premise containing it is called the major premise. 3 – and the third is the middle term which unites them, and in it the minor is included, and at the production the major is predicated on the minor, and the middle is cancelled; for example: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated'; its conclusion: the world is temporally originated, and that is clear.
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the inclusion (indiraj) of the minor term under the middle term in the first figure of the categorical syllogism, followed by a definition of the three syllogistic terms. The author first states that inclusion—the logical relationship wherein the minor term falls under the middle term—is specific to the first figure. However, because all other figures (second, third, fourth) can be reduced to the first figure for proof, the poet speaks of inclusion absolutely, as if it applies to all figures. The commentator then clarifies that inclusion holds regardless of whether the middle term is broader in extension than the minor (e.g., 'Every Arab is a human' where 'human' is broader than 'Arab') or equal in extension (e.g., 'Every human is rational' where 'human' and 'rational' are co-extensive). In both cases, the minor term's extension (its instances) is more specific than the concept (mafhum) of the middle term, so the minor is genuinely included under the middle. The author then addresses a potential counterexample: the syllogism 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated.' Here the world and the changing appear co-extensive, so inclusion might be questioned. The answer is that either they are equal in extension but the minor's essence is still more specific in the sense of inclusion, or more commonly, 'changing' is actually more general because it includes not only possible existents (like the world) but also accidental states (like being white or black) and relational attributes (like fatherhood) which are not subsistent entities. Thus inclusion is obvious. The passage then presents a couplet from the matn defining the three bounds: the minor term gives the minor premise, the major term gives the major premise, and the minor is the one included while the middle is eliminated in the conclusion. The commentary expands: the three bounds are 1) the minor term, which is the subject of the desired conclusion (or antecedent in conditional syllogisms), and the premise containing it is called the minor premise; 2) the major term, which is the predicate of the conclusion (or consequent), and its premise is the major premise; and 3) the middle term, which connects the two extremes, the minor is included under it, and in drawing the conclusion (intaj), the major is predicated of the minor while the middle is omitted. An example is given: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated' yields 'The world is temporally originated.' All examples are from the Islamic intellectual tradition: the Arab/human/animal example from logic discussion, and the world/changing/hadith example typical of kalam arguments for the temporality of the universe.

Key Takeaway
In a valid first-figure categorical syllogism, the minor term must be included under the middle term, which can occur even when the terms are equal in extension, as long as the minor's instances are more specific than the middle's concept.

The Middle Term: The Hidden Link in Syllogisms
Author defines the middle term and gives an example of its function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle term in any categorical syllogism from the Islamic sciences and explain why it is omitted in the conclusion.

Core Idea
The middle term is the hidden glue that makes syllogistic proof possible.

In any categorical syllogism, three terms work together: a minor, a major, and a middle. The author defines the middle term as the unifier, then shows how it enables inference. Understanding the middle term is essential for verifying the validity of deductive proofs in kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the three terms
In the example, locate the minor term (العالم), the major term (حادث), and the middle term (متغير). Note that the middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
The minor is the subject of the conclusion, the major is the predicate.
2
2. Understand the function of the middle term
The middle term 'جَامِعٌ بَيْنَهُمَا' (unites them). It includes the minor (يَنْدَرِجُ فِيهِ الأَصْغَرُ) and allows the major to be predicated of the minor in the conclusion.
Without the middle term, no connection exists between minor and major.
3
3. Observe the elimination of the middle term
In the conclusion (العالم حادث), the middle term متغير disappears. This is called 'إلغاء الوسط'. The inference is valid only because the middle term links the premises correctly.
If the middle term appeared in the conclusion, the syllogism would be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القياس. ٣ - وَالثَّالِثُ : الحَدُّ الوَسَطُ الجَامِعُ بَيْنَهُمَا ، وَفِيهِ يَنْدَرجُ الأَصْغَرُ، وَعِنْدَ الإِنْتَاجِ يُحْمَلُ الأَكْبَرُ عَلَى الأَصْغَرِ، وَيُلْغَى الوسط؛ نَحْوُ : «العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرُ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرٍ حَادِثٌ» نَتِيجَتُهُ : العَالَمُ حَادِثٌ، وَذَلِكَ وَاضِحٌ .
Chapter on Syllogism. 3 – The third is the middle term that unites them [the minor and major terms], and in it the minor is included, and upon production the major is predicated of the minor, and the middle is eliminated; for example: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated' its conclusion: 'The world is originated', and that is clear.
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage defines the middle term (al-hadd al-wasat) as the third component of a categorical syllogism, which brings together the minor and major terms. It explains that the minor term is encompassed by the middle, and in the conclusion, the major is predicated of the minor, while the middle disappears. The example given is the classic first-figure syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated.' This illustrates the essential function of the middle term as the link that yields a necessary conclusion.

The Four Figures of the Syllogism
Author defines figure and mood, then enumerates the four syllogistic figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four syllogistic figures based on the position of the middle term and distinguish them from the mood.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism depends entirely on where the middle term sits.

Every categorical syllogism has a structure determined by the placement of its middle term. This structure is called its 'figure'. Understanding the four figures is essential for evaluating syllogistic validity, because the first figure is the only one that yields conclusions directly without conversion. This lesson dissects the definitions and the four figures as taught in the classical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the two key concepts: figure and mood
The author defines 'figure' (shakl) as the form of the syllogism based on where the middle term appears in the two premises, and 'mood' (darb) as based on the quantifiers (affirmation/negation, universality/particularity). Focus first on figure, since it is more structural.
2
Extract the definition of figure
From the Arabic: 'يُسَمَّى : «شَكُلاً» بِاعْتِبَارِ الهَيْئَةِ الحَاصِلَةِ لَهُ مِنْ وَضْعِ : الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ فِي المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ'. The figure is the configuration resulting from the middle term's position. The genus is 'configuration' and the differentia is the specific position of the middle term.
3
Enumerate the four possible positions
The middle term can be: (1) subject in the minor and predicate in the major; (2) predicate in both; (3) subject in both; (4) predicate in the minor and subject in the major. These correspond respectively to the first, second, third, and fourth figures.
The versified lines in the passage summarise these: 'حمْل بصبْغرى وضعه بكبرى...' etc.
4
Match each position to its figure number
First figure: middle term subject in minor, predicate in major. Second: predicate in both. Third: subject in both. Fourth: predicate in minor, subject in major. Memorise this mapping.
The passage notes the fourth figure is the inverse of the first: in first, middle is subject in minor and predicate in major; in fourth, it's predicate in minor and subject in major.
5
Understand the order of perfection
The figures are listed in order of completeness. The first figure is the most perfect because it yields valid conclusions directly; the others require conversion or reduction to the first figure. This is why it is most commonly used in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ [ فِي الأَشْكَالِ ]. شرح البناني على السلم المنورق. يَعْنِي: أَنَّ مَجْمُوعَ قَضِيَّتَي القِيَاسِ يُسَمَّى : «شَكُلاً» بِاعْتِبَارِ الهَيْئَةِ الحَاصِلَةِ لَهُ مِنْ وَضْعِ : الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ فِي المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ؛ أَيْ : حَالَتِهِ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ مَوْضُوعاً فِي الصُّغْرَى وَمَحْمُولاً فِي الكُبْرَى، أَوْ مَحْمُولاً فِيهِمَا، أَوْ مَوْضُوعاً فِيهِمَا، أَوْ مَحْمُولاً فِي الصُّغْرَى وَمَوْضُوعاً فِي الكُبْرَى. وَيُسَمَّى بِاعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ : «ضَرْباً»؛ أَيْ : إِيْجَابَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ وَسَلْبَهُمَا وَكُلِّيَّتَهُمَا وَجُزْئِيَّتَهُمَا. ثُمَّ قَالَ النَّاظِمُ: وَلِلْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَشْكَالٌ فَقَط أَرْبَعَةٌ بِحَسَبِ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ: حَمْلٌ بِصُغْرَى وَضْعُهُ بِكُبْرَى يُدْعَى بِ: «شَكْلٍ أَوَّلِ» وَيُدْرَى، وَحَمْلُهُ فِي الكُلِّ : «ثَانِياً» عُرِفَ، وَوَضْعُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَالِثاً» أُلِفَ، وَرَابِعُ الأَشْكَالِ : عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ. وَهِيَ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ فِي التَّكَمُّلِ. يَعْنِي: أَنَّ الشَّكْلَ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَامٍ بِحَسَبِ اقْتِرَانِ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ بِكُلِّ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ.
Chapter on the Figures. Commentary of al-Bannani on al-Sullam al-Munawwaq. He means that the two premises of the syllogism are called a 'figure' with consideration of the form resulting from the position of the middle term in the two premises; i.e., its state of being a subject in the minor and a predicate in the major, or a predicate in both, or a subject in both, or a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major. And it is called a 'mood' with consideration of the quantifiers; i.e., the affirmation and negation of the two premises and their universality and particularity. Then the versifier said: 'And for the premises there are only four figures according to the middle term: (The first figure) is predication with the minor and its placement in the major is called the first figure and is known; and its predication in both is known as the second; and its placement in both is the third, well-known; and the fourth figure is the inverse of the first. And they are in order of perfection.' He means that the figure has four divisions according to the coupling of the middle term with each of the two extremes.
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage introduces the concepts of 'figure' (shakl) and 'mood' (darb) in syllogistic logic. The figure is determined solely by the position of the middle term in the two premises: whether it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major (first figure), predicate in both (second figure), subject in both (third figure), or predicate in the minor and subject in the major (fourth figure). The mood, on the other hand, depends on the quality (affirmation/negation) and quantity (universal/particular) of the premises. The passage then presents a poetic summary of the four figures, noting that they are arranged in order of completeness or perfection. The first figure is the most perfect because it yields all conclusions directly, while the others require conversion or reduction. The commentary explains that the figure is defined by how the middle term relates to the two extremes (minor and major).

ضرب
حد وسط

Key Takeaway
The four figures of the syllogism are defined solely by the position of the middle term, with the first figure being the most perfect and the others requiring conversion or reduction.

The Syllogism

Shakl and Darb: The Figure and Mood of a Syllogism
Author defines shakl as form of syllogism and darb as its mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shakl (figure) and darb (mood) in a categorical syllogism and identify them based on the middle term's position.

Core Idea
The figure is defined by the middle term's arrangement, the mood by its specific assignment.

In this lesson, we unpack two foundational terms of syllogistic logic: shakl (figure) and darb (mood). The passage from al-Bannani's commentary clarifies that the figure concerns the overall arrangement of the middle term in the two premises, while the mood refers to the specific state of the middle term being subject or predicate. Understanding this distinction is crucial for classifying syllogisms and determining their validity in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) in a syllogism.
In a categorical syllogism, the middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. For example, in the syllogism 'All A are B, all B are C, so all A are C', B is the middle term.
2
Determine the position of the middle term in each premise.
Check whether the middle term is the subject (mawdu') or predicate (mahmul) in each premise. In the first premise, is it the subject or predicate? In the second premise?
The source uses 'subject/antecedent' (mawdu'/muqaddam) and 'predicate/consequent' (mahmul/tali).
3
Classify the figure (shakl) based on the arrangement.
The overall arrangement of the middle term's positions yields one of four figures. For instance, if the middle term is predicate in the first premise and subject in the second, that is the first figure.
The passage says shakl is considered without quantifiers.
4
Identify the darb (mood) as the specific state.
The specific assignment of the middle term (e.g., subject-subject, subject-predicate, etc.) along with the quantifiers (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) gives the mood. The passage calls this specific state 'darb' and the quantifier combination 'qarina'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ [ فِي الأَشْكَالِ ] شرح البناني على السلمُ المَروَنَقَ قِيَاسِ (۸۲) «الشَّكْلُ عِنْدَ هَؤُلَاءِ النَّاسِ يُطْلَقُ عَنْ قَضِـ (۸۳) مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ إِذْ ذَاكَ بِالضَّرْبِ لَهُ يُشَارُ يَعْنِي: أَنَّ مَجْمُوعَ قَضِيَّتَي القِيَاسِ يُسَمَّى : «شَكُلاً بِاعْتِبَارِ الهَيْئَةِ الحَاصِلَةِ لَهُ مِنْ وَضْعِ : الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ فِي المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ ؛ أَيْ : حَالَتِهِ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ مَوْضُوعاً وَمُقَدَّماً، أَوْ مَحْمُولاً وَتَالِياً، يُسَمَّى : «ضَرْباً»، وَ: «قَرِينَةٌ) بِاعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ ؛ أَيْ : إِيْجَابَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ وَسَلْبَهَا وَكُلِّيَّتَهَا وَجُزْيَّتَهَا ) .
Section on the figures. Commentary of al-Bannani on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq: 'The figure according to these people is applied to two propositions without considering the quantifiers, at that time it is indicated by the mood.' Meaning: The combination of the two propositions of the syllogism is called 'shakl' in consideration of the form obtained from the position of the middle term in the two premises; i.e., its state of being subject and antecedent, or predicate and consequent, is called 'darb', and 'qarina' in consideration of the quantifiers; i.e., the affirmation and negation of the two premises and their universality and particularity.
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage introduces two key technical terms in syllogistic logic: shakl (figure) and darb (mood). The commentator explains that the entire pair of premises in a categorical syllogism is called a shakl based on the arrangement (hay'a) arising from the position of the middle term. Specifically, if the middle term is placed as subject in one premise and predicate in the other, that arrangement defines the figure. Additionally, the specific state of being subject-antecedent or predicate-consequent is called darb (mood). The term qarina is used when considering the quantifiers (aswar) — whether the premises are universal or particular, affirmative or negative. Thus, the same figure can have multiple moods depending on the quantifiers. This is foundational for understanding the classification of syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
شكل
ضرب
قرينة
أسوار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary criterion for defining a syllogism's shakl (figure)?
A) The quantifiers (affirmation/negation, universality/particularity) of the premises.
B) The position of the middle term in the two premises.
C) The number of terms in the syllogism.
D) The truth value of the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The figure of a syllogism is determined solely by the arrangement of the middle term, while the mood (darb) includes the specific positions and quantifiers.

The First Figure: The Perfect Natural Order
Author describes the first figure and its perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first figure of a categorical syllogism and explain why it is considered the perfect natural order.

Core Idea
The first figure mirrors the natural progression from lesser to greater, ensuring self-evident productivity.

The four figures of the categorical syllogism are determined by the position of the middle term. The first figure, where the middle is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, is called the 'perfect arrangement' because it follows the natural sequence. This lesson unpacks its structure and significance.

Guided Path
1
Define the first figure
The first figure occurs when the middle term is the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. Identify that unique positional pairing.
This is the only figure with this asymmetric placement.
2
Recognize the natural order
The natural order moves from the smaller extreme (subject of the conclusion) through the middle to the larger extreme (predicate). In the first figure, this progression is direct: minor premise establishes the middle in the subject, major premise connects it to the predicate.
This makes the first figure intuitive and self-evidently valid.
3
Test productivity
The first figure can produce all four types of categorical propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Verify that the moods Barbara, Celarent, Darii, and Ferio correspond to these.
No other figure achieves this full range.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Construct a first-figure syllogism from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the middle term (intoxicant) as predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
This illustrates the natural order from specific to general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (٨٤) وَلِلْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَشْكَالٌ فَقَط أَرْبَعَةٌ بِحَسَبِ الحَدِّ الوَسَط : (٨٥) حَمْلٌ بِصُغْرَى وَضْعُهُ بِكُبْرَى يُدْعَى بِ: «شَكْلٍ أَوَّلِ وَيُدْرَى (٨٦) وَحَمْلُهُ فِي الكُلِّ : «ثَانِياً» عُرِفْ وَوَضْعُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَالِثاً» أُلِف (۸۷) وَ رَابِعُ الأَشْكَالِ : عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ وَهْيَ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ فِي التَّكَمُّلِ يَعْنِي: أَنَّ الشَّكْلَ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَامٍ بِحَسَبِ اقْتِرَانِ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ بِكُلِّ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ؛ لأَنَّ الوَسَط : إِمَّا مَحْمُولٌ أَوْ تَالٍ فِي الصُّغُرَى، مَوْضُوعٌ أَوْ مُقَدَّمٌ فِي الكُبْرَى، وَيُسَمَّى هَذَا القِسْمُ ب: «الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ»، وَبِـ: «النَّظْمِ الكَامِلِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ الوَارِدُ عَلَى النَّظْمِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ، وَهُوَ : الانْتِقَالُ مِنَ الأَصْغَرِ إِلَى الأَوْسَطِ ، ثُمَّ مِنْهُ إِلَى الأَكْبَرِ، وَهُوَ البَيِّنُ الإنتاج، وَالمُنتج لِلْمَطَالِبِ الأَرْبَعَةِ .
His saying: 'And for the premises there are only four figures according to the middle term: (85) Predicate in the minor, subject in the major is called the first figure, and it is known; (86) and its being predicate in both is called the second, it is known; and its being subject in both is the third, it is familiar; (87) and the fourth figure is the inverse of the first, and they are in order of perfection.' Meaning: That the figure has four divisions according to the conjunction of the middle term with each of the two extremes; because the middle: either it is a predicate or consequent in the minor, and a subject or antecedent in the major, and this division is called the 'first figure', and also 'the perfect arrangement' because it comes upon the natural order, which is the progression from the smaller to the middle, then from it to the larger, and it is self-evidently productive, and produces the four sought things.
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage presents the classic Islamic logical classification of the four figures of a categorical syllogism. The commentator explains that the middle term can occupy four possible positions in the two premises (minor and major). The first figure occurs when the middle is predicate in the minor and subject in the major; it is called 'natural order' because it moves from the lesser extreme (subject of the conclusion) through the middle to the greater extreme (predicate of the conclusion). This figure is self-evidently productive and can produce all four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The second figure has the middle as predicate in both premises; the third has it as subject in both; and the fourth is the inverse of the first (middle as subject in minor and predicate in major). The ordering reflects decreasing perfection, with the first being most perfect. For example, in fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is intoxicant. Therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' — this is first figure (subject-predicate-subject across premises).

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
النظم الكامل
الحد الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it produces all four types of conclusions.
B) Because the middle term is the subject in both premises.
C) Because it follows the natural order from smaller to larger.
D) Because it is the only figure with four moods.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's natural order and self-evident productivity make it the standard for evaluating the validity of other figures.

The Second, Third, and Fourth Figures: Distinguishing by Middle Term Position
Author enumerates the remaining three figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second, third, and fourth figures of a categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term and rank them in order of perfection.

Core Idea
The middle term's position—predicate in both, subject in both, or inverted—defines the remaining three figures.

After mastering the first figure, we turn to the other three. The second figure has the middle as predicate in both premises, the third as subject in both, and the fourth is the inverse of the first. Their decreasing perfection is tied to how closely they approximate the natural order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure
The middle term is the predicate in both the minor and major premises. This means both premises share the same predicate term.
Example: 'No haram is beneficial. Every riba transaction is beneficial. Therefore no riba transaction is haram.' (Middle: beneficial, predicate in both)
2
Identify the third figure
The middle term is the subject in both premises. Both premises share the same subject.
Example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every intoxicant is a drink. Therefore some drink is forbidden.' (Middle: intoxicant, subject in both)
3
Identify the fourth figure
The middle term is the subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major—the inverse of the first figure.
This figure is rarely used in practice because it is less natural.
4
Rank by perfection
The ordering from most to least perfect is: first, second, third, fourth. This reflects how closely the progression from minor to major follows the natural order.
The second figure is less perfect than the first because it requires conversion of one premise for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (٨٤) وَلِلْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَشْكَالٌ فَقَط أَرْبَعَةٌ بِحَسَبِ الحَدِّ الوَسَط : (٨٥) حَمْلٌ بِصُغْرَى وَضْعُهُ بِكُبْرَى يُدْعَى بِ: «شَكْلٍ أَوَّلِ وَيُدْرَى (٨٦) وَحَمْلُهُ فِي الكُلِّ : «ثَانِياً» عُرِفْ وَوَضْعُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَالِثاً» أُلِف (۸۷) وَ رَابِعُ الأَشْكَالِ : عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ وَهْيَ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ فِي التَّكَمُّلِ يَعْنِي: أَنَّ الشَّكْلَ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَامٍ بِحَسَبِ اقْتِرَانِ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ بِكُلِّ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ؛ لأَنَّ الوَسَط : إِمَّا مَحْمُولٌ أَوْ تَالٍ فِي الصُّغُرَى، مَوْضُوعٌ أَوْ مُقَدَّمٌ فِي الكُبْرَى، وَيُسَمَّى هَذَا القِسْمُ ب: «الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ»، وَبِـ: «النَّظْمِ الكَامِلِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ الوَارِدُ عَلَى النَّظْمِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ، وَهُوَ : الانْتِقَالُ مِنَ الأَصْغَرِ إِلَى الأَوْسَطِ ، ثُمَّ مِنْهُ إِلَى الأَكْبَرِ، وَهُوَ البَيِّنُ الإنتاج، وَالمُنتج لِلْمَطَالِبِ الأَرْبَعَةِ .
His saying: 'And for the premises there are only four figures according to the middle term: (85) Predicate in the minor, subject in the major is called the first figure, and it is known; (86) and its being predicate in both is called the second, it is known; and its being subject in both is the third, it is familiar; (87) and the fourth figure is the inverse of the first, and they are in order of perfection.' Meaning: That the figure has four divisions according to the conjunction of the middle term with each of the two extremes; because the middle: either it is a predicate or consequent in the minor, and a subject or antecedent in the major, and this division is called the 'first figure', and also 'the perfect arrangement' because it comes upon the natural order, which is the progression from the smaller to the middle, then from it to the larger, and it is self-evidently productive, and produces the four sought things.

Commentary
This passage presents the classic Islamic logical classification of the four figures of a categorical syllogism. The commentator explains that the middle term can occupy four possible positions in the two premises (minor and major). The first figure occurs when the middle is predicate in the minor and subject in the major; it is called 'natural order' because it moves from the lesser extreme (subject of the conclusion) through the middle to the greater extreme (predicate of the conclusion). This figure is self-evidently productive and can produce all four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The Syllogism
intermediate

Dialectical Exchange on Figure Nobility
Marginal note on figure agreement and nobility objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that the third figure is more noble than the second and evaluate the commentator's response using the principle of the dropped middle term.

Core Idea
The middle term's position does not confer nobility because it is dropped in the conclusion.

In classical Islamic logic, the syllogistic figures are ranked by nobility, with the first figure being most noble, followed by the second, then the third. An objector argues that since the third figure agrees with the first in the subject position of the major premise—and the subject is considered more noble than the predicate—the third figure should be more noble than the second. The marginal note (hashya) refutes this by clarifying a key point: the term of agreement is the middle term, which is dropped when producing the conclusion. Thus, the agreement does not affect nobility. This lesson unpacks that dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Claim
The base commentary explains why the third figure agrees with the first in the major premise: because the middle term is subject in both majors.
The middle term in the third figure is subject in the major, just as in the first figure.
2
Find the Objection (qīla)
The objector argues that since the third figure agrees with the first in the more noble element (the subject), it should be more noble than the second figure, which agrees only in the predicate.
The objector assumes nobility is determined by the position of the agreed-upon term.
3
Analyse the Response (fīhi naẓar)
The commentator responds that the agreed-upon subject is the middle term, which is dropped (ilghā') at the time of production (intāj). Therefore, it does not contribute to the nobility of the figure.
Nobility is instead determined by the subject and predicate of the sought conclusion.
4
Assess the Logical Move
The response uses the principle that only terms appearing in the conclusion matter for nobility. Since the middle term is not in the conclusion, its position is irrelevant.
This refines the earlier ranking methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لِمُوَافَقَتِهِ لِلْأَوَّلِ فِي غَيْرِ أَشْرَفِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ، وَهِيَ الْكُبْرَى) إِنَّمَا وَافَقَهُ فِي الْكُبْرَى؛ لِأَنَّ الْحَدَّ الْأَوْسَطَ مَوْضُوعٌ فِي كُبْرَاهُمَا مَعًا، وَإِنَّمَا يَخْتَلِفَانِ فِي الصُّغْرَى، فَفِي الْأَوَّلِ مَحْمُولٌ فِيهَا، وَفِي الثَّالِثِ مَوْضُوعٌ فِيهَا. قِيلَ: يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ: أَنَّ الثَّانِيَ وَافَقَ الْأَوَّلَ فِي الصُّغْرَى، وَالثَّالِثَ وَافَقَ الْأَوَّلَ فِي الْكُبْرَى، وَمَرْجِعُ مُوَافَقَةِ الثَّانِي إِلَى مَحْمُولِ الصُّغْرَى، وَمَرْجِعُ مُوَافَقَةِ الثَّالِثِ إِلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ، وَقَدْ تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ الْمَوْضُوعَ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ الْمَحْمُولِ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرُوا، فَيَكُونُ الثَّالِثُ أَشْرَفَ مِنَ الثَّانِي. اهـ. وَفِيهِ نَظَرٌ؛ إِذِ الثَّالِثُ وَإِنْ وَافَقَ الْأَوَّلَ فِي الْمَوْضُوعِ، لَكِنَّ ذَلِكَ الْمَوْضُوعَ غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَرٍ لِإِلْغَائِهِ عِنْدَ الْإِنْتَاجِ، وَالْعِبْرَةُ إِنَّمَا هِيَ بِمَوْضُوعِ الْمَطْلُوبِ وَمَحْمُولِهِ، وَلَوِ اعْتَبَرْنَا الْمَوْضُوعَ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ مَوْضُوعٌ لِمَا فَضَّلْنَا الصُّغْرَى عَلَى الْكُبْرَى؛ لِوُجُودِهِ فِيهِمَا، وَاللَّهُ تَعَالَى أَعْلَمُ.
His saying: 'Due to its agreement with the first in its less noble premise, which is the major' — it only agrees with it in the major because the middle term is subject in both their majors, and they differ only in the minor: in the first it is predicate in it, and in the third it is subject in it. It is said: there is an objection against him: that the second agreed with the first in the minor, and the third agreed with the first in the major, and the reference of agreement of the second is to the predicate of the minor, and the reference of agreement of the third is to the subject, and it has been shown that the subject is more noble than the predicate as they mentioned, so the third is more noble than the second. End. But there is consideration: for the third, though it agrees with the first in the subject, that subject is not considered because it is dropped at the time of production; rather the consideration is with the subject and predicate of the sought. If we considered the subject qua subject, we would not prefer the minor over the major, because it exists in both. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This marginal hashya first explains why the third figure agrees with the first in the major: the middle term is subject in both majors. Then it presents a critique (qila) by someone who argues that since the third figure agrees with the first in the subject position, and the subject is considered more noble than the predicate, the third figure should be more noble than the second. However, the commentator (Bajuri or a later super-commentator) responds that this agreement is not meaningful because the subject in the third figure (the middle term) is not part of the conclusion—it is 'dropped' when producing the conclusion. The true measure of nobility is the subject and predicate of the sought conclusion, not the middle term. Therefore, the earlier claim about nobility of the second figure over the third stands. This is a classic hashiya that refines the argument.

Key Terms
حد أوسط
إنتاج
موضوع
محمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the hashya argue the third figure is not necessarily more noble than the second?
A) Because the third figure has no valid moods.
B) Because the middle term in the third figure is dropped in production.
C) Because the predicate is always more noble.
D) Because the first figure is the only noble one.

Key Takeaway
The hashya establishes that the middle term's position does not affect figure nobility because it is eliminated in the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Construct a brief objection (qīla) similar to the one in the passage but using a different example from fiqh. For instance: 'The first figure syllogism 'Every khamr is haram; every nabidh is khamr' uses a middle term (khamr) that is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. The second figure would have the middle term as predicate in both premises. If the third figure agrees with the first in having the middle term as subject in the major, does that make the third figure more noble than the second? Write the objection and then the response using the hashya's principle.'
?
2. Given the following syllogism: 'All intoxicants are forbidden. This drink is an intoxicant. Therefore, this drink is forbidden.' Identify which figure it belongs to and explain why this figure is considered more noble than the other figures based on the criteria discussed in the lesson.

The Syllogism
beginner
8 min
The Four Figures: How to Find the Middle Term's Position
Defining the four figures by middle term position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical syllogism into one of the four figures based on the position of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism is determined by where the middle term sits in each premise.

In this section, Bajuri defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism. Each figure is distinguished by the role of the middle term: whether it is the subject or predicate in each premise. Understanding this classification is the first step to evaluating the validity of syllogistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term (al-awsat).
For example, in 'Every intoxicant is haram; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is haram', the middle term is 'intoxicant'.
2
Check its position in the minor premise
The minor premise (sughra) contains the subject of the conclusion. Look at whether the middle term is the subject or predicate (or antecedent/consequent in conditional terms) in this premise.
The passage uses terms 'subject' (mawdu') and 'predicate' (mahmul) for categorical premises.
3
Check its position in the major premise
The major premise (kubra) contains the predicate of the conclusion. Again, see if the middle term is subject or predicate here.
Conditional syllogisms use 'antecedent' (muqaddam) and 'consequent' (talin), but the same logic applies.
4
Determine the figure
Apply the definitions: first figure: middle as predicate in minor, subject in major; second: predicate in both; third: subject in both; fourth: subject in minor, predicate in major.
The first figure is considered the most natural and perfect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الْأَشْكَالِ. إِمَّا مَحْمُولٌ أَوْ تَالٍ فِي الصُّغْرَى، مَوْضُوعٌ أَوْ مُقَدَّمٌ فِي الْكُبْرَى، وَيُسَمَّى هَذَا الْقِسْمُ: «الشَّكْلَ الْأَوَّلَ»، وَبِـ: «النَّظْمِ الْكَامِلِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ الْوَارِدُ عَلَى النَّظْمِ الطَّبِيعِيِّ، وَهُوَ: الِانْتِقَالُ مِنَ الْأَصْغَرِ إِلَى الْأَوْسَطِ، ثُمَّ مِنْهُ إِلَى الْأَكْبَرِ، وَهُوَ الْبَيِّنُ الْإِنْتَاجِ، وَالْمُنْتِجُ لِلْمَطَالِبِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ. وَإِمَّا مَحْمُولٌ أَوْ تَالٍ فِيهِمَا، وَيُسَمَّى هَذَا الْقِسْمُ: «الشَّكْلَ الثَّانِيَ»؛ لِمُوَافَقَتِهِ لِلْأَوَّلِ فِي أَشْرَفِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ وَهِيَ الصُّغْرَى؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى أَشْرَفِ الْمَطْلُوبِ وَهُوَ مَوْضُوعُهُ الَّذِي لِأَجْلِهِ يُطْلَبُ الْمَحْمُولُ إِيجَابًا أَوْ سَلْبًا، وَلِإِنْتَاجِهِ لِلْكُلِّيِّ الَّذِي هُوَ أَشْرَفُ وَإِنْ كَانَ سَلْبًا، مِنَ الْجُزْئِيِّ وَإِنْ كَانَ إِيجَابًا؛ لِأَنَّ الْكُلِّيَّ أَنْفَعُ فِي الْعُلُومِ، وَأَدْخَلُ تَحْتَ الضَّبْطِ. وَإِمَّا مَوْضُوعٌ أَوْ مُقَدَّمٌ فِيهِمَا، عَكْسَ الثَّانِي، وَيُسَمَّى هَذَا الْقِسْمُ: «الشَّكْلَ الثَّالِثَ»؛ لِمُوَافَقَتِهِ لِلْأَوَّلِ فِي غَيْرِ أَشْرَفِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ، وَهِيَ الْكُبْرَى. وَإِمَّا مَوْضُوعٌ أَوْ مُقَدَّمٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى، مَحْمُولٌ أَوْ تَالٍ فِي الْكُبْرَى، عَكْسَ الْأَوَّلِ، وَيُسَمَّى هَذَا: «الشَّكْلَ الرَّابِعَ»؛ لِمُخَالَفَتِهِ لِلْأَوَّلِ فِي مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ مَعًا، وَلِذَا كَانَ بَعِيدًا عَنِ الطَّبْعِ جِدًّا، حَتَّى أَسْقَطَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ عَنْ دَرَجَةِ الِاعْتِبَارِ. فَهَذِهِ أَرْبَعَةُ أَشْكَالٍ مُرَتَّبَةٌ فِي الْكَمَالِ عَلَى حَسَبِ مَا رَتَّبْنَاهَا، كَمَا قَالَ النَّاظِمُ: وَهِيَ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ فِي التَّكَمُّلِ، وَقَدْ عُلِمَ وَجْهُ ذَلِكَ. وَكُلُّ شَكْلٍ مِنْهَا يَتَقَرَّرُ فِيهِ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ ضَرْبًا؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ إِمَّا كُلِّيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، أَوْ جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، وَأَرْبَعَةٌ فِي أَرْبَعَةٍ بِسِتَّةَ عَشَرَ، فَمَجْمُوعُ الضُّرُوبِ أَرْبَعَةٌ وَسِتُّونَ؛ مِنْ ضَرْبِ أَرْبَعَةٍ عِدَّةِ الْأَشْكَالِ فِي سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ عِدَّةِ مَا فِي كُلِّ شَكْلٍ مِنَ الضُّرُوبِ. وَلَمْ يَعْتَبِرُوا فِي عَدِّ الضُّرُوبِ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ وَلَا الْمُهْمَلَةَ؛ لِأَنَّ الْأُولَى فِي قُوَّةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ، وَالثَّانِيَةَ فِي قُوَّةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ، فَاسْتَغْنَوْا بِهِمَا عَنْهُمَا.
Section on the figures. Either it is a predicate or consequent in the minor, subject or antecedent in the major, and this division is called the 'first figure', and the 'perfect arrangement' because it follows the natural arrangement, which is the transition from the smaller to the middle, then from it to the larger, and it is obviously productive, producing the four types of conclusions. And either it is a predicate or consequent in both, and this division is called the 'second figure' due to its agreement with the first in its more noble premise, which is the minor, because it contains the more noble of the sought, which is its subject, for the sake of which the predicate is sought affirmatively or negatively, and because it produces the universal, which is more noble even if negative, than the particular even if affirmative, because the universal is more useful in the sciences and more subject to control. And either it is subject or antecedent in both, opposite of the second, and this division is called the 'third figure' due to its agreement with the first in its less noble premise, which is the major. And either it is subject or antecedent in the minor, predicate or consequent in the major, opposite of the first, and this is called the 'fourth figure' because it opposes the first in both premises, and therefore it is very far from nature, to the extent that some have dropped it from consideration. So these are four figures arranged in perfection according to how we arranged them, as the versifier said: 'And they are in order of perfection.' And the reason for that is known. And each of these figures contains sixteen moods, because each of its two premises is either universal affirmative or negative, or particular affirmative or negative, and four times four makes sixteen. So the total number of moods is sixty-four, from multiplying four, the number of figures, by sixteen, the number in each figure. And they did not count the singular or the indefinite among the moods, because the former is in the power of the universal, and the latter in the power of the particular, so they sufficed with them instead of those.
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the four figures of the categorical syllogism. The author, Bajuri, is expanding upon the matn of al-Sullam al-Murunqq. He begins by defining each figure based on the position of the middle term relative to the minor and major premises. The first figure has the middle term as subject in the major and predicate in the minor, following the natural order from subject to predicate, and it is the only figure that produces all four types of conclusions (affirmative/negative, universal/particular). The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises; it is considered less perfect than the first but still valid. The third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises, opposite of the second. The fourth figure reverses the first, with the middle term as predicate in the major and subject in the minor; it is considered unnatural and some logicians (like al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and al-Ghazali) have discarded it. The author then notes the order of perfection: first, second, third, fourth. Next, he calculates the number of moods: each figure has 16 moods, since each premise can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative (4x4=16), and with 4 figures the total is 64. They exclude singular and indefinite propositions because they reduce to universal and particular respectively.

Key Terms
شكل
ضرب
صغرى
كبرى
أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first figure of the categorical syllogism, the middle term is:
A) subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor
B) subject in both premises
C) predicate in both premises
D) predicate in the major and subject in the minor

Key Takeaway
The figure of a syllogism is defined by the position of the middle term: first has middle as predicate in minor and subject in major; second as predicate in both; third as subject in both; fourth as subject in minor and predicate in major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the figure of the following syllogism used in a kalam argument: 'The world is contingent; everything contingent has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' (Answer: first figure, since middle term 'contingent' is subject in major and predicate in minor.)
?
Find a real example of a second-figure syllogism in a Islamic theological text, such as al-Ash'ari's arguments against the Mu'tazila.

The 64 Moods: How Many Valid Syllogisms Are There?
Calculating the total number of moods across the four figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of possible moods across all four figures and explain why singular and indefinite propositions are excluded.

Core Idea
Each premise can be one of four types, giving 16 moods per figure, but not all are valid.

After describing the four figures, Bajuri turns to the moods (adrib). He explains that each premise can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative, leading to 16 moods per figure and 64 total. He also notes that singular and indefinite propositions are not counted as separate types, as they reduce to universal and particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of categorical propositions
Recall that categorical propositions can be universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), or particular negative (O). These are the standard four.
The passage mentions 'كُلِّيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، أَوْ جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ'.
2
Calculate moods for one figure
For any given figure, each premise can be any of the four types, so 4 × 4 = 16 possible combinations (moods).
Example: first figure moods include AAA (Barbara), EAE (Celarent), etc.
3
Multiply by four figures
Since there are four figures, the total number of moods is 4 × 16 = 64.
This counts all combinations, even though many are invalid.
4
Explain why singular and indefinite are not counted
Singular propositions (الشخصية) treat an individual as a universal (since it is either true of all or none), and indefinite propositions (المهملة) are treated as particular. So they do not increase the count.
The passage says: 'لِأَنَّ الْأُولَى فِي قُوَّةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ، وَالثَّانِيَةَ فِي قُوَّةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ'.

Source Text

فَاسْتَغْنَوْا بِهِمَا عَنْهُمَا.
Section on the figures. Either it is a predicate or consequent in the minor, subject or antecedent in the major, and this division is called the 'first figure', and the 'perfect arrangement' because it follows the natural arrangement, which is the transition from the smaller to the middle, then from it to the larger, and it is obviously productive, producing the four types of conclusions. And either it is a predicate or consequent in both, and this division is called the 'second figure' due to its agreement with the first in its more noble premise, which is the minor, because it contains the more noble of the sought, which is its subject, for the sake of which the predicate is sought affirmatively or negatively, and because it produces the universal, which is more noble even if negative, than the particular even if affirmative, because the universal is more useful in the sciences and more subject to control. And either it is subject or antecedent in both, opposite of the second, and this division is called the 'third figure' due to its agreement with the first in its less noble premise, which is the major. And either it is subject or antecedent in the minor, predicate or consequent in the major, opposite of the first, and this is called the 'fourth figure' because it opposes the first in both premises, and therefore it is very far from nature, to the extent that some have dropped it from consideration. So these are four figures arranged in perfection according to how we arranged them, as the versifier said: 'And they are in order of perfection.' And the reason for that is known. And each of these figures contains sixteen moods, because each of its two premises is either universal affirmative or negative, or particular affirmative or negative, and four times four makes sixteen. So the total number of moods is sixty-four, from multiplying four, the number of figures, by sixteen, the number in each figure. And they did not count the singular or the indefinite among the moods, because the former is in the power of the universal, and the latter in the power of the particular, so they sufficed with them instead of those.

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the four figures of the categorical syllogism. The author, Bajuri, is expanding upon the matn of al-Sullam al-Murunqq. He begins by defining each figure based on the position of the middle term relative to the minor and major premises. The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises; it is considered less perfect than the first but still valid. The third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises, opposite of the second. The fourth figure reverses the first, with the middle term as predicate in the major and subject in the minor; it is considered unnatural and some logicians (like al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and al-Ghazali) have discarded it. The author then notes the order of perfection: first, second, third, fourth. Next, he calculates the number of moods: each figure has 16 moods, since each premise can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative (4x4=16), and with 4 figures the total is 64. They exclude singular and indefinite propositions because they reduce to universal and particular respectively.

Key Terms
ضرب
كلي
جزئي
شخصية
مهملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 64
C) 4
D) 256

Key Takeaway
There are 64 possible mood-figure combinations if each premise is one of four types, but singular and indefinite propositions are absorbed into universal and particular, keeping the count at 64.

The Quran and Syllogistic Figures: A Critique
Main text with hashya discusses the presence of three figures in the Quran, giving the example of Ibrahim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique the claim that the Quran contains only the first three syllogistic figures by distinguishing between potential logical forms and actual textual wording.

Core Idea
Quranic arguments are not confined to three figures; they can be interpreted in any form.

This lesson examines a claim by al-Ghazali that the first three figures of the syllogism are found in the Quran, using Prophet Ibrahim's argument as evidence. A later commentator refines this, arguing that the Quran's proofs are flexible and can fit any figure, including the fourth. This illustrates the importance of distinguishing between logical potentiality and fixed textual forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original Claim
Read the main text (tanbih) which states that the first three figures exist potentially in the Quran, citing Ibrahim's argument as an example of the first figure.
The claim is about potential existence, not explicit wording.
2
Locate the Authority
The hashya attributes this claim to al-Ghazali's book al-Qistas al-Mustaqim. Recognize that the commentator is engaging with a specific source.
Al-Ghazali is known for integrating logic into Islamic theology.
3
Analyze the Critique
The commentator argues that specifying only three figures is not apparent because Quranic proofs can be conceived in any of the four figures. The fourth figure is not excluded.
The critique is based on the adaptability of Quranic argumentation.
4
Understand the Reasoning
The commentator explains that the arrangement of figures is due to rhetorical occasions (munasabat khitabiyya), not logical necessity. The wording may suggest one figure but does not limit the logical form.
This distinction between rhetorical presentation and logical structure is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Two warnings: The first: They mentioned that the first three figures exist potentially in the Noble Quran. As for the first: it is in the argument of al-Khalil (Abraham), peace be upon him, for the uniqueness of God Almighty in lordship and its negation. And the verification: that this arrangement they mentioned between the figures is only from rhetorical occasions. His saying: 'they mentioned etc.' — the one who mentioned that is Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali in his book 'al-Qistas'. His saying: 'the first three etc.' — his specification of Quranic proofs to three without the fourth is not apparent, because the Noble Quran does not contain a specific form of the four figures; rather it contains proofs that are suitable to be conceived in any of the figures, whether the fourth or other, but one specific figure may come to mind from the wording and be established by it. And God knows best.

Commentary
This lesson combines the main tanbih (warning) and its hashya. The author of the main text mentions that the first three figures of the syllogism are present in potential form in the Quran, giving the example of Prophet Ibrahim's argument for the oneness of God. The hashya then makes two critical points. First, it identifies al-Ghazali as the source of this claim in his book 'al-Qistas al-Mustaqim'. Second, it criticizes the claim that only three figures are in the Quran, arguing that the Quran does not have fixed forms; its arguments can be interpreted in any figure, including the fourth. The only reason one figure seems present is due to the wording suggesting one form. This is a typical refinement that challenges an over-specific claim.

Defining the Fourth Figure and Its Place Among the Figures
Author defines the fourth figure and states the four figures are ordered in perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth syllogism figure by the position of its middle term and explain the historical debate over its naturalness.

Core Idea
The fourth figure inverts the first and its naturalness is disputed among logicians.

This passage introduces the fourth categorical syllogism figure, characterized by the middle term being the predicate in the minor premise and the subject in the major premise. The author notes that due to its unnaturalness, some logicians rejected it entirely, while others accepted it. We will examine the definition and the historical context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of the fourth figure
The author states that in the fourth figure, the subject of the conclusion appears in the minor premise and the predicate in the major, opposite of the first figure. The middle term is thus predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
Contrast with the first figure where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
2
Understand why it is called 'fourth' and its naturalness
The figure is called fourth because it differs from the first in both premises. The author says it is very far from natural disposition, so much so that some logicians dropped it from consideration entirely.
The commentary mentions that al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and al-Ghazali rejected it, while Galen and al-Razi accepted it.
3
Analyze the ordering of figures in perfection
The author states the four figures are arranged in order of perfection but adds that this ordering is merely a matter of rhetorical appropriateness, not logical necessity.
The commentary clarifies that 'completeness' means honor and virtue, and the ranking is based on what is most fitting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِمَّا مَوْضُوعٌ أَوْ مُقَدَّمٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى، مَحْمُولٌ أَوْ تَالٍ فِي الكُبْرَى، عَكْسَ الأَوَّلِ، وَيُسَمَّى هَذَا بِـ: «الشَّكْلِ الرَّابِعِ»؛ لِمُخَالَفَتِهِ لِلأَوَّلِ فِي مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ مَعاً، وَلِذَا كَانَ بَعِيداً عَنِ الطَّبْعِ جِدًّا، حَتَّى أَسْقَطَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ عَنْ دَرَجَةِ الِاعْتِبَارِ. فَهَذِهِ أَرْبَعَةُ أَشْكَالٍ مُرَتَّبَةٌ فِي الكَمَالِ عَلَى حَسَبٍ مَا رَتَبْنَاهَا، كَمَا قَالَ النَّاظِمُ: وَهِيَ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ فِي التَّكَمُّلِ، وَقَدْ عُلِمَ وَجْهُ ذَلِكَ. والتَّحقيقُ: أنَّ هذا الترتيب الذي ذكروه بين الأشكال إنَّما هو مِنَ المُنَاسَبَاتِ الخِطَابِيَّةِ. قوله: (حَتَّى أَسْقَطَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ) أراد به الفارابي، وابن سينا، والغزالي. وذكره جالينوس، وتابعه الإمام الرَّازي، وتبع الإمام من بعده. قوله: (عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ فِي التَّكَمُّلِ) أي: الشَّرف والمزيَّة؛ قال في شرح المطالع: وهذا الترتيب أمر استحساني دعا إليه الاستحسان، والأخذ بالأليق والأولى.
And sometimes the subject [of the conclusion] is the premise in the minor, and the predicate [of the conclusion] is the conclusion in the major, opposite of the first [figure]. This is called the 'fourth figure' because it differs from the first in both its premises. Therefore, it is very far from natural [disposition], so much so that some have dropped it from the level of consideration. So these are four figures arranged in perfection according to how we have ordered them, as the poet said: 'They are in order of completeness.' And the verification: this ordering they mentioned among the figures is merely from rhetorical appropriateness. His statement: 'until some dropped it' refers to al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and al-Ghazali. Galen mentioned it, and Imam al-Razi followed him, and those after the Imam followed. His statement: 'in order of completeness' means honor and virtue; it was said in Sharh al-Matali': This ordering is a matter of preference driven by what is most fitting and appropriate.
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth figure of the syllogism and discusses its position relative to the other figures. The author begins by describing the structure of the fourth figure: In the minor premise, the subject of the conclusion appears as the subject, but in the major premise, the predicate of the conclusion appears as the predicate – opposite of the first figure where the subject is the middle term. He calls this the fourth figure because it differs from the first in both premises [the middle term is predicate in minor and subject in major, unlike the first where it is subject in minor and predicate in major]. He notes that this figure is very unnatural, so much so that some logicians (al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, al-Ghazali) have dropped it entirely, although Galen and al-Razi included it. He then states that the four figures are arranged in order of perfection, with the first figure being most perfect, followed by the second, third, and fourth. However, he adds a verification that this ordering is merely a matter of rhetorical suitability and preference, not a logical necessity. The commentary clarifies that 'completeness' means honor and virtue, and that the ranking is based on what is most fitting.

Counting Syllogistic Moods: 16 per Figure, 64 Total
Author enumerates moods per figure and total, justifying omission of indefinite and individual propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of moods in any syllogistic figure and explain why indefinite and individual propositions are omitted from the count.

Core Idea
Each figure has 16 moods because 4 premise types combine as 4×4.

In this lesson, we explore how logicians enumerate the moods of the syllogism. By understanding the four basic premise types (universal affirmative/negative, particular affirmative/negative), you will see why each figure contains exactly 16 moods, and why the total across four figures is 64. The passage also clarifies that indefinite and individual propositions are not counted separately because they reduce to universal and particular respectively—a key insight for mastering syllogistic theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four premise types
The passage states each premise can be universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), or particular negative (O). List these four types.
These correspond to Arabic: كلية موجبة، كلية سالبة، جزئية موجبة، جزئية سالبة.
2
Compute the combinations for one figure
With two premises, each having 4 possibilities, the total combinations are 4 × 4 = 16. This is the number of moods per figure.
The author explicitly says 'أربعة في أربعة بستة عشر' (four times four equals sixteen).
3
Extend to all four figures
Multiply the 16 moods per figure by the 4 figures to get 64 total moods.
The author: 'من ضرب أربعة عدة الأشكال في ستة عشر' (multiplying 4 figures by 16).
4
Explain the omission of indefinite and individual propositions
The author notes that indefinite propositions (مهملة) act as universals, and individual propositions (شخصية) act as particulars. Therefore, they are already covered by the four main types and not counted separately.
This simplification keeps the mood count manageable.
5
Apply the lesson to an Islamic example
Consider a syllogism in kalam: 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause. The world is temporally originated. Therefore, the world has a cause.' Identify the mood (first figure, AAA) and verify that it is one of the 16 moods.
This shows the practical use of mood enumeration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ شَكْلٍ مِنْهَا يَتَقَرَّرُ فِيهِ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ ضَرْباً؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ إِمَّا كُلِّيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، أَوْ جُزَيْيَّةٌ مُوجَبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ، وَأَرْبَعَةٌ فِي أَرْبَعَةٍ بِسِتَّةِ عَشَرَ، فَمَجْمُوعُ الضُّرُوبِ أَرْبَعَةٌ وَسِتُّونَ؛ مِنْ ضَرْبِ أَرْبَعَةٍ عِدَّةِ الأَشْكَالِ فِي سِتَّةِ عَشَرَ عِدَّةِ مَا فِي كُلِّ شَكْلٍ مِنَ الضُّرُوبِ. وَلَمْ يَعْتَبِرُوا فِي عَدِّ الضُّرُوبِ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ وَلَا المُهْمَلَةَ؛ لِأَنَّ الأُولَى فِي قُوَّةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، وَالثَّانِيَةَ فِي قُوَّةِ الجُزَيْيَّةِ، فَاسْتَغْنُوا بِهِمَا عَنْهُمَا.
And every figure has sixteen moods, because each of its two premises is either universal affirmative or negative, or particular affirmative or negative. Four times four is sixteen. So the total number of moods is sixty-four, from multiplying the number of figures (four) by sixteen (the number of moods per figure). And they did not count in the number of moods the indefinite or the unqualified [propositions], because the former is in force of the universal and the latter in force of the particular; so they dispensed with them by using those.
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage enumerates the moods of the syllogism. The author explains that each of the four figures has sixteen possible moods. This is because each premise can be one of four types: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), or particular negative (O). With two premises, the combination is 4 × 4 = 16. Multiplying by the four figures gives 64 total moods. However, the logicians did not count indefinite (or unquantified) propositions as separate types, because an indefinite proposition is treated as universal in force, and an unqualified (or 'individual') proposition is treated as particular. Thus, they are already covered by the four standard quantifications. This distinction is important because the moods are defined by the quantity and quality of the premises, and only four quantifications are considered basic.

Key Terms
ضرب
كلية
جزئية
مهملة
شخصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 4
B) 16
C) 64
D) 8

Key Takeaway
Each syllogistic figure has 16 moods derived from combining four premise types, for a total of 64 across four figures, with indefinite and individual propositions omitted as redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Reconstructing the First-Figure Syllogism from Ibrahim's Argument
Author shows how the first figure is exemplified in Ibrahim's argument against Namrud in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a first-figure categorical syllogism from Ibrahim's Quranic argument against Namrud and verify its validity using the conversion technique of 'aks al-naqid.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's argument is a perfect example of a first-figure categorical syllogism in the Quran.

The passage from our text shows how the classical logicians found the first figure of the categorical syllogism exemplified in the Quran, specifically in Ibrahim's debate with Namrud. Your task is to extract the logical skeleton from the narrative, identify the premises and conclusion, and see how the major premise is derived through a conversion technique. This will deepen your appreciation for how Islamic logic uses revealed texts as data for reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natija)
Find the statement that Ibrahim is trying to prove against Namrud. The text explicitly gives it: 'You are not my Lord' (أنت لست بربي) or 'You are not a god' (أنت لست بإله). This is the target of the syllogism.
The conclusion is a singular negative proposition.
2
Extract the minor premise (sughra)
The minor premise is a fact about Namrud that leads to the conclusion. From the command 'Bring it from the west' (فأت بها من المغرب), interpreted as a command of incapacitation (amr ta'jiz), we derive 'You (Namrud) cannot bring the sun from the west' (أنت لا تقدر أن تأتي بالشمس من المغرب). This is the minor premise.
The command implies inability because it is impossible for Namrud to fulfill.
3
Extract the major premise (kubra)
The major premise connects inability with not being a lord. It is 'Everyone who cannot bring the sun from the west is not a lord' (كل من لا يقدر على ذلك فليس بربي). This is derived from the conceded truth that Allah brings the sun from the east, which implies 'My Lord is able to bring the sun from the west' (ربي يقدر أن يأتي بالشمس من المغرب). By 'aks al-naqid (conversion of the contradictory), this affirmative proposition yields the universal negative major: 'Everyone who is not able is not a lord'.
The conversion is valid: from 'All able are lords' (implied) to 'All not-able are not-lords' is contraposition.
4
Arrange in first-figure form
Write the syllogism in standard first-figure order: Major premise first (predicate of conclusion), then minor premise (subject of conclusion), then conclusion. The middle term is 'cannot bring the sun from the west' (or 'unable'). Check that the premises are both true (conceded by Namrud) and the form is valid: the middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor. This is Barbara (AAA) when using affirmative terms: 'All unable are non-lords', 'Namrud is unable', therefore 'Namrud is non-lord'.
The mood is Barbara if we treat 'unable' and 'non-lord' as positive terms. The validity ensures the conclusion is necessary.
5
Verify the argument's force
The text notes that Namrud conceded both premises (the minor was obvious, and he conceded the major due to his inability to deny Allah's power). Therefore the conclusion follows irresistibly, and Namrud was astounded (بهت). This shows how a valid syllogism compels assent when premises are accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْبِيهَانِ: الأَوَّلُ: ذَكَرُوا أَنَّ الأَشْكَالَ الثَّلَاثَةَ الأُوَلَ مَوْجُودَةٌ بِالقُوَّةِ فِي القُرْآنِ العَظِيمِ: – أَمَّا الأَوَّلُ: فَفِي احْتِجَاجِ الخَلِيلِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ عَلَى انْفِرَادِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى بِالرُّبُوبِيَّةِ، وَنَفْيُهَا عَنِ النَّمْرُوذِ؛ حَيْثُ ادَّعَاهَا، وَقَالَ لِلْخَلِيلِ: مَنْ رَبُّكَ؟»، فَقَالَ: «رَبِّي الَّذِي يُحْيِي وَيُمِيتُ»، فَأَحْضَرَ رَجُلَيْنِ فَقَتَلَ أَحَدَهُمَا وَتَرَكَ الآخَرَ، وَقَالَ: «أَنَا أُحْيِي وَأُمِيتُ، فَهَذَا أَمَتْهُ وَهَذَا أَحْيَيْتُهُ»، فَانْتَقَلَ الخَلِيلُ إِلَى مَا لَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ كَسْبُ المَخْلُوقِ فَقَالَ: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ﴾ فَبُهِتَ الَّذِي كَفَرَ، وَانْحَسَمَتْ شُبْهَتُهُ، فَقَوْلُهُ: «إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي ... إلخ» فِي قُوَّةِ قَوْلِهِ لَهُ: أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ أَنْ تَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَغْرِبِ، وَكُلُّ مَنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ فَلَيْسَ بِرَبِّي، يُنْتِجُ: «أَنْتَ لَسْتَ بِرَبِّي»، فَالصُّغْرَى يُمْكِنُ أَنْ تُؤْخَذَ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ: «فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ المَغْرِبِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ أَمْرُ تَعْجِيزٍ، وَتُؤْخَذُ أَيْضاً مِنْ مُشَاهَدَةِ حَالِ النَّمْرُوذِ – لَعَنَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى – حَيْثُ لَمْ يَسَعُهُ إِنْكَارُهَا، وَالكُبْرَى عَكْسُ نَقِيضِ قَضِيَّةٍ مَفْهُومَةٍ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ: «إِنَّ اللهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَشْرِقِ»، وَهِيَ: رَبِّي يَقْدِرُ أَنْ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَغْرِبِ؛ أَيْ: كَمَا أَتَى بِهَا مِنَ المَشْرِقِ، فَتَنْعَكِسُ بِالمُوَافِقِ إِلَى الكُبْرَى، وَالنَّمْرُوذُ أَيْضاً يُسَلِّمُهَا، فَلِذَا بُهِتَ. قوله: (لِأَنَّهُ أَمْرُ تَعْجِيزٍ) أي: فَيَكُونُ مَعْنَاهُ حِينَئِذٍ النَّفْيَ؛ أي: أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ أَنْ تَأْتِيَ بِهَا، وَهِيَ عَيْنُ الصُّغْرَى. قوله: (وَالكُبْرَى) يَعْنِي: وَكُلُّ مَنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ. قوله: (مَفْهُومَةٍ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ: إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَشْرِقِ) بَيَانُهُ: أَنَّ قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ﴾ ... إلخ يَسْتَلْزِمُ أَنَّ اللَّهَ قَادِرٌ عَلَى الإِتْيَانِ بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَغْرِبِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ دَلاَلَةِ الفِعْلِ عَلَى قُدْرَةِ فَاعِلِهِ المُخْتَارِ؛ إِذْ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَادِراً لَمَا فَعَلَهُ، وَهَذِهِ القَضِيَّةُ سَلَّمَ النَّمْرُودُ صِدْقَهَا، وَلِذَلِكَ قَامَتْ عَلَيْهِ الحُجَّةُ، وَحَيْثُ سَلَّمَ صِدْقَهَا لَزِمَهُ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ المُوَافِقِ: كُلُّ مَنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ أَنْ يَأْتِيَ بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَغْرِبِ فَلَيْسَ بِإِلَهٍ وَهِيَ الكُبْرَى، وَبِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ المُخَالِفِ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِمَّنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ أَنْ يَأْتِيَ بِالشَّمْسِ إِلَهٌ» وَكِلَاهُمَا يَصْلُحُ كُبْرَى لِلْقَضِيَّةِ المَحْفُوظَةِ أَوَّلاً، وَيُنْتِجُ: أَنْتَ لَسْتَ بِإِلَهٍ. وَذَكَرُوا أَنَّ النَّمْرُودَ لَمَّا حَاجَّهُ إِبْرَاهِيمُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ؛ قَالَ: إِنْ كَانَ مَا يَقُولُ إِبْرَاهِيمُ حَقّاً فَلَا أَنْتَهِي حَتَّى أَصْعَدَ إِلَى السَّمَاءِ فَأَعْلَمَ مَا فِيهَا، فَعَمَدَ إِلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَفْرَاخٍ مِنَ النَّسْرِ فَرَيَّاهَا حَتَّى شَبَّتْ، وَاتَّخَذَ
Two alerts: The first: They mentioned that the first three figures are found potentially in the Great Quran. As for the first figure, it is in the argument of al-Khalil (Ibrahim) upon him peace, for the exclusivity of Allah Most High with lordship, and its negation from Namrud, when he claimed it. He said to al-Khalil: 'Who is your Lord?' He said: 'My Lord is the One who gives life and causes death.' So he brought two men and killed one and left the other, and said: 'I give life and cause death; this one I killed and this one I brought to life.' So al-Khalil moved to something that the creature cannot acquire and said: 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west.' So the one who disbelieved was astounded, and his doubt was cut off. So his saying 'Indeed Allah brings...' is in force of his saying to him: 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west, and everyone who is not able to do that is not my Lord', producing: 'You are not my Lord.' So the minor premise can be taken from his saying 'So bring it from the west' because it is a command of incapacitation, and it can also be taken from observing the state of Namrud, may Allah curse him, where he could not deny it. And the major premise is the converse of the contradictory of a proposition understood from his saying 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east', which is: 'My Lord is able to bring the sun from the west', i.e., just as He brought it from the east. So it is converted by agreement to the major premise, and Namrud also conceded it, so he was astounded. His statement: 'because it is a command of incapacitation' means its meaning then is negation; i.e., you are not able to bring it, and that is the minor premise itself. His statement: 'and the major' means: everyone who is not able. His statement: 'understood from his saying: Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east' its explanation is that the saying of Allah Most High 'Indeed Allah brings the sun' ... implies that Allah is able to bring the sun from the west, necessarily because the action indicates the ability of its agent who is a free chooser; for if He were not able, He would not have done it. And this proposition Namrud conceded its truth, and therefore the argument was established against him. Since he conceded its truth, it becomes necessary for him by the converse of the contradictory of the corresponding [proposition]: 'Everyone who is not able to bring the sun from the west is not a god' – that is the major. And by the converse of the contradictory of the opposite: 'Nothing that is not able to bring the sun is a god' – both are suitable as major for the proposition preserved first, and produces: 'You are not a god.' And they mentioned that when Namrud disputed with Ibrahim, peace be upon him, he said: 'If what Ibrahim says is true, I will not stop until I ascend to the heaven to know what is in it.' So he took four chicks of a vulture and raised them until they grew up, and took...
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed analysis of Ibrahim's argument against Namrud as an example of a first-figure syllogism found in the Quran. The author first sets the context: Ibrahim used the argument to prove that Allah alone is the Lord, and Namrud's claim to lordship is false. When Namrud tried to counter by bringing two men and claiming to give life and death, Ibrahim shifted to an argument that Namrud could not imitate: the ability to bring the sun from the west. The key syllogism is reconstructed: Minor premise: 'You (Namrud) cannot bring the sun from the west' (derived from the command 'bring it from the west' which is a command of incapacitation). Major premise: 'Everyone who cannot bring the sun from the west is not a god' (derived from the conceded truth that Allah brings the sun from the east, via a conversion by contradiction). Conclusion: 'You are not a god.' The author explains that the minor premise is either explicitly from the command or from the observed fact that Namrud could not deny it. The major premise is obtained by taking the understood proposition 'My Lord is able to bring the sun from the west' (since Allah brings it from the east implies He can also bring it from the west) and then converting it by 'aks al-naqid (conversion of contradictory) to yield 'Everyone who is not able is not a god'. Namrud conceded both premises, so the conclusion follows. The passage also includes a closer look at the conversion steps and a continuation of the story about Namrud's attempt to fly.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
أمر تعجيز
