Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the syllogism in Ibrahim's argument as reconstructed by the author?
A) Allah is the only creator.
B) Namrud is not a god.
C) The sun rises from the east.
D) Namrud can bring the sun from the west.

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's argument against Namrud is a valid first-figure categorical syllogism, with the major premise derived by 'aks al-naqid from the conceded truth that Allah alone controls the sun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct another Quranic argument as a categorical syllogism. For example, take the verse 'If there were any gods besides Allah, they would have corrupted the heavens and the earth' (Q 21:22). Identify the conclusion, premises, and figure, and check validity.
?
Explain why the major premise in Ibrahim's argument must be derived via 'aks al-naqid rather than directly stated, and demonstrate the conversion step with symbolic notation.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Reconstructing Ibrahim's Syllogism Against Namrud
Commentator analyzes the logical structure of Ibrahim's argument against Namrud in the first figure of the categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism in the first figure from Ibrahim's debate with Namrud, identifying the minor premise, major premise, and the logical operation used to derive them.

Core Idea
Ibrahim’s argument is a first-figure categorical syllogism hidden in plain language.

The commentator shows that Ibrahim’s challenge to Namrud is not just rhetoric but a precise logical argument. By identifying the premises and the derivation of the major premise via contrapositive conversion, we see how the Qur'an uses reasoning that aligns with the first figure of the categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The overall argument aims to show Namrud is not a Lord. The conclusion is 'You are not my Lord' (أنت لست بربي). This is the final statement that Ibrahim proves.
2
Extract the minor premise
The minor premise is 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west'. It is derived from the command 'So bring it from the west' (فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ), which is a command of incapacitation (أمر تعجيز). The commentator notes it is a positive modified proposition (موجبة معدولة) to ensure the middle term is repeated.
The premise is understood as 'You are incapable of bringing the sun from the west'.
3
Derive the major premise
The major premise is 'Everyone who cannot bring the sun from the west is not my Lord'. It is obtained from the proposition 'Allah brings the sun from the east' (إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ). This implies 'My Lord is able to bring the sun from the west'. Then, by contrapositive conversion (عكس نقيض), we get 'Everyone who cannot bring the sun from the west is not my Lord'.
Namrud conceded the implied proposition, so the proof stands.
4
Check the figure and validity
In this syllogism, the middle term is 'not able to bring the sun from the west' (or 'incapable'). It is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise, placing it in the first figure. Both premises are universal negatives, and the conclusion is a universal negative. The syllogism is valid because the middle term is distributed in both premises.
The anecdote about Namrud's attempted flight is not part of the logical analysis but illustrates his arrogance.

Source Text

فصل [في الأشكال]
عَنِ النَّمْرُوذِ؛ حَيْثُ ادَّعَاهَا، وَقَالَ لِلْخَلِيلِ : مَنْ رَبُّكَ؟»، فَقَالَ: «رَبِّي الَّذِي يُحْيِي وَيُمِيتُ»، فَأَحْضَرَ رَجُلَيْنِ فَقَتَلَ أَحَدَهُمَا وَتَرَكَ الآخَرَ ، وَقَالَ: «أَنَا أُحْيِي وَأُمِيتُ، فَهَذَا أَمَتْهُ وَهَذَا أَحْيَيْتُهُ ، فَانْتَقَلَ الخَلِيلُ إِلَى مَا لَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ كَسْبُ المَخْلُوقِ فَقَالَ: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ فَبُهِتَ الَّذِى كَفَرُ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٥٨]، وَانْحَسَمَتْ شُبْهَتُهُ، فَقَولُهُ: «إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي ... إلخ» فِي قُوَّةِ قَوْلِهِ لَهُ : أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ أَنْ تَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَغْرِبِ، وَكُلُّ مَنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ فَلَيْسَ بِرَبِيِّ، يُنْتِجُ : «أَنْتَ لَسْتَ بِرَبِّي»، فَالصُّغْرَى يُمْكِنُ أَنْ تُؤخَذَ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ المَغْرِبِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ أَمْرُ تَعْجِيزِ، وَتُؤخَذُ أَيْضاً مِنْ مُشَاهَدَةِ حَالِ النَّمْرُوذٍ - لَعَنَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى - حَيْثُ لَمْ يَسَعُهُ إِنْكَارُهَا ، وَالكُبْرَى عَكْسُ نَقِيض قَضِيَّةٍ مَفْهُومَةٍ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : «إِنَّ اللهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَشْرِقِ، وَهِيَ : رَبِّي يَقْدِرُ أَنْ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَغْرِبِ؛ أَيْ : كَمَا أَتَى بِهَا مِنَ المَشْرِقِ، فَتَنْعَكِسُ بِالمُوَافِق إلَى الكُبْرَى، وَالتَّمْرُوذُ أَيْضاً يُسَلِّمُهَا ، فَلِذَا بُهِتَ.
قصارة.
قوله : (لِأَنَّهُ أَمْرُ تَعْجِيز) أي: فيكون معناه حينئذٍ النَّفي؛ أي: أنت لا تقدر أن تأتي بها، وهي عين الصُّغرى.
قوله : ( وَالكُبْرَى) يعني : وكلُّ مَن لا يقدر .
قوله : (مَفْهُومَةٍ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ المَشْرِقِ) بيانُهُ : أَنَّ قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ [البقرة : ۲۵۸] . . . إلخ يستلزم أنَّ الله قادر على الإتيان بالشَّمس مِنَ المغرب؛ ضرورة دلالة الفعل على قدرة فاعله المختار؛ إذ لو لم يكن قادراً لما فعله، وهذ القضيَّة سلّم النمرود صدقها، ولذلك قامت عليه الحجة، وحيث سلَّم صدقها لزمه بعكس النقيض الموافق : كلُّ مَن لا يقدر أن يأتي بالشَّمس مِنَ المغرب فليس بإله وهي الكبرى، وبعكس النقيض المخالف: «لا شيء ممَّن لا يقدر أن يأتي بالشَّمس إله وكلا العكسين يصلح كبرى للقضيَّة المحفوظة أولاً، وينتج :
أنت لست بإله» .
وذكروا أنَّ النَّمرود لمَّا حاجه إبراهيم عليه السَّلام ؛ قال : إن كان ما يقول إبراهيم حقا فلا أنتهي حتى أصعد إلى السَّماء فاعلم ما فيها، فعمد إلى أربعة أفراخ من النسر فريَّاها حتَّى شبَّت، واتَّخذ تابوتاً فجعل له باباً من أعلى وباباً من أسفل، وقعد النمرود مع رجل في التابوت، ونصب خشباً في أطراف التابوت، فجعل على رؤوسها اللحم وربط التّابوت بأرجل النسور، فطارت طمعاً في اللحم حتّى مضى يوم واحد في الهواء فقال النمرود لصاحبه : افتح البابين وانظر»، ففعل وقال: أرى الأرض مثل الحبَّة، والجبال مثل الدُّخان»، ثمَّ طارت النُّسور حتَّى حالتِ الريح بينها وبين الطيران، فقال لصاحبه : افتح البابين وانظر»، ففعل ووجد السَّماء كهيئتها، والأرض سوداء مظلمة، ثم أمره أن يصوّب الخشب وينكس اللحم ففعل، فهبطت النُّسور، فسمعت الجبال طنين التابوت فكادت تزول من أماكنها وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كَانَ مَكْرُهُمْ لِتَزُولَ مِنْهُ الْجِبَالُ [إبراهيم: ٤٦] .
شخ البناني على السلم المرونق
وَالظَّاهِرُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ فِي الصُّغْرَى : أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ مُوجَبَةٌ مَعْدُولَةٌ ; لِيَكُونَ الوَسَطُ مُكَرَّراً، وَقَوْلُهُمْ فِي الكُبْرَى : وَكُلُّ مَنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ ... إلخ» مُحَصِّلَةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُسَوِّرْهَا بِالسُّوْرِ المُتَعَارَفِ لِلْكُلِّيَّةِ [ج/ ٣٤] السَّالِبَةِ ; لِأَنَّ المُعْتَبَرَ المَعْنَى لَا اللَّفْظُ، وَلَوْ جَعَلَ عَاجِز» مَكَانَ لَا تَقْدِر»، وَيُقَالُ فِي الكُبْرَى: وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْعَاجِزِ بِرَبِّي لَكَانَ أَوْلَى . ثُمَّ يَصِحُ سَوْقُ الدَّلِيلِ اسْتِثْنَائِيًّا؛ بِأَنْ يُقَالَ : لَوْ كُنْتَ رَبِّي لَقَدِرْتَ عَلَى الإِنْيَانِ بِالشَّمْسِ، لَكِنَّكَ لَا تَقْدِرُ عَلَيْهِ، فَلَسْتَ بِرَبِّي، وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُسَاقَ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي بِأَنْ يُقَالَ: «مَا أَنْتَ قَادِرٌ أَنْ تَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ، وَرَبِّي قَادِرُ أَنْ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ، فَمَا أَنْتَ بِرَبِّي»، فَلَيْسَ المَقْصُودُ حَصْرَ المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنَ الآيَةِ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَكَأَنَّهُمْ رَأَوْهُ أَظْهَرَ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ.
Section on the figures. Concerning Namrud when he claimed divinity and said to al-Khalil [Ibrahim]: 'Who is your Lord?' He replied: 'My Lord is the One who gives life and causes death.' So he brought two men, killed one and left the other, and said: 'I give life and cause death; this one I killed and this one I gave life.' So al-Khalil shifted to what the creature cannot acquire and said: {Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west} [al-Baqarah 2:258], and the one who disbelieved was confounded, and his doubt was dispelled. So His statement: 'Indeed Allah brings...' etc. is in the force of saying to him: 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west, and everyone who is not able to do that is not my Lord,' producing: 'You are not my Lord.' The minor premise can be taken from His statement: 'So bring it from the west' because it is a command of incapacitation, and it can also be taken from observing the state of Namrud — may Allah curse him — since he could not deny it. The major premise is the converse of the contrapositive of a proposition understood from His statement: 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east,' which is: 'My Lord is able to bring the sun from the west,' i.e., just as He brought it from the east. It is then converted by agreement to the major premise, and Namrud also conceded it, hence he was confounded. Gloss. His statement: 'Because it is a command of incapacitation' means its meaning then is negation, i.e., you cannot bring it, and it is exactly the minor premise. His statement: 'And the major' means: and everyone who cannot... His statement: 'Understood from His statement: Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east' — its explanation: that the statement of the Most High: {Indeed Allah brings the sun} [al-Baqarah 2:258]... etc. entails that Allah is able to bring the sun from the west, due to the necessity of the action indicating the ability of its free agent; for if He were not able, He would not have done it. Namrud conceded the truth of this proposition, so the proof stood against him. Since he conceded its truth, it follows by the contrapositive of the consistent: 'Everyone who cannot bring the sun from the west is not a god' — that is the major. And by the contrapositive of the contrary: 'Nothing that cannot bring the sun is a god.' Both converses are suitable as a major for the proposition preserved earlier, and produce: 'You are not a god.' They mentioned that when Namrud argued with Ibrahim, peace be upon him, he said: 'If what Ibrahim says is true, I will not stop until I ascend to heaven and know what is in it.' So he took four young vultures, reared them until they matured, and made a chest, making a door at the top and a door at the bottom. He sat with a man in the chest, set up wooden poles at the edges of the chest, placed meat on their heads, and tied the chest to the legs of the vultures. They flew in desire for the meat until a day passed in the air. Namrud said to his companion: 'Open both doors and look.' He did and said: 'I see the earth like a grain, and the mountains like smoke.' Then the vultures flew until the wind came between them and flight. He said to his companion: 'Open both doors and look.' He did and found the sky as it is, and the earth black and dark. Then he ordered him to tilt the wood and invert the meat; he did, and the vultures descended. The mountains heard the hum of the chest and almost moved from their places. That is the statement of the Most High: {And their plot was to cause the mountains to vanish} [Ibrahim 14:46]. Al-Bannani's commentary on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq: The apparent meaning is that their statement in the minor: 'You cannot' is a positive modified proposition, so that the middle term is repeated. And their statement in the major: 'And everyone who cannot... etc.' is a non-modalized negative, even if they did not quantify it with the usual quantifier for universal negative, because what matters is the meaning, not the wording. If they had used 'incapable' instead of 'cannot', and said in the major: 'Nothing of the incapable is my Lord,' it would be more appropriate. Then it is valid to present the proof as a conditional exception, by saying: 'If you were my Lord, you would be able to bring the sun, but you cannot, so you are not my Lord.' And it is possible to present it from the second figure by saying: 'You are not able to bring the sun, and my Lord is able to bring the sun, so you are not my Lord.' So the intention is not to restrict what is derived from the verse to the first figure; rather, they saw it as more apparent than others.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a section of al-Sullam al-Murawnaq dealing with the figures of the syllogism (al-ashkal). The commentator uses the famous debate between Prophet Ibrahim (AS) and Namrud (Nimrod) to illustrate a syllogism in the first figure. The argument proceeds as follows: Ibrahim asks Namrud who his Lord is; Namrud claims to give life and death, but Ibrahim shifts to an act Namrud cannot perform—bringing the sun from the west. The commentator identifies two premises: minor: 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west'; major: 'Everyone who is not able to do that is not my Lord'. The conclusion: 'You are not my Lord.' The minor premise is derived from the Qur'anic command 'So bring it from the west', which is a command of incapacitation (amr ta'jiz), implying inability. The major premise is derived from the converse of the contrapositive of the understood proposition from 'Allah brings the sun from the east', yielding 'My Lord is able to bring the sun from the west'. Then by conversion by agreement (عكس بالموافق) it becomes the major. The commentator also notes that the minor premise is a positive modified proposition (موجبة معدولة) to ensure the middle term is repeated in both premises, a necessary condition for a first-figure syllogism. The major is a universal negative, even if not worded with the typical quantifier, because meaning matters over phrasing. He suggests that using 'incapable' instead of 'cannot' avoids the need for modification. The same argument can also be expressed as a conditional syllogism (استثنائي) or in the second figure, showing that the logical structure is not limited to one figure but is most apparent in the first. The passage also includes a long anecdote about Namrud's attempt to ascend to heaven, which serves as background but is not directly part of the logical analysis. The hashya (marginal note) by al-Bannani further clarifies the nature of the premises and the flexibility of logical representation in the Qur'an.

Key Terms
شكل (shakl): Figure of a categorical syllogism, determined by the position of the middle term in the premises. Here discussing the first figure (shakl awwal) with its standard mood. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
صغرى (sughra): The minor premise of a syllogism. In this analysis, it is 'You are not able to bring the sun from the west' or 'You cannot'.
كبرى (kubra): The major premise of a syllogism. Here, it is 'Everyone who cannot bring the sun from the west is not my Lord' or a similar universal negative.
عكس نقيض (aks naqid): Conversion of the contrapositive (contrapositive conversion). The major premise is derived by converting the contrapositive of the proposition 'Allah brings the sun from the east' to yield 'Everyone who cannot bring the sun from the west is not God'. [Note: This is a classical logical operation; remains stable.]
موجبة معدولة (mujaba ma'dula): A positive modified proposition where the predicate includes a negation of the opposite term. Here, 'You are not able' (أنت لا تقدر) is considered a positive modified because it affirms inability (عجز) in a subject, equivalent to 'You are incapable'. [Note: The terminology of 'positive modified' is standard in Aristotelian logic; no significant shift.]
محصلة سالبة (muhassala saliba): A non-modalized (simple) negative proposition. The major premise 'Everyone who cannot...' is a universal negative but not explicitly worded with the standard quantifier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the analysis of Ibrahim's argument against Namrud, what is the minor premise of the syllogism?
A) Everyone who is not able to bring the sun is not a god.
B) You are not able to bring the sun from the west.
C) Allah brings the sun from the east.
D) If you were my Lord, you would be able to bring the sun.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The First Figure and the Affirmative Modified Premise
Commentator explains the logical form of the argument's first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proposition that appears negative is actually an affirmative modified (mujaba ma'dula) to satisfy the first figure's condition.

Core Idea
The first figure needs an affirmative minor; 'anta la taqdir' is treated as affirmative modified.

In the debate between Ibrahim and Nimrod, the premise 'You cannot bring the sun' seems negative. The commentator explains it must be interpreted as an affirmative modified so the middle term repeats and the first figure produces a conclusion. This lesson unpacks why meaning governs logical form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument's premises
The Qur'anic argument: Ibrahim says 'You cannot bring the sun' (minor) and implies 'Everyone who cannot bring the sun is not a god' (major). The conclusion is 'You are not my god.'
2
Check the first figure condition
First figure requires the minor premise to be affirmative. The minor 'anta la taqdir' uses 'la' which normally negates, but the logician treats it as a single modified term 'not-able', making the proposition affirmative in form.
The middle term must appear in the minor as subject in the first figure.
3
Understand 'mujaba ma'dula'
An affirmative modified has a predicate that is a negated term, but the proposition is still affirmative because the negation is part of the term. Example: 'Zaid is not-able' is affirmative, not negative.
The commentator insists this interpretation is necessary for valid first-figure syllogism.
4
Formalize the syllogism
Minor: You are not-able (affirmative). Major: Everyone who is not-able is not a god (simple negative universal). Conclusion: You are not a god. Middle term: 'not-able' (al-ajz).
Even if quantifier explicit, meaning matters more than wording.

Source Text

سَالِبَةٌ ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُسَوِّرْهَا بِالسُّوْرِ المُتَعَارَفِ لِلْكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ المُعْتَبَرَ المَعْنَى لَا اللَّفْظُ، وَلَوْ جَعَلَ عَاجِز» مَكَانَ لَا تَقْدِر»، وَيُقَالُ فِي : ( وَالظَّاهِرُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ فِي الصُغْرَى : أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ» مُوجَبَةٌ مَعْدُولَةٌ) الصَّوابُ : أَنَّه متعين يجب حملها عليه ؛ لأنَّ شرط إنتاج الشكل الأوّل إيجاب الصُّغرى، فلو لم تكن كذلك سوقه مِنَ الرَّابع .
And apparent is that their statement in the minor premise 'You are not able' is an affirmative modified (mawjuba ma'dula) so that the middle term is repeated, and their statement in the major premise 'And everyone who is not able... etc.' is a simple negative, even if they did not quantify it with the customary quantifier for universal negative; because consideration is given to meaning, not wording. If he had made 'incapable' in place of 'you are not able', and said in the major 'And nothing of the incapable is my Lord', that would be more appropriate. Then it is valid to drive the proof as a conditional (istithna'i); by saying: If you were my Lord you would be able to bring the sun, but you are not able to do it, so you are not my Lord. It can also be driven from the second figure by saying: 'You are not able to bring the sun, and my Lord is able to bring the sun, so you are not my Lord.' Thus the intent is not to restrict what is derived from the verse to the first figure; rather, they saw it as more evident than others. Story: He made a chest, gave it a door from above and a door from below, and Nimrod sat with a man in the chest, set up wood at its ends, placed meat on top, and tied the chest to the legs of vultures. They flew desiring the meat until one day passed in the air. Nimrod said to his companion: Open the two doors and look. He did and said: I see the earth like a grain and the mountains like smoke. Then the vultures flew until the wind prevented them from flying. He said to his companion: Open the two doors and look. He did and found the sky as it is, and the earth black and dark. Then he ordered him to lower the wood and turn the meat down; he did, and the vultures descended. The mountains heard the hum of the chest and almost moved from their places. That is the saying of the Exalted: 'And indeed, their plot was to remove from it the mountains.' His saying: ('And apparent is that their statement in the minor premise 'You are not able' is an affirmative modified) The correct is that it is necessarily to be interpreted as such; because the condition for the first figure's productivity is the affirmation of the minor, so if it were not so, the condition would be breached. His saying: ('It would be more appropriate') i.e. because of the lack of interpretation in it. His saying: ('Then it is valid to drive the proof as a conditional') In it is a refutation of those who restricted the existence of only three figures in the Qur'an; rather, the conditional syllogism also exists in it potentially. Thus it appears that the Qur'an does not have a specific form of syllogisms. Hence the commentator said: It can be driven from the second figure, and in the subsequent verse he said: It can be driven from the first, and it can be driven from the fourth.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe by the commentator on the first example: the argument between Ibrahim and Nimrod. The commentator begins by clarifying the logical structure of the argument 'You are not able to bring the sun' (anta la taqdir). He states that the minor premise is an affirmative modified (mawjuba ma'dula) to ensure the middle term is repeated, and the major premise is a simple negative universal, even if the wording lacks an explicit quantifier. The principle is that meaning governs, not wording. Then he suggests that if we replace 'you are not able' with 'incapable' (al-'ajiz), it is more straightforward. He then shows that the same argument can be cast as a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) or as a second figure syllogism. The commentator stresses that the Qur'anic argument is not restricted to the first figure; the choice of first figure is for clarity only. Then the passage inserts a long narrative about Nimrod's flight in a chest carried by vultures, illustrating the verse 'And indeed, their plot...' This story provides historical background. After that, there are three glosses (qawlahu). The first gloss insists that the minor premise must be interpreted as affirmative modified because the first figure requires an affirmative minor. The second gloss clarifies that 'more appropriate' means avoiding figurative interpretation. The third gloss refutes the claim that only three syllogistic figures appear in the Qur'an, asserting that conditional syllogisms are also present. The commentator ends by noting that the same argument can be expressed in the fourth figure, though it is not preferred.

Key Terms
صغرى
كبرى
موجبة معدولة
شكل أول

Why is the minor premise 'anta la taqdir' considered an affirmative modified (mujaba ma'dula) rather than a negative proposition?
A) Because the word 'la' appears in it.

C) Because the major premise is negative.

Key Takeaway
The first figure requires an affirmative minor; when a premise appears negative, it must be interpreted as an affirmative modified to preserve syllogistic validity.

?

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Ibrahim-Nimrod Argument in the Second Figure
Commentator shows how to express the argument in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recast a first-figure syllogism into the second figure and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The same argument fits the second figure by switching the order of premises.

The commentator shows that the Ibrahim-Nimrod argument can be expressed in the second figure with the premises 'You are not able to bring the sun' and 'My Lord is able to bring the sun.' This lesson guides you through the conversion and checks the conditions for a valid second-figure syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Recall the second figure structure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. The syllogism must have one negative premise and the major must be universal.
2
Rephrase the premises
The minor becomes 'You are not able to bring the sun' (negative). The major becomes 'My Lord is able to bring the sun' (affirmative). The middle term is 'able to bring the sun' (qudra on ityan), which is predicate in both.
The conclusion is 'You are not my Lord.'
3
Check the conditions
The minor is negative, the major is affirmative and universal (implied). One premise is negative, and the major is universal. These satisfy the second figure conditions.
The conclusion is negative, which is required.
4
Formalize the syllogism
Minor: You are not able to bring the sun (negative). Major: Every Lord (i.e., my Lord) is able to bring the sun (affirmative universal). Conclusion: You are not a Lord. This is a valid Cesare syllogism.
The argument is valid; the first figure version was also valid but the second figure may be less intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالظَّاهِرُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ فِي الصُغْرَى : أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ مُوجَبَةٌ مَعْدُولَةٌ ; لِيَكُونَ الوَسَطُ مُكَرَّراً، وَقَوْلُهُمْ فِي الكُبْرَى : وَكُلُّ مَنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ ... إلخ» مُحَصِّلَةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُسَوِّرْهَا بِالسُّوْرِ المُتَعَارَفِ لِلْكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ المُعْتَبَرَ المَعْنَى لَا اللَّفْظُ، وَلَوْ جَعَلَ عَاجِز» مَكَانَ لَا تَقْدِر»، وَيُقَالُ فِي الكُبْرَى: وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْعَاجِزِ بِرَبِّي لَكَانَ أَوْلَى . ثُمَّ يَصِحُ سَوْقُ الدَّلِيلِ اسْتِثْنَائِيًّا؛ بِأَنْ يُقَالَ : لَوْ كُنْتَ رَبِّي لَقَدِرْتَ عَلَى الإِنْيَانِ بِالشَّمْسِ، لَكِنَّكَ لَا تَقْدِرُ عَلَيْهِ، فَلَسْتَ بِرَبِّي، وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُسَاقَ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي بِأَنْ يُقَالَ: «مَا أَنْتَ قَادِرٌ أَنْ تَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ، وَرَبِّي قَادِرُ أَنْ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ، فَمَا أَنْتَ بِرَبِّي»، فَلَيْسَ المَقْصُودُ حَصْرَ المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنَ الآيَةِ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَكَأَنَّهُمْ رَأَوْهُ أَظْهَرَ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. قصارة تابوتاً فجعل له باباً من أعلى وباباً من أسفل، وقعد النمرود مع رجل في التابوت، ونصب خشباً في أطراف التابوت، فجعل على رؤوسها اللحم وربط التّابوت بأرجل النسور، فطارت طمعاً في اللحم حتّى مضى يوم واحد في الهواء فقال النمرود لصاحبه : افتح البابين وانظر»، ففعل وقال: أرى الأرض مثل الحبَّة، والجبال مثل الدُّخان»، ثمَّ طارت النُّسور حتَّى حالتِ الريح بينها وبين الطيران، فقال لصاحبه : افتح البابين وانظر»، ففعل ووجد السَّماء كهيئتها، والأرض سوداء مظلمة، ثم أمره أن يصوّب الخشب وينكس اللحم ففعل، فهبطت النُّسور، فسمعت الجبال طنين التابوت فكادت تزول من أماكنها وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كَانَ مَكْرُهُمْ لِتَزُولَ مِنْهُ الْجِبَالُ﴾. قوله : ( وَالظَّاهِرُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ فِي الصُغْرَى : أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ» مُوجَبَةٌ مَعْدُولَةٌ) الصَّوابُ : أَنَّه متعين يجب حملها عليه ؛ لأنَّ شرط إنتاج الشكل الأوّل إيجاب الصُّغرى، فلو لم تكن كذلك لاختل شرط الإنتاج . قوله : (لَكَانَ أَوْلَى) أي : لعدم التأويل فيه . قوله : (ثُمَّ يَصِحُ سَوْقُ الدَّلِيلِ اسْتِثْنَائِيَّا) فيه ردّ على من حصر وجود الأشكال الثلاثة فقط في القرآن، بلِ الشَّرطي الاستثنائي موجود أيضاً فيه بالقوَّة، فظهر : أنَّ القرآن ليست فيه صورة معينة من الأشكال، ولذا قال الشَّارح: ويمكن أن يساق مِنَ الشَّكل الثاني»، وفي الآية بعد هذه قال : ويمكن سوقه مِنَ الأوَّل، ويمكن سوقه مِنَ الرَّابع .
And apparent is that their statement in the minor premise 'You are not able' is an affirmative modified (mawjuba ma'dula) so that the middle term is repeated, and their statement in the major premise 'And everyone who is not able... etc.' is a simple negative, even if they did not quantify it with the customary quantifier for universal negative; because consideration is given to meaning, not wording. If he had made 'incapable' in place of 'you are not able', and said in the major 'And nothing of the incapable is my Lord', that would be more appropriate. Then it is valid to drive the proof as a conditional (istithna'i); by saying: If you were my Lord you would be able to bring the sun, but you are not able to do it, so you are not my Lord. It can also be driven from the second figure by saying: 'You are not able to bring the sun, and my Lord is able to bring the sun, so you are not my Lord.' Thus the intent is not to restrict what is derived from the verse to the first figure; rather, they saw it as more evident than others. Story: He made a chest, gave it a door from above and a door from below, and Nimrod sat with a man in the chest, set up wood at its ends, placed meat on top, and tied the chest to the legs of vultures. They flew desiring the meat until one day passed in the air. Nimrod said to his companion: Open the two doors and look. He did and said: I see the earth like a grain and the mountains like smoke. Then the vultures flew until the wind prevented them from flying. He said to his companion: Open the two doors and look. He did and found the sky as it is, and the earth black and dark. Then he ordered him to lower the wood and turn the meat down; he did, and the vultures descended. The mountains heard the hum of the chest and almost moved from their places. That is the saying of the Exalted: 'And indeed, their plot was to remove from it the mountains.' His saying: ('And apparent is that their statement in the minor premise 'You are not able' is an affirmative modified) The correct is that it is necessarily to be interpreted as such; because the condition for the first figure's productivity is the affirmation of the minor, so if it were not so, the condition would be breached. His saying: ('It would be more appropriate') i.e. because of the lack of interpretation in it. His saying: ('Then it is valid to drive the proof as a conditional') In it is a refutation of those who restricted the existence of only three figures in the Qur'an; rather, the conditional syllogism also exists in it potentially. Thus it appears that the Qur'an does not have a specific form of syllogisms. Hence the commentator said: It can be driven from the second figure, and in the subsequent verse he said: It can be driven from the first, and it can be driven from the fourth.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe by the commentator on the first example: the argument between Ibrahim and Nimrod. The commentator begins by clarifying the logical structure of the argument 'You are not able to bring the sun' (anta la taqdir). He states that the minor premise is an affirmative modified (mawjuba ma'dula) to ensure the middle term is repeated, and the major premise is a simple negative universal, even if the wording lacks an explicit quantifier. The principle is that meaning governs, not wording. Then he suggests that if we replace 'you are not able' with 'incapable' (al-'ajiz), it is more straightforward. He then shows that the same argument can be cast as a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) or as a second figure syllogism. The commentator stresses that the Qur'anic argument is not restricted to the first figure; the choice of first figure is for clarity only. Then the passage inserts a long narrative about Nimrod's flight in a chest carried by vultures, illustrating the verse 'And indeed, their plot...' This story provides historical background. After that, there are three glosses (qawlahu). The first gloss insists that the minor premise must be interpreted as affirmative modified because the first figure requires an affirmative minor. The second gloss clarifies that 'more appropriate' means avoiding figurative interpretation. The third gloss refutes the claim that only three syllogistic figures appear in the Qur'an, asserting that conditional syllogisms are also present. The commentator ends by noting that the same argument can be expressed in the fourth figure, though it is not preferred.

Key Terms
شكل ثان
صغرى
كبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must hold for a second-figure syllogism to be valid?
A) The minor must be affirmative.
B) One premise must be negative and the major universal.
C) The middle term must be subject in both premises.
D) Both premises must be affirmative.

Key Takeaway
A single argument can often be expressed in multiple figures; the second figure requires one negative premise and a universal major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram; nabidh is intoxicant; so nabidh is haram.' Recast it in the second figure. Identify the premises and check validity.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
The Argument as a Conditional Syllogism (Modus Tollens)
Commentator shows the argument can be driven as a conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) from a categorical argument.

Core Idea
The Ibrahim-Nimrod argument becomes a modus tollens: If you were a god, you could bring the sun; you cannot; so you are not a god.

The commentator shows that the same argument can be rendered as a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). This lesson demonstrates how to transform categorical premises into a conditional form and identify the type of conditional inference used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
The argument in conditional form: 'If you were my Lord, you would be able to bring the sun.' This is the major premise (muttasil).
2
Identify the exception (istithna')
The exception is the denial of the consequent: 'But you are not able to bring the sun.' This is the minor premise.
3
Apply modus tollens
Modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. Here P = 'you are my Lord', Q = 'you can bring the sun'. Denying Q leads to denying P.
This form is valid and yields the same conclusion: 'You are not my Lord.'
4
Compare with categorical form
The conditional version is more direct and avoids the need to interpret 'la taqdir' as a modified affirmative. It uses a clear if-then structure.
The commentator notes that this shows the Qur'an contains conditional syllogisms, not just categorical ones.

Which form of conditional argument is used in 'If you were my Lord you would be able to bring the sun, but you are not able, so you are not my Lord'?

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Multiple Syllogistic Forms in the Qur'an
Refutation of the claim that only three figures exist in the Qur'an, showing the conditional and fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify that a single Qur'anic argument can be expressed in multiple figures and forms, refuting the claim that only three figures exist in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
The Qur'an contains all syllogistic forms, including conditional and fourth figure, not just the first three.

Some scholars claimed only three syllogistic figures appear in the Qur'an. The commentator refutes this by showing the Ibrahim argument can be expressed in the first, second, and fourth figures, as well as conditionally. This lesson shows the diversity of logical forms in the Qur'an and the importance of not restricting divine speech to one form.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Some earlier scholars said the Qur'an only contains categorical syllogisms in the first three figures (first, second, third).
2
See the commentator's examples
The commentator shows the Ibrahim argument works in the first figure, second figure, and even the fourth figure (mentioned later). He also provides a conditional formulation.
The passage explicitly states the conditional form and second figure, and a later gloss mentions the fourth figure.
3
Recognize the principle
The Qur'an does not restrict itself to any one logical form. The same argument can be cast in different ways to convey its point.
The commentator says 'the Qur'an does not have a specific form' (ليس في القرآن صورة معينة من الأشكال).
4
Apply to tafsir
When interpreting Qur'anic arguments, don't force them into a single figure. Be open to multiple logical readings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالظَّاهِرُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ فِي الصُغْرَى : أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ مُوجَبَةٌ مَعْدُولَةٌ ; لِيَكُونَ الوَسَطُ مُكَرَّراً، وَقَوْلُهُمْ فِي الكُبْرَى : وَكُلُّ مَنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ ... إلخ» مُحَصِّلَةٌ سَالِبَةٌ ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُسَوِّرْهَا بِالسُّوْرِ المُتَعَارَفِ لِلْكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ المُعْتَبَرَ المَعْنَى لَا اللَّفْظُ، وَلَوْ جَعَلَ عَاجِز» مَكَانَ لَا تَقْدِر»، وَيُقَالُ فِي الكُبْرَى: وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْعَاجِزِ بِرَبِّي لَكَانَ أَوْلَى . ثُمَّ يَصِحُ سَوْقُ الدَّلِيلِ اسْتِثْنَائِيًّا؛ بِأَنْ يُقَالَ : لَوْ كُنْتَ رَبِّي لَقَدِرْتَ عَلَى الإِنْيَانِ بِالشَّمْسِ، لَكِنَّكَ لَا تَقْدِرُ عَلَيْهِ، فَلَسْتَ بِرَبِّي، وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُسَاقَ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي بِأَنْ يُقَالَ: «مَا أَنْتَ قَادِرٌ أَنْ تَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ، وَرَبِّي قَادِرُ أَنْ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ، فَمَا أَنْتَ بِرَبِّي»، فَلَيْسَ المَقْصُودُ حَصْرَ المُسْتَنْبَطِ مِنَ الآيَةِ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ، وَكَأَنَّهُمْ رَأَوْهُ أَظْهَرَ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. قصارة تابوتاً فجعل له باباً من أعلى وباباً من أسفل، وقعد النمرود مع رجل في التابوت، ونصب خشباً في أطراف التابوت، فجعل على رؤوسها اللحم وربط التّابوت بأرجل النسور، فطارت طمعاً في اللحم حتّى مضى يوم واحد في الهواء فقال النمرود لصاحبه : افتح البابين وانظر»، ففعل وقال: أرى الأرض مثل الحبَّة، والجبال مثل الدُّخان»، ثمَّ طارت النُّسور حتَّى حالتِ الريح بينها وبين الطيران، فقال لصاحبه : افتح البابين وانظر»، ففعل ووجد السَّماء كهيئتها، والأرض سوداء مظلمة، ثم أمره أن يصوّب الخشب وينكس اللحم ففعل، فهبطت النُّسور، فسمعت الجبال طنين التابوت فكادت تزول من أماكنها وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كَانَ مَكْرُهُمْ لِتَزُولَ مِنْهُ الْجِبَالُ﴾. قوله : ( وَالظَّاهِرُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ فِي الصُغْرَى : أَنْتَ لَا تَقْدِرُ» مُوجَبَةٌ مَعْدُولَةٌ) الصَّوابُ : أَنَّه متعين يجب حملها عليه ؛ لأنَّ شرط إنتاج الشكل الأوّل إيجاب الصُّغرى، فلو لم تكن كذلك لاختل شرط الإنتاج . قوله : (لَكَانَ أَوْلَى) أي : لعدم التأويل فيه . قوله : (ثُمَّ يَصِحُ سَوْقُ الدَّلِيلِ اسْتِثْنَائِيَّا) فيه ردّ على من حصر وجود الأشكال الثلاثة فقط في القرآن، بلِ الشَّرطي الاستثنائي موجود أيضاً فيه بالقوَّة، فظهر : أنَّ القرآن ليست فيه صورة معينة من الأشكال، ولذا قال الشَّارح: ويمكن أن يساق مِنَ الشَّكل الثاني»، وفي الآية بعد هذه قال : ويمكن سوقه مِنَ الأوَّل، ويمكن سوقه مِنَ الرَّابع .
And apparent is that their statement in the minor premise 'You are not able' is an affirmative modified (mawjuba ma'dula) so that the middle term is repeated, and their statement in the major premise 'And everyone who is not able... etc.' is a simple negative, even if they did not quantify it with the customary quantifier for universal negative; because consideration is given to meaning, not wording. If he had made 'incapable' in place of 'you are not able', and said in the major 'And nothing of the incapable is my Lord', that would be more appropriate. Then it is valid to drive the proof as a conditional (istithna'i); by saying: If you were my Lord you would be able to bring the sun, but you are not able to do it, so you are not my Lord. It can also be driven from the second figure by saying: 'You are not able to bring the sun, and my Lord is able to bring the sun, so you are not my Lord.' Thus the intent is not to restrict what is derived from the verse to the first figure; rather, they saw it as more evident than others. Story: He made a chest, gave it a door from above and a door from below, and Nimrod sat with a man in the chest, set up wood at its ends, placed meat on top, and tied the chest to the legs of vultures. They flew desiring the meat until one day passed in the air. Nimrod said to his companion: Open the two doors and look. He did and said: I see the earth like a grain and the mountains like smoke. Then the vultures flew until the wind prevented them from flying. He said to his companion: Open the two doors and look. He did and found the sky as it is, and the earth black and dark. Then he ordered him to lower the wood and turn the meat down; he did, and the vultures descended. The mountains heard the hum of the chest and almost moved from their places. That is the saying of the Exalted: 'And indeed, their plot was to remove from it the mountains.' His saying: ('And apparent is that their statement in the minor premise 'You are not able' is an affirmative modified) The correct is that it is necessarily to be interpreted as such; because the condition for the first figure's productivity is the affirmation of the minor, so if it were not so, the condition would be breached. His saying: ('It would be more appropriate') i.e. because of the lack of interpretation in it. His saying: ('Then it is valid to drive the proof as a conditional') In it is a refutation of those who restricted the existence of only three figures in the Qur'an; rather, the conditional syllogism also exists in it potentially. Thus it appears that the Qur'an does not have a specific form of syllogisms. Hence the commentator said: It can be driven from the second figure, and in the subsequent verse he said: It can be driven from the first, and it can be driven from the fourth.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe by the commentator on the first example: the argument between Ibrahim and Nimrod. The commentator begins by clarifying the logical structure of the argument 'You are not able to bring the sun' (anta la taqdir). He states that the minor premise is an affirmative modified (mawjuba ma'dula) to ensure the middle term is repeated, and the major premise is a simple negative universal, even if the wording lacks an explicit quantifier. The principle is that meaning governs, not wording. Then he suggests that if we replace 'you are not able' with 'incapable' (al-'ajiz), it is more straightforward. He then shows that the same argument can be cast as a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) or as a second figure syllogism. The commentator stresses that the Qur'anic argument is not restricted to the first figure; the choice of first figure is for clarity only. Then the passage inserts a long narrative about Nimrod's flight in a chest carried by vultures, illustrating the verse 'And indeed, their plot...' This story provides historical background. After that, there are three glosses (qawlahu). The first gloss insists that the minor premise must be interpreted as affirmative modified because the first figure requires an affirmative minor. The second gloss clarifies that 'more appropriate' means avoiding figurative interpretation. The third gloss refutes the claim that only three syllogistic figures appear in the Qur'an, asserting that conditional syllogisms are also present. The commentator ends by noting that the same argument can be expressed in the fourth figure, though it is not preferred.

Key Terms
أشكال
استثنائي
شكل رابع

D) The Qur'an does not contain any syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's arguments are not limited to one syllogistic form; they can be expressed in multiple figures and conditional forms, demonstrating the richness of divine reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibrahim's Argument in Multiple Syllogistic Forms
Commentator shows how Ibrahim's argument can be expressed in multiple syllogistic forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibrahim's argument from Surah al-An'am in the first, second, fourth, and conditional syllogistic forms, and evaluate why the fourth figure is avoided.

Core Idea
A single Qur'anic argument can be expressed in multiple syllogistic figures, but the fourth figure is unnatural and avoided.

In this passage, the commentator shows how the logical core of Ibrahim's statement 'I do not love those that set' can be formalized in several syllogistic figures. By learning to recast the same argument into different figures, you will sharpen your ability to see the logical structure underlying Qur'anic reasoning and recognize which figures are preferred in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Extract the implicit premises from the verse
The verse 'When it set, he said: I do not love those that set' implies two premises: 'This celestial body is a setting thing' (minor) and 'My Lord is not a setting thing' (major). The conclusion is 'This is not my Lord.'
The minor premise comes from 'flamma afal' and the major from 'la uhibbu al-afilin' interpreted as 'I do not love their worship because the Lord does not set.'
2
Identify the syllogistic figure used by the commentator
The commentator first presents the second figure: major premise 'My Lord is not a setting thing', minor premise 'This is a setting thing'. The middle term 'setting thing' is the predicate in both premises.
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises, and the conclusion is negative.
3
Recast in the first figure
Rearrange to first figure: major 'No setting thing is my Lord', minor 'This is a setting thing', conclusion 'This is not my Lord'. This is easier because the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor.
The first figure is considered the most natural and obvious.
4
Recast as a conditional syllogism
Using modus tollens: 'If this were my Lord, it would not have set; but it set; so this is not my Lord.' This is a conditional (istithna'i) syllogism.
The conditional form directly uses the implication between being Lord and not setting.
5
Recast in the fourth figure and evaluate
Fourth figure: major 'No setting thing is my Lord', minor 'This is a setting thing'. Conclusion: 'My Lord is not this', which converts to 'This is not my Lord'. The commentator notes that the fourth figure is unnatural (ba'id 'an al-tab') and avoided when others are possible.
The conversion step is needed to get the standard conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [في الأشكال] لِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ : ﴿فَلَمَّا أَفَلَ قَالَ لَا أُحِبُّ الأَفَلِينَ﴾ فِي قُوَّةٍ قَوْلِهِ : هَذِهِ آفِلَةٌ ، وَرَبِّي لَيْسَ بِآفِل يُنْتِجُ مِنَ الثَّانِي: هَذِهِ لَيْسَتْ بِرَبِّي فَالصُّغْرَى مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : «فَلَمَّا أَفَلَ»، وَالكُبْرَى مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : لَا أُحِبُّ الآفِلِينَ»؛ إِذِ المَعْنَى : لَا أُحِبُّ عِبَادَتِهِمْ؛ لأَنَّ الرَّبَّ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَسْتَحِقُ أَنْ يُعْبَدَ لَا يَافَلُ، وَيُمْكِنُ سَوْقُهُ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ، وَهُوَ أَسْهَلُ ؛ بِأَنْ يُقَالَ: هَذِهِ آفِلَةٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الآفِلِ بِرَبِيِّ ، فَهَذِهِ لَيْسَتْ بِرَبِّي، وَيُمْكِنُ سَوْقَهُ مِنَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي بِأَنْ يُقَالَ: «لَوْ كَانَتْ هَذِهِ رَبِّي مَا أَفَلَتْ ... إلخ»، وَيُمْكِنُ سَوْقَهُ مِنَ الرَّابع بِأَنْ يُقَالَ : الآفِلُ لَيْسَ بِرَبِّي، وَهَذِهِ آفِلَةٌ» يُنْتِجُ مِنْهُ : رَبِّي لَيْسَ بِهَذِهِ»، وَيَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى : هَذِهِ لَيْسَتْ ربِّي ، لَكِنَّ الرَّابِعَ لِبُعْدِهِ عَنِ الطَّبْعِ لَا يُصَارُ إِلَيْهِ مَعَ تَأَتي غَيْرِهِ.
Section on the figures. Because His saying: 'But when it set, he said, 'I do not love those that set.'' is in the strength of his saying: 'This is a setting thing, and my Lord is not a setting thing.' It produces from the second: 'This is not my Lord.' So the minor is from His saying: 'When it set', and the major from His saying: 'I do not love those that set.' For the meaning is: I do not love their worship; because the Lord, who deserves to be worshipped, does not set. And it can be driven from the first, and it is easier: by saying: 'This is a setting thing, and nothing of the setting thing is my Lord, so this is not my Lord.' And it can be driven from the conditional (istithna'i) by saying: 'If this were my Lord, it would not have set... etc.' And it can be driven from the fourth by saying: 'The setting thing is not my Lord, and this is a setting thing.' It produces from it: 'My Lord is not this,' and it inverts to: 'This is not my Lord.' But the fourth, due to its unnaturalness, is not resorted to when others are possible.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe showing how Ibrahim's argument in the Qur'an (Surah al-An'am, verse 76) can be cast in multiple syllogistic figures. The commentator begins by analyzing the logical force of the verse: 'When it set, he said: I do not love those that set.' He paraphrases it as 'This celestial body is a setting thing, and my Lord is not a setting thing.' This immediately yields a second figure syllogism: the middle term ('setting thing') is the predicate in both premises. The minor premise is derived from the phrase 'when it set', and the major from 'I do not love those that set', interpreted as 'I do not love their worship because the Lord, who deserves worship, does not set.' Then the commentator shows that the same argument can be expressed in the first figure, which is easier: 'This is a setting thing, and no setting thing is my Lord, so this is not my Lord.' Next, it can be cast as a conditional syllogism: 'If this were my Lord, it would not have set; but it set, so it is not my Lord.' Finally, it can be expressed in the fourth figure: 'No setting thing is my Lord; this is a setting thing; therefore my Lord is not this,' which converts to 'This is not my Lord.' However, the commentator notes that the fourth figure, being unnatural, is avoided when other forms are available. This demonstrates the flexibility of Qur'anic arguments and the skill of the logician in mapping them to different figures.

Key Terms
آفِلَة (aafila): A thing that sets (like the sun, moon, star); used as the middle term.
رَبّ (rabb): Lord, the one who deserves worship (al-mustahaqqu li al-'ibada).
شكل رابع (shakl rabi'): The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, considered unnatural (ba'id 'an al-tab'). [Note: Some logicians reject the fourth figure entirely; here it is acknowledged but discouraged.]
انعكاس (in'ikas): Conversion (of a proposition), here 'My Lord is not this' converts to 'This is not my Lord'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Second figure.
B) First figure.
C) Fourth figure.
D) Conditional syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's argument can be formalized in multiple syllogistic figures, but the first figure is preferred for its naturalness, while the fourth figure is avoided.

The Syllogism

Syllogistic Refutation in the Qur'an: From Particular to Universal
Commentator extracts a syllogism from the verse refuting the Jewish claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism implicit in a Qur'anic verse and assess its validity given the treatment of particular premises.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's refutation of the Jews' claim relies on a syllogism where a particular premise functions as a universal.

In this passage, the commentator extracts a categorical syllogism from the Qur'an's refutation of the Jewish claim that no human has received revelation. The Jews attempted to deny the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) by asserting that Allah has not sent down anything to any human. The Qur'an responds by mentioning the revelation given to Moses. The commentator shows that this response contains a logical argument: two premises that the Jews concede, leading to a conclusion that contradicts their claim. An objection arises because both premises are about a specific individual (Moses), but the commentator explains that in their logical system, such particular propositions have the force of universals. This lesson will guide you through reconstructing the syllogism and understanding the logical move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The Jews asserted that Allah has not sent down anything to any human (a universal negative proposition). This is the target of the Qur'anic response.
2
Extract the implicit premises from the verse
From the verse, the commentator extracts two premises: 'Moses is a human' (minor premise) and 'Moses received a book/revelation' (major premise). Both premises are conceded by the Jews.
3
Construct the conclusion
From the two premises, the conclusion 'Some humans have received revelation' (particular affirmative) follows. This directly contradicts the Jewish universal negative claim.
4
Address the objection about particular premises
An objection is raised: both premises are particular (shakhsiyya) because they are about a specific individual. In standard syllogistic rules, two particular premises cannot yield a conclusion. The commentator responds that in their logical system, a particular premise (shakhsiyya) is treated as universal (kulliyya).
5
Assess the validity
Given the rule that shakhsiyya counts as universal, the syllogism is valid. Both premises are treated as universal affirmative (A), and the conclusion is particular affirmative (I). In the third figure, this mood is valid under the commentator's framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الثَّالِثُ : فَفِي رَدّ اللهِ تَعَالَى عَلَى اليَهُودِ فِي قَوْلِهِمْ : وَمَا أَنزَلَ اللهُ عَلَى بَشَرٍ مِّن شَيْءٍ ; لِيَتَوَصَّلُوا بِذَلِكَ إِلَى إِنْكَارِ رِسَالَةِ نَبِيِّنَا مُحَمَّدٍ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ، فَرَدَّ اللهُ تَبَارَكَ وَتَعَالَى عَلَيْهِمْ إِبْطَالاً لِمَا زَعَمُوا ; بِأَنَّ هُنَاكَ بَشَراً أُنزِلَ عَلَيْهِ كِتَابٌ، يُسَلِّمُونَ بِشَرِيَّتِهِ وَنُزُولِ الْكِتَابِ عَلَيْهِ ; إِذْ لَوْلَا ذَلِكَ لَمَا قَامَتِ الْحُجَّةُ عَلَيْهِمْ، فَخَرَجَ مِنْ ضِمْنِ الآيَةِ : مُوسَى بَشَرٌ» وَهِيَ الصُّغْرَى، وَمِنْ صَرِيحِهَا : أَنَّهُ أُنزِلَ عَلَيْهِ الْكِتَابُ» وَهِيَ الكُبْرَى، وَهُمَا مُسَلَّمَتَانِ عِنْدَهُمْ، فَيَنْتُجُ : «بَعْضُ الْبَشَرِ أُنزِلَ عَلَيْهِ كِتَابٌ»، وَهُوَ نَقِيضُ الْكُلِّيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. فَإِنْ قُلْتَ : الْمُقَدَّمَتَانِ شَخْصِيَّتَانِ مَعاً، وَلَا يُنْتِجُ الثَّالِثَ حَتَّى تَكُونَ إِحْدَاهُمَا كُلِّيَّةً. قُلْتُ : تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّ الشَّخْصِيَّةَ عِنْدَهُمْ فِي حُكْمِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ.
And as for the third: it is in the refutation of Allah the Exalted against the Jews in their statement: 'And Allah has not sent down upon any human being anything'—so that they might thereby reach the denial of the prophethood of our Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). So Allah the Blessed and Exalted refuted them, nullifying what they claimed, by [pointing out] that there is a human being upon whom a book was sent down, they acknowledge his humanity and the sending down of the book upon him; for otherwise the proof would not have stood against them. So from the implication of the verse comes: 'Moses is a human'—that is the minor premise, and from its explicit wording comes: 'The book was sent down upon him'—that is the major premise, and both are conceded by them. So it produces: 'Some humans have a book sent down upon them,' which is the contradictory of the universal negative. If you say: Both premises are particular (shakhsiyya), and the third [figure] does not produce a conclusion unless one of them is universal. I say: It has preceded that the particular (shakhsiyya) in their view is in the ruling of the universal.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe analyzing how the Qur'an refutes the Jewish claim that no human has received any revelation (universal negative). The commentator identifies the implicit syllogism: from the verse, we derive that Moses is a human (minor premise) and that a book was sent down to him (major premise). Both premises are accepted by the Jews. The conclusion is 'Some humans have had a book sent down upon them,' which directly contradicts the universal negative claim. An objection is raised: both premises are particular (shakhsiyya, i.e., about a specific individual), and in the third figure, a universal premise is required for a conclusion. The commentator answers that in their logical system, particular propositions (shakhsiyya) are treated as universal in effect. This demonstrates how Qur'anic arguments can be analyzed syllogistically and how logicians handle the issue of particular premises in the third figure.

Key Terms
شخصية (shakhsiyya) - A proposition about a specific individual, treated as having the force of a universal in this system.
كلية سالبة (kulliyya saliba) - Universal negative proposition, e.g., 'No human has received revelation.'
نقيض (naqid) - Contradictory opposite; the conclusion is the contradictory of the universal negative.
مقدمتان شخصيتان (muqaddimatani shakhsiyyatani) - Two premises both about a particular individual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what logical rule allows the syllogism to be valid despite both premises being particular?
A) The premises are converted.
B) Particular premises are treated as universal in their system.
C) The figure is changed to the first figure.
D) A third premise is added.

Key Takeaway
The logical structure of Qur'anic arguments can be analyzed syllogistically, and the treatment of singular propositions as universals allows valid deductions in such contexts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Construct a categorical syllogism from the Qur'anic verse 'We sent Our messengers with clear proofs' (Qur'an 57:25) and the fact that Muhammad is a messenger. Extract premises and conclusion, and identify if any premise is particular.
?
Task 2: In the above syllogism, if the premise 'Muhammad is a messenger' is considered particular, explain how the commentator's rule about shakhsiyya applies to make the argument valid.
